Then pour a small quantity of hot water into the cannula, hold your hand still, and gently rotate it with the pressure of your hand for a short time, so that the edge of the cannula may press against the root of the corn, and the hot water find its way to the depth, to the root of the corn: and let the infirm person wait for a while upon the biting of the hot water for one hour.
Then release it, for the corn is rooted out with its roots.
So do with them one by one, until you come upon all that are of them in the body.
Then treat them after they are rooted out, with that which generates flesh from ointments.
Concerning the method of cauterization in Trembling.
When trembling occurs in someone from cold in a nerve, or from a quartan fever, or otherwise: then it is necessary that you cauterize it with four cauteries, or five, over the spinal vertebrae of the back, and between each vertebra with a cautery, and on his chest with a cautery, and on his stomach with a cautery.
For trembling ceases with the cautery of the olive, and the health of the cold sickness is hastened.
Concerning the method of cauterizing Albathir.
Chapter LVII.
Sometimes foul things, that is, pustules, are expelled in the body of Albathir, which are made from corrupted gross matter.
Therefore, when they appear, it is necessary first to cauterize every pustule on the head with a delicate cautery made of myrtle wood, whose tip has been ignited. Or with the root of long aristolochia. Or with a lenticular cautery.
And sometimes they are cauterized at the beginning of their expulsion in this manner: and they are not increased: and the superfluity causing them is broken, and they are healed from them.
However, it is necessary that this be done after evacuation with phlebotomy.
Concerning the method of cauterizing in a blood flow from an artery in a wound.
Often a blood flow from an artery occurs, which is cut in a wound, that happens externally, or at the incision of an abscess, or cauterization of a limb, and similar to them: and it becomes difficult to sever it.
Therefore, when this happens to someone, then quickly apply your index finger to the opening of the artery; and press it firmly, until the blood is blocked beneath your finger, and nothing more flows out from it.
Then place small, olive-shaped cauteries, and many more large ones, in the fire; and blow on them until they are very hot.
Then take one of them, whether small or large, according to the wound and the place where the artery is ruptured, and apply the cautery to the vein itself, after you have quickly removed your finger; and hold the cautery until the blood is severed.
If it is expelled when you lift your finger from the artery's opening, and you extinguish the cautery, then quickly take another cautery from the cauteries that are prepared in the fire.
Therefore, do not cease to do this, one after another, until the blood is severed.
And beware, lest you burn the nerve that is there, and another harm befall the sick person.
And know that when blood flows from an artery, then it is not possible to sever it, especially when the artery is large, unless with one of the four methods. Or with cautery, as we have said. Or with extraction, when it is not extracted. For when it is extracted, its extremities contract, and the blood is severed. Or it is tied with a strong ligature. Or let medicinal substances of a nature to sever the blood be placed upon it, and let it be constricted with a fitting cushion constriction.
However, whoever administers its excision with a ligature, and constriction with a cloth, or the application of burning things, and similar matters, does not truly benefit it, unless rarely.
If however this should befall someone, and a physician or medicine be not present for them, then let them begin with the application of the index finger over the opening of the vein itself, as we have narrated, and its strong constriction, until the blood is blocked from the upper part of the wound, and above the artery. And the finger should not be removed, from above it with very cold water, constantly, until the blood is congealed, and thickened, and cut away.
And meanwhile let him consider what it needs from cautery, or from medicine.
Concerning Incision, Perforation, and Phlebotomy in wounds, and similar matters.
We have already said in the First Chapter, every illness in which it is suitable to use cautery with fire and a burning medicine, and the occasions for it, its causes, and its instruments, and the forms of cauteries.
And I have placed there chapters from the head to the foot.
And I will proceed in this chapter the same way, so that the seeker might have his question relieved.
And before I begin that, it is necessary that you learn, O children, that in this chapter is from the terror that is in the first chapter on cauterization.
And on account of that, it is necessary that there be more caution in it, since in the operation that is in this chapter, many times evacuation occurs with blood, with which life stands at the opening of a vein, or an incision over an abscess, or the opening of an exit, or the treatment of a wound, or the extraction of an arrow, or an incision over a stone, and similar things: from all of which is associated trembling, and fear, and death occurs in many of them.
And I warn you, lest that in which there is hesitation befall you, for sometimes there come to you in this art the manners of men with the manners of illnesses. For among them is he who is angry with his illness, and death is easy for him, because of the intensity of what he finds in his illness and the length of its malice. And with illness is the fear that signifies death.
And among them is he who gives his lyre, and enriches you with it, hoping for health, and his illness is harmful.
Therefore you ought not to agree with him who comes to you from among them, whose manner is such. And let your caution be more intense than your greed and your rapacity. And do not presume upon anything from it unless after certain knowledge, which verifies to you that to which a praiseworthy succession has arrived.
And use in the treatment of all your sick people, prognostication and indication of that to which health is fittingly directed.
For in that is help for you concerning the acquisition of fame and glory, and of memory and praise.
May God inspire you, children, with his understanding, and may he not take away from you guidance and inspiration, for it is in his hand.
And I have indeed arranged this chapter by distinguishing it into chapters, according to what preceded in the chapter on the cautery, from the neck to the feet: so that your seeking of it, and what you need from it, may be alleviated.
Concerning the treatment of Water which collects in the heads of children.
Chapter 1.
Know that this ailment often happens to children at birth, or when the midwife tightens the child's head without gentleness.
And sometimes it happens also from a hidden cause which is not known.
And I myself have not seen this illness in others than children: and death has hastened to every one whom I have seen of them: therefore, it has seemed good to me to omit the operation on it.
And I myself have seen one of them, a child whose head was filled with water, and the head grew larger every day until it could not sit upon itself, because of the size of its head: and the moisture increased until he died.
And this moisture, indeed, either gathers between the skin and the bone, or gathers under the bone above the sinus.
And the operation on it, if the moisture is between the skin and the bone, and the abscess is small, then one should make a single fissure in the middle of the head, in width, and the length of the fissure should be like two knots, until the moisture runs out.
And this is the form of a lancet.
If therefore the moisture is more increased, and the abscess is larger, then place those two fissures, according to this form.
And if the moisture is under the bone, and the sign of it is that you see the sutures of the head open from every side, and water is depressed when you press it with your hand inwards, and that is not hidden from you: then you should make a fissure in the middle of the head with three fissures, according to this method.
And after the incision, extract all the moisture. Then bind the fissures with cloths and lint. Then apply a liniment of wine and oil to it until the fifth day.
Then loosen the bandage, and treat the wound with sutures and ointments.
And do not allow the head to be bound with equality. And feed the sick person with all dry foods of little moisture, until the limb is strengthened and healed.
And another method of incision is to consider where the size of the abscess and the collection of water appear.
Because sometimes there is more in the occiput, or in the forehead, or in the right, or in the left.
Therefore, ascend with the incision where the abscess and the filling of water appear to you: and cut it according to what is possible for you.
And observe, lest you cut an artery, and a flow of blood occur, and the sick person die from that flow with the evacuation of moisture.
Concerning the incision of arteries which are behind the ears.
Chapter II.
When cataracts befall someone in the eyes or chest, and it becomes longstanding, and treatment with medicines does not help, then the ultimate treatment for it is the incision of these two arteries.
Therefore, when you wish to cut them, it is necessary that the sick person shave his head. Then rub the place with a rough cloth, so that the arteries appear. Then the sick person should tie his neck with any cloth he has. Then consider where the vein beats. And the places of both are the places which are depressed behind the ears: and it is rarely hidden, except in some men.
Then mark with ink over each: and cut each with a spatula carefully on both sides, the incision reaching to the bone: and let it be done across the width of the head.
And if you wish, insert the spatula under the artery, and cut it upwards with the incision.
And let the length of the incision be like two fingers joined together.
For when a vein is cut, blood flows out with a pulsating flow, which leaps to the interior with frequent jumps.
If however the artery does not appear to the sense, then it is necessary that you measure from the ear a space of three fingers' breadth. Then mark with ink, and cut down to the mouth.
And that which ought to be emitted of blood is six ounces, and perhaps you emit from it more, or less. All that according to the quantity that appears to you from the weakness of the sick person and the fullness of his artery.
Then consider the wound. Then if anything remains at the mouth from the sifac, then cut it, so that a hot abscess does not occur: then bind the wound with bandages of linen cloth.
Then treat it with ointment until it is healed.
Concerning the extraction of arteries, which are in the temples.
Chapter III.
When a man suffers from chronic hemicrania, or acute catarrhs due to acrid humors, and heat in the temple muscles, or old, severe, and similar afflictions; and is treated by methods of medical cure, and it does not help: then we are experienced in these illnesses in the extraction of arteries from the temples, or their cauterization, as we have narrated.
And the method of the operation in extracting them is that the weak person should shave the hairs which are in the temples, and direct himself to the apparent artery in the temple. For it indeed shows itself to you from its pulse, and is rarely concealed except in a few people, or by the severity of cold.
If it is concealed from you, then the weak person should press his head with any cloth of his: then rub the place with a rough cloth. Or steam the place with warm water until it appears to you by clear manifestation. Then take the spatula which I have shaped a little earlier. Then scrape the skin with it gently until you reach the artery. Then insert a hook into it, and pull it upwards until you draw it out of the skin, and cause it to escape from the tissues beneath it on all sides.
If however the artery is fine: then wrap it with the end of the hook. Then cut off a part of it according to the amount by which its extremities will be elongated and contracted, and so that no blood flow occurs. For if it is not cut and if it is not severed, the blood does not rise completely.
Then evacuate six ounces of blood, up to three.
If the artery is large, then it is necessary to tie it in two places with a strong double thread, of silk or of alhohod cords, so that putrefaction does not quickly reach it before the wound's consolidation, and blood flow occurs.
Then cut away the excess which is between the two ligatures. Do that at that hour, or after another hour.
And if you wish, cauterize it with a cautery, up to the bone, with a knife-cautery, until its extremities are cut, and it will stand in place of the same work.
And it is better work, unless the patient has a fever or is of a hot temperament. Because cauterization is from among those things which help in ending humidities. Therefore it is a better help.
And after the extraction of the arteries, the place should be filled with old cotton: and pads well made should be placed upon it.
And after the removal, it should be treated with dry medicines that cause flesh to grow, and with bandages, until it is healed.
If, meanwhile, while you are working, a flow of blood from an artery occurs, then hurry to cut it off with a cauterizing instrument, or to fill the place with zegi; and bind your hand tightly, until the blood is stopped. If none of those are present for you, then place your finger on the area, until the blood clots. And apply the area with water of intense cold, until the sharpness subsides, and it is bound as necessary.
And because what is lighter and easier to extract than an artery, is to cauterize the vein with this cauterium having two blades; and let the sharpness of the two blades be similar to an almond. However, they are of much less sharpness than two blades. Because if they are sharp, like two blades, the cold affects both, and they do not cut flesh with speed. Therefore, since there is some thickness in them, the heat of the fire is retained in them, and the flesh is severed quickly; with this, therefore, the work is better than any other, and lighter, and easier.
And this is the form of cautery.
And let the space between the two blades be the thickness of a finger, after you have marked two places with ink; and place both of them intensely hot, until they reach the bone, and cut the vein in two places, so that that which is between its extremities may be lengthened. For they are not completely consolidated with this cautery.
Chapter IV.
Concerning the treatment of acute and persistent lacrimal discharge from the eyes.
When there is a constant flow of tears, and their course is from the veins that are on the outside of the skull: and no medicinal treatment with medicines helps them, and you see the sick person's face already red, and he feels a crawling sensation on his forehead, like the crawling of ants: and his eyes are weakened, damp, so that their eyelashes are already corroded with the corrosion: and his eyelids are inflamed from the severity of the tears: then the ancients needed this operation in their treatment, and they called this treatment, with a Knife.
And it is that you should instruct the sick person to shave the hairs that are on his forehead. Then, make three equally spaced incisions along the length of the forehead: and let the length of each incision be about two fingers wide, and one incision be equidistant from the length of the nose in the middle of the forehead, and the second slightly along the length away from the movement of the temples, and the third on the other side: and be careful not to cut the arteries that are on either side: and keep your hand away from the continuation of the two lower jawbones, and let the space of each incision be the amount of three joined fingers: and have a piece of sponge prepared, or many dry cloths, with which to absorb the blood.
Then insert a spatula with two sharp ends, whose following form is this. From the incision that is on the temple side, up to the second incision: and scrape away all the skin, that is between the two incisions, with it, as if it were over the bone. Then do that also from the middle incision to the second incision. Then withdraw this spatula, and insert into the first incision again another instrument, which is called a sharp scalpel, with one side smooth, not sharp, on the other side. And this is its form.
And place its sharp side towards the flesh attached with the skin, and its smooth side towards the bone; and drive it, until it reaches the middle fissure; and cut with it all the vessels, which descend from the head to the eyes; except that the incision continues up to that which appears of the skin.
Then do that in the middle fissure up to the other fissure.
And after a quantity of blood has flowed out, express the places from clots of coagulated blood.
Then place in every fissure a linen cloth from old cotton, and place on them cushions soaked in wine, or oil, or vinegar. And see that a hot abscess does not occur.
And on the third day, loosen the bandage, and administer a plentiful infusion with lukewarm water. Then treat it with basil ointment, and with the other remedies used for wounds, until it is healed.
Chapter V.
Concerning the treatment of acute tears and catarrhs to the eyes from the interior of the head.
When acute, sharp, persistent catarrhs occur to someone; and you see his eyes weakened, small, and their appearance already debilitated; and the eyelids ulcerated, and hairs fall from them, and there is sharp, painful, and injurious pain deep within the head, and a consequent sneezing; then, take from among these occurrences that these matters, and catarrhs, do not come except from many veins.
And the better treatment for them is this. And it is that you should order the sick person to shave his forehead. Then make a single incision in the middle of the forehead, or a little further along the breadth, and begin with the incision from the left temple to the right temple; and let the incision extend to the bone; and move your hand away from the temporal muscles, which move during chewing; until, when the bone is exposed; and you will dry all the blood with a sponge.
Then separate the two lips of the incision with old cloth, or with linen strips. Then bind it from above with pads; and soak the pads with wine and oil, so that a hot abscess does not occur.
And when you loosen it, and see that the hot abscess is already lessening: then it is necessary that you rub the bone, until flesh begins to grow on it.
Then treat it with a drying regimen that causes flesh to grow: such as if you take two parts of wheat flour and four parts of colophony; and prepare a plaster from them, and administer it to the birth of flesh in these wounds.
Chapter VI.
Concerning the treatment of that which falls into the Ear.
Everything that falls into the ear is one of four kinds: either a mineral stone, or something similar to a stone, such as iron and glass; or it is from a plant, such as chickpeas and beans; or it is a flowing substance, such as water, vinegar, and similar things; or some animal.
Therefore, when a stone, or a kind of stone, falls into the ear, from those things which are not added to, nor grow in the ear, expose the ear to the sun. If, therefore, you see a stone, then instill into the ear some of violet oil, or sesame oil. Then administer its extraction, and make the person sneeze with condiments, and obstruct the nostrils at the coming of the sneeze, after you place around the ear scraps of cloth, or wool, and extend the ear upward.
For it often comes out with this treatment.
If, however, it does not come out, then administer its extraction with Gestes, that is, with fine forceps: the shape of which is this.
If therefore it comes out with the forceps, it is good.
And if not, then administer its extraction with a fine, single-looped hook.
If therefore it does not come out through that, then make a tube of bronze, and insert the end of the tube into the ear hole deeply, and plug whatever is around the tube with softened wax mixed with oil, so that there is no passage for the wind except through the tube; then extract with your suction with strong traction.
For many times it comes out with what we have narrated. If however it does not come out in this way, then take a small amount of  birdlime or glue, with which birds are caught, and place it at the end of a reed after you have neatly wrapped a bit of cotton around it; then insert it into the ear hole easily, after you have dried the ear from moisture.
If therefore it does not come out with all that we have narrated, then hasten to incision, before a hot abscess or spasm occurs.
And the method of the incision is, that the phlebotomist should first make a weak incision in the cephalic vein, and draw from it blood according to the measure of his strength. Then make the sick person sit between your hands, and turn his ear upwards, and make a small incision at the root of the ear near his fat in the depressed area there: and let the incision be in the shape of a crescent, until you reach the stone; then remove it with whatever instruments are possible for you.
Then close the incision quickly, and care for it until it heals.
If the thing falling into the ear is one of the grains that grow and swell, then administer its extraction.
If however it does not yield to you for extraction, then take a thin, slender spatula, according to this shape.
And administer it with that to cut out that kind of grain that falls into the ear, and do not do it when you think that the grain has already been moistened by the ear's vapor, until you place it into many fragments; then extract them with hooks, or with a fine syringe, or with suction, as we have said, for its extraction is thus relieved.
However, for water that enters the ear, the afflicted person should use sneezing with condiments first, and now fill his ear with some old cotton; and he is lying on that ear, in which the water is. If therefore it emerges through that, it is good.
And if not, then take many stones, according to the length of a finger, thin, smooth, and heat them with fire for a little while, and let the afflicted person insert one of them into the opening of his ear; and let him jump on his foot on that side, and strike with another stone against the stone that is in the ear. Therefore, let him not cease doing that with stone and stone until all the water emerges.
And now water emerges such that you take one from papyrus, or from reed, and insert one end of it into the ear; and light the other end with fire, until most of it is burned. Then again another reed, and do this many times, until all the water emerges; and extract it with a cannula according to what was previously stated concerning the stone.
In extracting an animal that has entered it, if it is of small body, like a flea, and similar to it, treat it as I said in the division.
But if it is of large body, that appears to the senses, then administer its extraction with forceps and hooks; and its removal is easier than all things held within the ear.
In the extraction of a worm generated in the ear, when you treat it with what we said in the division, and in the treatise on distillations, and your treatment does not help: then it is necessary to look at the ear in the sun. If therefore anything of the worms appears there, then extract it with forceps, or with fine hooks.
If, however, nothing appears there, then take a cannula, whose form is this, narrow below, wide above; and insert the slender end into the ear according to the quantity that the infirm patient tolerates; then suck with a strong suction. Do this many times, until all the worms emerge.
If, however, they do not yield to you for exit, then coat the circumference of the cannula with wax, as I said in the section on the stone.
If, however, they do not emerge with what we have said, then administer the distillations which the ancients have already experienced in the destruction of worms: and you will find it in the treatise on distillations.
And let your application of oils and medicines into the ear be with this instrument, whose form is depicted here. As you see, make it from silver, or bronze, narrowed at the bottom, in which there is a small opening, wider above.
Let the part that pushes, which is in the concavity of the cannula, be made of bronze, properly fashioned. And if you wish, take a rod, and wrap a bit of cloth around its end in a neat wrapping; then pour oil, or juice, or whatever medicine you wish from these, into the cannula; and let it be in the ear; then insert the rod with the cloth on top, and express it with a moderate pressure of your hand, until the oil is pushed into the concavity of the ear, and the sick person feels it inwardly; and that which is poured into the ear should already be warmed with fire for a short time; and beware that the thing which is distilled into it is not very cold; for the medicines do not tolerate that.
Chapter 7.
On the treatment of sudden ear blockage.
Sometimes infants emerge from their mothers' wombs unperforated.
And sometimes blockage in the ear canal occurs in people from a wound, or flesh growing in it.
And this blockage sometimes occurs deep within the ear cavity, not followed by sight; and sometimes it occurs near the outer part of the cavity, and sight follows it.
Therefore, that which occurs deep within the ear, and which sight does not encompass, is in many cases difficult to cure.
And in that which is followed by sight, you should place the patient's ear in the sun, and look at it. Then, if you see the blockage appearing, open it with a thin probe, whose form is as follows:  its end should have a small width, part of which is pointed, and the rest of the probe should be smooth on both sides, so as not to injure the ear.
If, therefore, the blockage is from flesh grown in the ear canal, then grasp it with a fine hook and cut it with forceful ease until all the flesh is removed.
If the blockage is deep within the ear, then take a thin, smooth rod, and warm it a little with fire; then insert it into the ear canal.
If, therefore, you sense a blockage, with the patient experiencing difficulty hearing, attempt to open it with care, and be careful not to injure the ear's nerves.
Then place a bandage in the ear, according to the size of the opening, having wrapped it in Egyptian ointment.  Do this for several days, until you are sure of the wound's healing.
Or take a bandage, soak it in rosewater, and sprinkle powdered zigi on it, and use it.
If you see that the ear is already suppurating, then you should remove the bandage, and replace it with another bandage wrapped in a cloth made with rose oil, until the hot abscess subsides; then treat it until it heals.
If, however, a bleeding occurs, then soak a sponge or cloth in cold water and apply it to the ear; and use the rest of the treatment, which cuts off the bleeding as described in the treatise on distillations.
Concerning the treatment of the eyelid's ailment.
Chapter VIII.
These ailments of the eyelids, which occur in the eye, are sometimes moist, and sometimes dry.
Therefore, you should grasp them with forceps or a hook, and cut them off with a spatula from their roots. Then place powdered zegi over the affected area.
If blood flows from them, then cauterize them with a fine, lenticular cautery.
And to cauterize them is better after the incision. For they often return when something remains from their roots. And when they are cauterized with fire, those roots are burned, and they do not return.
Concerning the treatment of granulations of the eyelids of the eye.
Chapter IX.
Often something occurs in the eyelids that resembles granulation in its strength and hardness, and because of this is called Granulation: and it is a collection of thick moisture in the upper eyelid and the lower eyelid.
Therefore the operation in them is, that you consider then, if it is Granulation proceeding in that which appears of the eyelid, and they move to every part, and its removal is easy.
Therefore make a cut across it, along its width. Then scrape it from every side with ease, until it comes out. Then suspend it with a hook, and cut it off.
However, if its removal is not prepared for you unless after you pierce the eyelid with a small incision, then that harms the sick person in some way.
If the cut is large, then stitch it together and treat it until it is healed.
And if it is small, no harm will come to you from it. For ointment restores it and makes it flesh again.
And if the swelling is sloping inwards towards the inner surface of the ear, then invert the eyelid and suspend the swelling with a hook; besides which, you will need an incision and press it from every side.
If the eyelid is extended by incision, that will not harm it in any way.
Then wash the eye after the incision of the swelling with salty water, and care for the place with what heals, until the sick person is healed.
Concerning the treatment of the swelling of the eyelids.
Chapter X.
Axirnath is a fat that is generated in the membranes of the upper eyelid; it mostly occurs in children.  It weighs down their eyes, and they experience morning colds, and cannot look at sunlight, because tears rush to them, and because of this you see them always lying on their faces, and they sleep on them, and their eyelids, beneath their eyebrows, are wet, already covered by the swelling.  The prominence of the Axirnath is apparent to the eye, and when you press the area with your fingers, you feel the Axirnath between them.
And the method of its operation is that the sick person should place their head in your lap.
Then take a linen cloth and prepare bandages on it, and make a circle from that bandage according to the amount that Axirnath contains on all sides. Then place it over it, and press with your fingers on all sides, so that the Axirnath is gathered in the middle of the circle.
Then find the middle of that moisture with a lancet, making incisions across its width, and do not make a larger incision than that which is made in phlebotomy in the depth.
Or it is necessary to cut through the entire skin until you reach the Axirnath, for in many arrangements the Axirnath proceeds from the incision according to the form of a piece of white fat.
Therefore, extract it with the cloth, which you have inserted between your fingers on the outside; and you shall turn your hand to the right and to the left, until it is dispersed; and be careful not to add to the incision, so that you do not reach the eye with the lancet and injure it.
If, however, Axirnath does not appear to you at the beginning of the incision, you shall add a little to the incision with ease, until Axirnath proceeds. Then extract it, as we have said.
Then immerse the cloths in vinegar and water, and place them over the area, and bind it with bandages.
And there are men who grind salt, and place it in the concavity of the incision, so that what remains of that moisture will liquefy.
Then treat it until it heals.
If however a hot abscess occurs in the area, then treat it with soothing plasters, until it heals.
Concerning the methods for Ataxmir of the eye, that is, when the eyelid of the eye softens, causing it to wrinkle improperly.
Chapter XI.
When extra hairs grow in the eyelids, beyond the natural hairs, and grow old, they harm the eye and cause various illnesses, such as frequent tears, eyelid softening, and clouding, until it becomes the cause of the eye's destruction.
And indeed, Ataxmir of the eye is treated according to four methods. Or by cauterization with fire. Or with a sharp medicine, according to what was previously stated in the chapter on cauteries. Or Ataxmir is treated by incision and suture. Or by tubes, according to what I will say.
You should place the head of the sick person in your bosom. Then turn the eyelid of the eye with your left hand.
If therefore it turns, it is good. And if not: insert a needle into it, in which there is thread from the lower part of the eyelid, and cause the needle with the thread to penetrate from above, and let it be near the hairs themselves, that is, near the edge of the eyelid where the hairs grow; and pull the thread upward with the eyelid, and turn it with a lever.
Then find in the inner part of the eyelid beneath the hairs, with the spatula running from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct.
Then withdraw the thread, and place a small cushion of cotton, or cloth, beneath the eyelid; then mark on the eyelid with ink, which should be like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
However, it is necessary that it be shaped according to the size and elevation of the eyelid, since it is already diverse in humans.
For among them is one who needs a large portion of the eyelid to be cut away, according to the amount that is softened by the eyelid.
Then, with the spatula, find along the two lines you marked, and begin from the larger tear duct to the smaller tear duct, and let the fissure be close to the natural hairs, as is the thickness of a ray.
Then insert the hook into one of the two angles of the skin. Then scrape it all away. Then bring together the two edges with a suture, using a needle and fine wool thread; and wipe away the blood, and cause whatever excess thread remains over the two eyebrows to adhere with certain incanescent substances, if you wish to do so; and if not, do not bother.
Then leave the suture and threads for up to three or four days; afterwards, treat it.
And if you wish, leave the wound without sutures, and treat it with that which dries and contracts it. For the eyelid is raised at the sealing and bringing together of the wound: but it will be better.
These methods of treatment the ancients taught to Ataxmir.
However, there is care for the sick person in it, and it is from good operation, and there is no fear in it.
And another method is in Ataxmir also: and it is, that you mark on the eyelid a figure, which should be like the figure of a myrtle leaf, as we have narrated.
Then raise the eyelid with three separate or combined hooks, according to this following form, then cut what is superfluous with small scissors, according to this form, with equal cutting.
If, however, it is not possible for you to hold the hook, and it is not equal: then take a needle in which there is thread, and insert it in the middle of the figure; and insert another thread near the larger tear duct, and a second thread near the smaller tear duct; and gather the threads between your fingers with equality.
Then raise your hand with equal elevation; and raise the skin over which the sign is marked, as we have narrated.
Then join the two edges of the wound with sutures, and care for it until it heals.
If, however, an abscess occurs at your incision, or your fissure: soothe that abscess with cauterium, and similar things, from soothing ointments.
And sometimes it happens to the eyelids also, that its hairs are turned.
Therefore, you should use what we have said about the incision; but the suture is more artificial for both. Concerning the treatment of Ataxmir with reeds.
And it is, that you turn the eyelid, and cut the fissure that is within, according to what I have recounted. Then make two reeds, or two thin sticks, whose length is according to the length of the eyelid, and their width is less than the width of the spatula; and you have already cut at the ends of each from both sides where you hold the threads.
Then carefully attach each to that which is superfluous from the eyelid of the eye, and bind the two reeds from both sides with a strong ligature, and leave it for a few days. For the tightened skin will die and become black and putrefy, until it falls away by itself.
If therefore it is delayed, then cut it with scissors. Then care for it until it heals.
When therefore it is consolidated, the eyelid is raised: and the hairs do not prick.
And this is the form of two tubes, which you shall make according to this figure, and quantity in length and width.
Chapter XII.
Concerning the raising of pricking hairs in the eye with a needle.
The operation in it is that you should take a thin needle, and introduce into it a thin, smooth silk thread. Then tie its ends, and make a very small knot on each end: and let the length of the threads be one palm.
Then place another thin thread under it in the Alinxotati, and tie its ends if you wish.
Then let the sick person place their head in your lap, and be near the sun so that the operation is revealed; for it is a subtle or minute operation.
Then introduce the needle with the alinxotati into the root of the eyelid, and make it penetrate into the eyelid quickly, until you pull it out over the natural hair.
Then pull the hair up over the alinxotati: and introduce that hair into the alinxotati; if there is one, or two, or three, not more.
Then pull your hand with it, until the hair emerges with the alinxotati into the eyelid with the natural hair.
However, if the alinxotati is pulled out, and the hair does not emerge with it: you will pull the alinxotati downwards, with the thread that you have placed in it, until the alinxotati emerges from the upper opening, and you see it with your eye's vision. Then you will pull the alinxotati and the thread: and remove both, and close the eye: and leave it closed for two days, or three, until the hair adheres to its place, and flesh grows over it.
If the hair is short, then either associate a long hair from the natural hairs with it, and lift it with it: or leave it until it lengthens after a time, and then lift it.
Chapter XIII.
When this Alxatrati happens to the eye, it is called Leporina.
And this Alxatrati is either natural or accidental.
Therefore, the accidental one arises from the consolidation of a wound, or incision, or cautery, and similar things to them.
And the method of treatment in it is that you find that consolidation, and that you separate its edges, and place in what is between them a linen pad, and bind it, until it is healed.
And it is not necessary that you use in its treatment things that dry and constrict: for if you do that, the Alxatrati returns worse than it was: indeed, use in it things that soften, such as Greek hay; and embrocate with water in which althea, flaxseed have been cooked; and with the unguent dyaquilon, which has been liquefied with some of the oils; and wrap it with the linen pad, and treat it with that.
And the treasure of your treatment is that you should strive with all your ingenuity so that it is not consolidated according to the form it was in at first.
And this operation, which we have described, is only a certain rectification of the Alxatrati, so that the shape of the place does not return to what it was entirely.
Concerning the treatment of Alxatrati which occurs in the lower eyelid, and is a wrinkling or contraction upwards to the right and left.
Chapter XIV.
This Alxatrati, which occurs in the lower part, is truly called Alxatrati. And it is natural, and it is accidental.
And the accidental one results from a wound, or a fissure, or a cauterization, and similar things.
And the method of operation in it is to take a needle, in which there is a doubled thread, and to dip it into the flesh, and to make it penetrate from the left tear duct to the right tear duct, until the thread reaches in two extremities of flesh.
Then extend the flesh upwards with the needle, and cut it with a broad spatula. If therefore the shape of the eyelid returns as it should, it is good.
And if not, take a radius, and place it in the fissure, and turn the eyelid with it: and split two fissures in the inner side of the eyelid, and let the extremities of the two fissures be from the two angles of the incision, until they meet and a corner is made from them: until when they are aggregated the shape of them becomes this shape, and it is the letter lambda Greek.
Then remove that flesh by the quantity that is its sharp lower side, from that which follows the eyelid. Then join the separated parts: sew it with a wool thread.
Then treat them with what we have said, of softening medicines, and with bandages, until they are healed.
And if an Alxatrati occurs from a fissure, and a suture, or cauterization: then it is necessary that you split a flat fissure under the hairs of the eyelids, also according to what preceded. Then separate between the two lips with a bandage, according to what we have said.
And the sum of the discourse on treating Alxatrati, when it is above or below, is that the operation should proceed according to the form of the Alxatrati.
For it is itself of many varieties in form: and a skillful craftsman prepares his ingenuity with whatever means is possible to him, until he restores the figure to its natural form, or nearly so, and the infirm one himself, according to what is suitable for him from the operation and instruments in various arrangements.
Chapter XV.
Concerning the adhesion of the eyelid to the conjunctiva, or cornea.
Sometimes this consolidation happens to many people, so that the upper eyelid is consolidated with the white of the eye, and hinders it from its natural functions.
Therefore, you should introduce the tip of the probe under the eyelid, and lift it upward; or extend it with a hook. Then, cut the adhesion with a thin spatula, which should not be sharp, but rather slightly blunt, so that the patient is not disturbed during the operation, and the spatula is removed and does not injure the eye with its sharpness.  Your incision should be like scraping off that adhesion with ease, until the eyelid returns to its natural form and the entire adhesion is healed.
Then, pour salty water into the eye, washing it with water; or distil it into it, from a sieve made of dissolved zinc with water.
Then separate the eyelid and the eye with linen thread. Place wool soaked with egg white over the eye. After three days, use a sieve and consolidants until healed.
Concerning the incision of the toenail and the prominence of the tear ducts.
Chapter XVI.
Know that the toenail is according to two ways. Or it is nervous and is similar to hard, fine leather. Or it is not nervous, similar to frozen white moisture: which, when touched with iron, or you try to take it with a hook, is cut, and the hook does not fix itself to it.
And both kinds do not begin, unless from the larger tear duct, until it gradually proceeds until it covers the sight, and hinders light, and the movement of the eye.
And the method of operation in its incision is: that the sick person place their head in your bosom. Then open his eyes, and raise the eyelid with your hand. Then collect the nail with a hook of little duplication, and extend it upwards. Then take a needle, and insert into it a hair from the hair of a horse, or ox, or cow: and let the thread be strong, and double the tip of the needle a little, and fasten it in the middle of the nail: and pierce it with the needle, and tie the nail with the thread, and extend it upwards: and scrape the side of the nail that follows the pupil with the hair, as if you were sawing it with the hair until its end.
Then cut the remainder at the root of the tear duct with a small spatula, or with small scissors: and leave the natural tear duct flesh, so that a continuous flow of tears does not result from its incision.
And sometimes the nail is extended with a hook alone, or with a thread, as we said. Then scrape with a gentle spatula, whose shape is this.
And be careful that the spatula does not touch the horny membrane, and a rupture occurs in it, and the pupil is pierced.
Then after the incision, apply some crushed salt to the eye, or a preparation made from ziniar, and leave it until the next day. Then treat it with what is necessary until it is healed.
If the nail is not nervous, and you cannot insert a needle into it, nor is a hook fixed in it, then this type of nail is often treated with medicines and healed as I have described in the division.
If you wish to cut it, then open the sick person's eye and take a fine, gentle, weakly sharpened spatula, according to this form. And scrape the nail from above with care. If you see that it becomes thin and breaks, and the scraping is imprinted on it, then distil from your hour into the eye a sief made from ziniar, or from red sief, or crushed salt; and plug the eye until another day.
Then repeat the operation upon it until the whole of it is removed. Unless there be a hot abscess in the eye, in which case you should leave it and treat the abscess until it is healed.
Then repeat the operation upon it with scraping until it is healed.
However, if a fleshy prominence of the tear duct causes great harm to the eye, suspend it with a hook and cut away a portion of it; and do not make the incision too deep, so that the flow of tears is not interrupted.
Then instill into the tear duct a red, or prepared, ziniari, until healed.
Concerning the incision of Alguardenic, and flesh grown in the eye.
Chapter XVII.
Sometimes in some people's eyes, red flesh grows, either compounded or superimposed, until it covers the pupil, or approaches, or presses against the eyelids, and perhaps turns the eyelids outward: and it resembles a rose-hip.
And the treatment in it is also that the sick person should put his head in your bosom. Then open his eyes, and collect that entire flesh with suitable hooks for it: and hold it with a single forceps, or tweezers.
Then cut the flesh first, until all of it is finished with the scalpel. And be cautious of the eye, so that you do not injure it during the operation: and let your incision, with which you cut it, be either with a scalpel, as we described in the incision of the hoof, or with small scissors, with which the asebel is collected, according to the form it will take after this chapter.
When therefore your incision is complete, and the entire alguardenici is removed, then fill the eye with crushed salt; or distill into it red powder, and similar things from corrosive medicines: and place over the eye externally a poultice with the white of an egg, so that it is secured from a hot abscess.
If anything of the alguardenici remains, and hot blood dominates you, and you fear a hot abscess, then leave the eye and treat it with that which soothes the abscess.
Then repeat the operation on it again until it is healed.
And do likewise with added flesh that occurs in the eye of this kind.
However, it is necessary that you avoid treating any weak eye by natural means, or by accidental means, operating with iron: and do not insert your hand into any of these operations, until you see that the member is tolerant of that operation.
Concerning the collection of Sebel from the eye.
Chapter XVIII.
Sebel are red veins that are woven over the eye, and prevent the sight from performing its function; and they weaken the eye with the length of days.
Therefore, it is necessary for you first to consider if the eye in which there is Sebel is strong, and there is no other illness in it besides Sebel; then collect the Sebel from it.
And it is so that you instruct the sick person to place their head in your lap.
Then suspend those veins with one hook, or two according to your judgment; and let the hook be a thin double hook, according to this form, or let there be two hooks graduated on one body, according to this form.
Then collect those veins with small scissors with delicacy, and wipe away the blood hour after hour, until you see those veins removed from the eye and they dissolve; and beware that you do not injure the eye with the ends of the scissors.
And let your operation be in the midday sun, and be very firm in your operation, so that you do not injure anything but those veins.
And when you have finished, then instill into the eye green or red sief, so that its sharpness corrodes what remains of the scab.
If however it is not possible for you to collect it all in that hour, then plaster the eye with that which soothes the eye's pain, and leave it for a few days, until its pain is calmed; and secure it from a hot abscess; then again treat it according to the same method, until it is healed.
And this is the form of the scissors.
Concerning the reduction of the arissatum to the nose.
Chapter XIX.
Physicians call the Arissatum a fistula, which you treat with a cautery, or with a burning sharp medicine, according to its previous description.
And if it does not heal, then there is no skill in it; unless you should cut open the abscess near its origin and extract all the moisture and pus within it until the bone is exposed.
When, therefore, the bone is exposed, and you see corruption or blackness upon it, then scrape it with an instrument of this form.
And it is called an Asperi head.
Let it be made of tempered iron, and let its head be round like a azir, which shall be pointed by filing or by a fine aliskilferi point, and place it upon the corrupted place of the bone.
Then revolve it between your fingers, and you should press your hand a little while, until you know that the corruption has been scraped off; and do this several times.
Then restore the place with drying, astringent medicines.
If therefore the place is consolidated, and flesh arises in it, and the course of pus is cut off; and it remains for forty days, and is not renewed, and does not become an abscess, and no accident happens in it; then know that it is now healed.
And if not, then there is no remedy for it, except to reduce the fistula to the nostril opening, according to this form.
And it is, that you expose the second bone with an iron instrument, or with a sharp medicine; and when the bone is exposed, then take a drill according to this form, let its iron extremity be triangular; and let its wood be a tapered pyramid down to the tip, as you see.
Then place it on the bone itself: and let it be near the tear duct; and move your hand slightly away from the eye. Then revolve your hand with the drill, until it penetrates the bone, and the patient feels it; through this, that he finds air coming out of it, when he holds his hand over his nose.
Therefore, then restore the place with astringent drying medicines, as I taught you.
When therefore flesh arises, and the place hardens, then the matter which was discharged to the exterior returns to the nose; therefore it is lighter for the patient.
Concerning the reduction of the prominent eye.
Chapter XX.
When the eye protrudes completely, and no damage or impairment occurs to the sight, then it is necessary to loosen the patient, then bleed him, afterward apply cupping to the neck without scarification, but with suction.
Then apply a liniment to the eye made from acacia, aloe, frankincense, and sarcocolla. Then bind the eye from above over the plaster with many bandages.
And the manner of the bandages is this: that you take many soft cloths, and each cloth is doubled four times; make many of them; then place them one over the other, according to the quantity needed for the eye; then bind them with a hard cloth with strong constriction.
And again apply cupping with suction without scarification.
Then remove the cupping instruments, and leave the eye bound day and night.
However, if the bandage softens again, then it is necessary to bind it tightly; then remove the bandage after a day and a night, as we have said.
If therefore you see the eye has returned to the state of the uvea tunic, it is good; and if not, then again apply the plaster, bandages, constriction, and cupping, until it is healed.
Of the incision of the uvea tunic.
Chapter XXI.
When a rupture occurs in the uvea tunic, and then it protrudes and proceeds outside the eyelids like a uvea grain, and disfigures the human form because of it, and one wants to cut it: then the operation on it is as I describe: and it is that you introduce a needle into the root of the uvea from the lower to the upper part.
Then introduce another needle, on which is a double thread, from the tear duct side, and make it penetrate; and leave the first needle according to its position.
Then cut the place of the double thread, and tie with it the part of the uvea in the circuit of the needle; and tighten it very much.
Then pull out the needle, and place on the eye wool steeped in the white of an egg; and leave it, until the thread falls with the uvea.
Then treat the eye with that which strengthens it, until the wound is healed.
And sometimes the uvea is tightened from every side with a circle of cloth, until the eye adds great prominence.
Then it is pierced with a fine spatula, which reaches the depth of the eye, and the white moisture runs out, and the eye sinks in, and enters immediately; then tighten it until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of Alcomnat.
Chapter XXII.
This disease, called Alcomnat, is nothing but pus that collects in the eye, similar to descending water, and is not itself.
And the method of treatment in it is to have the sick person sit on a prepared seat, or stand upright.
Then take his head in your hands at two points, and move it until you see the pus reach the lower parts of your eyes. Then let it be fixed, and do not let it descend, and let the light be released; and let the sick person see things as he used to; and if it did not descend lower, know that it is water.
If however, the descent of the pus is not prepared with what we have said, then make the sick person sit between your hands. Then take a thin spatula, and with it make a small incision over the corneal membrane near the junction of the conjunctiva and the cornea, at the limbus, until the pus comes out.
When it comes out, then instill warm water in which you have mixed honey into the eye; or water in which you have already boiled fenugreek and honey; then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of water that descends into the eye, or cataract.
Chapter XXIII.
We have already discussed the types of water in division, and what is suitable for piercing, the speech declaring and explaining; then take that same [substance] according to the health.
Then it is necessary that you make the weak person sit between your hands in a square shape, opposite the light, near the sun; and bind his healthy eye, and tightly bind it; then, with your right hand, if the eye in which the water is, is the left eye, or with your left hand, if it is the right eye, you should press the eyelid of his eye.
Then take with your right hand a small stone, if the eye is left, or with your left hand, if the eye is right.
Then place the tip of the small stone close to the edge of the visible whitish part of the eye, the smaller tear duct; then push the small stone with force and turn your hand with it until it penetrates the white of the eye; and you will feel that the small stone has now reached the empty area.
And it is necessary that the measure of the small stone's path to the depth be the measure of the space from the pupil to the end of the blackness; and that is the edge of the eye. For you will see them in the very pupil of the eye's vision, because of the clarity of the cornea.
Then place the small stone to the place where the water is. Then press it downward, one time after another.
If therefore the water descends immediately, then the sick person sees that on which he opens his sight immediately, and the small stone in his eye.
Then rest a little. If therefore the water descends, then put it down the second time, except that the small stone emerges.
When therefore it is fixed, and does not ascend: then extract the small stone with ease: and you turn your hand gradually.
Then dissolve some clear gem salt in water: and wash the eye inside with it. Then place all the wadding, or wool soaked in rose oil and egg white, outside the eye, and bind the healthy eye with it.
We, and those of our time, put crushed cumin with egg white upon it.
If the small stone does not yield to you in entering the eye because of its hardness, since it is from people whose eye is very hard, then it is necessary to take a spatula, which is called Alberid, of which this is the shape.
Therefore, scrape with it only the conjunctiva itself, and do not put your hand into the hole: and it is only so that you make a subtle path to the small stone's place.
Then introduce the small stone, according to what we have said.
And at the completion of your work, let a suitable bedroom be prepared for the sick person, in which they sleep on their back in a dark room: and let them be forbidden from all movement, and from coughing: and place their food such as to soothe their nature: and let them not move their head to the right, nor to the left at all.
And let the bandage remain according to its placement until the third day.
Then loosen it in that dark room, and test his sight: and make him see things. Then reduce the bandage until the seventh day.
And it is not appropriate to do this at the time of treatment, or immediately after the incision; rather, it is necessary to delay it, because water ascends quickly through forceful vision.
If, however, a hot abscess occurs, then it is necessary to loosen the eye before the seventh [day], and treat it with that which calms the abscess, until it is calmed. Then, release the sight itself, and place over his face an oral [application] by which his sight is governed, for a few days, him being in that dark room.
Then let him gradually leave the house and associate with his own people.
And know that in zaltea a student is not excused from observing [or looking at] things at times; then he may dare to do the operation.
And indeed, one of those who are from Alayrach has come to us, who said that a magdan, perforated, was made in Alayrach, into which water is drawn.
However, I have not seen anyone in your land who has done this; nor have I read it in any of the books of the ancients; and it is possible that it is new.
And these are the shapes of the species from the magdan, so that whoever is ignorant of them may stand upon them.
Let them be made of proper metal; and let their extremities be triangular with this same thinness, and pointed.
Chapter Twenty-Four.
Concerning the treatment of flesh born in the nose.
Sometimes various growths are born in the nose, of which some resemble a scorpion with many feet; and of this is cancerous flesh, stony, of dark color; and of it is that which is soft flesh, not dark in color.
In that therefore which of these flesh is soft, not deceitful, nor cancerous, it is necessary that you make the sick person sit between your hands facing the ground, and open his nostrils, and thrust a hook into that flesh; then draw it outward, and cut what you have grasped of it with a thin, sharp spatula on one side, until you know that all the flesh has been removed.
If anything remains of it whose removal is not possible, then scrape it with one of the delicate instruments with ease, until nothing remains of it. If, however, blood overcomes you, or a hot abscess occurs, then apply to it what is appropriate; or if it is of malignant abscesses, then hurry in cauterizing it, until the blood is cut off, and all the flesh is removed.
Then vinegar, and water, or wine, should be placed in the nose after the incision. If however the nose opens, and moisture flows from it to the palate, then know that it is now healthy.
If, however, the moisture does not penetrate as it should, then know that within it is flesh growing on the upper bones of the nostril, to which the instrument did not reach with the incision.
Then it is necessary to insert a linen thread of some thickness, and tie many knots on it; and put between each knot a quantity of a finger, or less; and let the infirm person, that he send one extremity of the thread into the nose with a probe, or with whatever is possible for him, after he has made it similar to azer; and pull his odor, until it reaches the caassum, and emerges upon his throat.
And many times indeed boys in schools do something similar to this Attomi; and it is an easy thing for him who wishes.
Then place the two ends of the thread, one end which emerges from the nose, and the other which remained in the nose. Then and with the saw, cut the flesh with the knots that are on the thread. Do that until you know that the flesh is now with the knots of the thread.
Then pull out the thread, and put into the nose after cleansing the blood a cloth that you have soaked in Egyptian ointment: do this for three days, or more, until the ointment corrodes all that remains of the flesh.
Then place at the end of the matter in the nose a leaden cannula for several days, until it is healed.
If however it needs a treatment that will dry it out, use that.
For performing nasal douching, with which oils and other medicines are instilled into the nose, make it of silver, or bronze; let it be similar to a small lamp, open like a shell; and let its passage be similar.
And if you wish, make it a closed cannula, like a reed.
And the shell of the instrument for nasal douching should be full, uncovered; to which there should be a handle at its end, with which you hold it, when you heat oil in it, or whatever you wish from juices, and fluid substances.
Chapter XXV.
Often a wart arises at the tip of the nose, and grows larger and larger with the days, until its appearance becomes unsightly.
And therefore it is necessary, that you cut it off at the beginning of its appearance, and remove all of it. Then place upon the area either a cautery or a burning medicine in its stead.
If therefore its incision is delayed until it grows large, then consider, if it is stony, hard, of dark color, with little sensation: then do not touch it with iron; for it is a cancerous abscess.
For often I have seen that one who incised such an abscess has been visited by great harm.
And if the abscess is soft to the touch, not dark in color, and you see that incision is possible throughout its entirety, then evacuate the infirmity, and cut it open without fear, without hesitation; and treat the area with that which dries and constricts, until it heals.
Chapter XXVI.
Concerning the suturing of the nose, lip, and ear.
Know that when a separation of continuity occurs in one of these cartilages, little help is afforded in the operation, except in certain people.
Therefore, when something of that sort happens to someone, you should consider if it is a fresh wound with its own blood; and then, gather the edges of the wound together with stitches, then treat it until it heals.
And if the separation of continuity has already caused the lips to separate, and each part has become sound, then it is necessary to scrape away all the part from its visible skin until it bleeds. Then gather the two lips together with stitches, and tighten them, and powder the area with powdered dragon's blood and crushed olibanum, and place a piece of diafinicon plaster, and another of the consolidating plasters, over the powders; and leave it bound for two or three days. Then loosen it, and change the medicine, and leave it until the threads are cut off by themselves. Then treat it with ointment until it heals.
The method of stitching is to bring the separation of continuity together either with needles, as we described in the stitching of the belly, or to bring the separation together with thread, as I have made you know.
Chapter XXVII.
On the extraction of lip nodules.
Many people are often afflicted with hard abscesses resembling herb seeds on the inner parts of their lips; and some of them are smaller, and some larger.
Therefore, it is necessary to turn back the lip, and to open a passage over each nodule, and to lift it with a hook, and to cut it off from every side.
Then fill the place after the incision with pounded zegi, until the blood ceases.
Then it should be rinsed with vinegar, and the place where there is astringency should be treated with it, until the wounds are healed.
Chapter XXVIII.
On the incision of flesh grown on the gum.
Flesh often grows upon the gum, which the ancients call *abuluss*.
Therefore, you should suspend it with a hook; or hold it with forceps, and cut it at its root, and let the pus, or blood, run.
Then place on the affected area ground zegi, or other astringent, drying powders.
If that flesh returns after treatment, for it often does, then cut it again and cauterize it; for it will not return after cauterization.
Chapter XXIX.
Concerning the removal of teeth with iron.
Sometimes rough, foul, and sometimes darkened, yellowish, or greenish crusts collect on the surfaces of the teeth from the inner and outer sides and between the gums, until the corruption reaches the gum, and the teeth become unsightly because of it.
Therefore, you should have the sick person sit between your hands, and his head in your lap, and scrape the molar and tooth where you see the crusts, and a substance like sand, until nothing remains of them, and do the same with the blackness, greenness, and yellowness, and the others, until they are cleaned.
If therefore that which is in them from the beginning of the scraping is removed, it is good; and if not, then repeat the scraping on them another, second, and third day, until finally you achieve what you desire.
And know that a tooth requires scrapers of various shapes and many forms, according to how it is prepared for your operation; because the scraper used to scrape the tooth from the bottom is different from the scraper used to scrape between the teeth, according to different shapes.
And have these shapes of scrapers prepared, which you see.
Chapter Thirty.
Concerning the extraction of toothache.
You should treat the tooth from its pain with all skill, and be slow in its eradication. For there is nothing to replace its function when it is extracted, since it is a subtle substance, until when there is no excuse for its extraction, then it is necessary when the sick person is forced to extract it, that it may be strengthened, and the aching tooth be certified by you.
For often pain deceives the sick person, and he thinks that it is in the tooth itself and extracts it; then the pain does not cease until the sick person's tooth is extracted.
For we have already seen that from the operation of phlebotomists.
Therefore, when the aching tooth itself is certified by you, then it is necessary that you scarify around the tooth with a spatula, in which there is something of virtue, until the gum is loosened on every side; then move it with your fingers, or with delicate forceps, at first gently, until you break it.
Then place large forceps in a good position on it, and place the patient's head between your knees; which you have already held well, so that it does not move; then pull the tooth straight, so that you do not break it.
If therefore it does not come out, then take some of those instruments, and insert it under it from every side with ease, and try to move it, as you did at first.
If however the tooth is perforated or corroded, then it is necessary that you fill that hole with cloth; and bind it with the tip of a fine radius, so that it does not break at the time of your constriction upon it with forceps.
And it is necessary that you meticulously do the scarification around the gum from every side very much.
And guard your strength, so that you do not break it, and a part of it remains, and returns upon the sick person from that malice which may be greater pain to him at first.
And beware, lest you do that which ignorant phlebotomists do in their haste and boldness in removing it, beyond what we have described.
For they often cause great illnesses in men, which are harder to break, and all or some of its roots remain; or to extract it with certain jawbones, as we have witnessed many times.
Then it should be washed after its removal with wine, or with vinegar and salt.
If a flow of blood occurs from the locations (for this often happens), then take some of the zegi and fill the place with it; and if not, cauterize it if the zegi is not helpful to you.
The forms of the fine forceps with which the tooth is moved initially should have long extremities, a short handle, and be thick, so that they do not double when pressed on the tooth; they should be like the handles of thick things, so that when you tighten them, they do not yield nor double; with short extremities, and made of strong iron or well-made steel, with straight extremities; and on their extremities should be teeth, some of which fit into others, and grip with a suitable, strong constriction.
And sometimes the extremities are shaped like the beak of a bird called a stork, and they are of strong constriction.
Chapter XXXI.
Concerning the removal of teeth, and the extraction of fractured jawbones.
When, during the removal of a tooth, a piece remains, then it is necessary to place a poultice of butter on the site for one or two days, until the area softens.  Then introduce to it forceps, or pliers, whose extremities resemble the beak of a bird called a stork. This is the shape of the forceps.
Their extremities should be shaped like a file inside, or like an aliskilfegi.
If, therefore, it does not yield to extraction with these forceps, then it is necessary to carefully cut around the root, and expose all the flesh, and insert an instrument called a small lever, whose shape is this.
Let it be short in length, somewhat thick, and not brittle, so that it does not break.
If, therefore, the root comes out with it, it is good; and if not, it is necessary to help with these other instruments, whose shapes are these.
The shape of the first is triangular with a thickened extremity.
The shape of a triangular, slender extremity.
And with other instruments and tools which preceded in the matter of teeth.
There is also help with this instrument, resembling a large hook, of this form: a triangular, twisted extremity, thickened so it will not break, and not soaked.
And know that instruments for teeth are many; and likewise other instruments are not reckoned, and the skilled craftsman, subtle in his art, finds for himself the instrument, according to which the operation and the sicknesses themselves are signified.
Because there are illnesses for which the ancients did not mention an instrument, due to the diversity of their species.
If, however, a bone breaks from the jaw, or from other bones of the mouth, or it rots, then investigate it in its place with what suits it from the instruments and forceps which I have mentioned in the extraction of roots, and help with the gest, whose form is this.
Let there be a gradual thickening in it, so that the bone is grasped or held with it, and does not escape and move, until the bone emerges; and restore the place with suitable medicines to it.
If, however, there is a small rot in the bone, then scrape it of its rot, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter XXXII.
Of the sawing of prominent teeth above others.
When teeth protrude not according to their natural course, a deformed shape results from it; and especially when this happens in women and young girls, namely the ones that are for sale.
It is therefore necessary that you consider. For if a tooth is born after another tooth, and is not positioned properly, or if sawing or filing it is not possible, then remove it; and if it is attached to another tooth, then cut it away with this instrument, of which this is the form.
It is similar to a small beak, and is made of tempered iron, with a very sharp tip.
And let your incision, by which you cut it, be made over many days, and because of the tooth's hardness, and so that another tooth is not moved.
If however, it is prominent enough for filing, then file it with a file of tempered iron, of this form.
Let it be entirely of tempered iron, and its handle of very small points, like the file with which needles are made; with which the tooth is filed gradually over many days with ease, so that the tooth is not moved, and falls out; then smooth it, and finally shave it with some razors.
If a tooth is fractured, and the piece injures the tongue: then it must be filed also, until the roughness of that fracture is removed, and it is made even; and flattened, and does not injure the tongue, nor impair speech.
Chapter XXXIII.
Of the retention of teeth with gold wires.
When the front teeth suffer concussion and displacement from a blow or fall, and the patient cannot bite on what they eat lest it fall out; and you have treated them with astringent remedies, and the treatment has been unsuccessful; then the expedient is that they be bound with gold wire: for silver becomes green, and rots after days; but gold is never changed.
And let the wire be medium in fineness and thickness according to the amount it will take between the teeth.
And the method of retention is this: take a wire, and insert twice its length between two healthy teeth; then weave the two ends of the wire between the loosened teeth, whether one or several, until you reach with the weaving to a healthy tooth on the other side. Then repeat the weaving back to the part from which you began; and tie your hand with care and skill, until it is entirely firm; and let your tightening of the wire be near the roots of the teeth, so that it does not loosen.
Then cut the excess ends of the wire with scissors; and bring both together, and turn them with a gesture; and hide them between the healthy tooth and the loose tooth, so that they do not injure the tongue; then let it be so tight that it remains in place.
If however it loosens or is cut, tie them with another wire, and it will stay with it for the whole time.
And this is the method for teeth, and the form of retention of two healthy teeth and two loose teeth, as you see.
And sometimes it happens that one or two teeth return to their places after being dislodged, and are held as we have described, and remain; and an artisan, prudent and experienced, does not do this.
And sometimes a piece of bone is adapted, and it is made like the shape of a tooth, and placed in the gap, by which the tooth is strengthened and tied, as we said: and it remains, and serves with it for a long time.
Chapter Thirty-Four.
Sometimes this ligament which occurs under the tongue is either natural, with which a man is born, or accidental, from a wound which has already healed.
And the operation on it is that you open the sick person's mouth at his head in your presence, and raise his tongue; then cut that nervous ligament across, until the tongue is released from its restraint.
If there is something of hardness and knotting in it, and it is from the consolidation of a wound: then apply a hook to it, and split it across, until you reach the ligament, and the knotting is released.
And beware, lest there be a fissure deep within the flesh, and you cut an artery there, and a flow of blood result.
Then wash in the immediate area of the incision with rose water, or with vinegar, or with cold water.
Then place a linen bandage under the tongue, which the patient should hold all night; so that it will not consolidate again.
If a flow of blood occurs, then place pounded zegi on the area. However, if blood overcomes you, then cauterize the area with a lentil-shaped cautery, which is suitable for it; then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter XXXV.
Sometimes an abscess occurs under the tongue, resembling a small frog, preventing the tongue from its natural function; and perhaps it enlarges to such an extent that it fills the mouth.
And the operation in it is that you open the sick person's mouth before the sun: and consider the abscess.
If therefore you see it of a dark color, or black, hard, to which the sick person has no feeling: then do not touch it, for it is cancer.
And if it is inclined towards whiteness, in which there is moisture, then insert a hook into it, and cut it with a thin spatula, and free it from every side. If blood overcomes you during your operation, then place crushed zegi upon it until the blood is stopped; then return to your operation until you extract it with its fullness.
Then it should be washed with vinegar and salt: and treat it with the rest of the appropriate treatment for it, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of an abscess of the two tonsils.
Chapter XXXVI.
Sometimes there occur between the throat, i.e., the glands, which resemble algodad, which occur externally: and they are called the two tonsils: which when you treat with what we said in the division, and they do not heal, then consider.
If therefore it is a hard abscess, of dark colour, with little sensation, then do not touch it with iron.
And if it is of red colour, and its root is thick, then do not touch it even with iron, fearing a flow of blood, indeed leave it until it matures. And then either you will pierce it, or it will burst by itself.
And if it is white in colour, round, and its root is subtle, then it is necessary to cut it.
And the operation upon it is that you consider before its operation, if its abscess has calmed, its heat completed peacefully, or it is diminished by some diminution: then, therefore, make the sick person sit in front of the sun, and let his head be in your bosom: and open his mouth, and let the attendant take in their hands, and press his tongue downwards with an instrument, of which this is the shape.
Let it be made of silver, or bronze, which is as subtle as a knife.
Therefore, when you press the tongue with it, and the abscess is shown to you, and your sight falls upon it, then take a hook, and fix it in the tonsil, and pull it outwards as much as you can, besides pulling with it something from the coverings.
Then cut it with an instrument, of which this is the following form. It resembles shears; however, its extremities should be curved, so that the opening of each of them is directly opposite the other's sharp point: therefore, take care that it is made of tempered iron or imbued with alfuled.
If however this instrument is not present, then cut it with a spatula, of which this is the form. Be sharp on one side, not sharp on the other.
And after you cut one tonsil, cut the other according to this same type of incision.
Then after the incision, let the sick person gargle with cold water, or with vinegar and water.
If however there occurs a flow of blood, let him gargle with water in which the pomegranates' peels have been boiled: or the myrtle leaves, and similar ones from astringents, until the flow of blood is stopped: then treat him, until he is healed.
And sometimes other abscesses arise in the throat, besides the two tonsils. Therefore, cut those according to what I have said in the incision of the two tonsils equally.
And indeed I have already treated a woman from an abscess which had arisen between her throat, which was drawing her to blackness, few sensations, which had almost closed her throat: and the woman breathed with a narrow passage, and I had already forbidden her eating and drinking water: and she had already come to death if she had remained for one day or two days.
And the abscess had risen so much that it had emitted two branches, until they had emerged above the two nostrils.
Therefore, I hastened and fixed a hook in one of them. Then I pulled it, and a large portion came out from it.
Then I cut it off where I grasped it from the nostril.
Then I did the same to that which emerged from the other nostril.
