Then I opened its mouth, and pressed its tongue; and afterward I fixed a hook in the abscess; then I cut off a part of it, and only a little blood ran from it: therefore the woman's throat was healed, and she immediately began to drink water, and then to eat.
Therefore I did not cease to cut from that abscess many times for a long time, and the abscess succeeded the place I was cutting, until it was prolonged excessively, along with it.
Therefore I devised and cauterized the abscess between the throat.
Therefore it stood away from the addition; then I walked away from it, and I did not know what God did with it after me.
Concerning the incision of the abscess of the uvula.
Chapter XXXVII.
When the catarrh descends to the uvula, and it becomes abscessed, and is long, then it is called perpendicular; and if it is in the lower part thick, round, then it is called a grape.
When it is treated with what we said in the division, and the treatment does not help; and you see that the abscess, which was hot, has settled away from it, and is thin; then it is necessary that you cut it.
And what is collected from it, round, and has no length, and is bloody, or of a brown color, or black, and has no feeling: then it is necessary that you avoid its incision. For there is fear upon the weak one.
Therefore, when you see it according to the manner which I said, from its whiteness and length, you should make the sick person sit facing the sun; and press his tongue with the instrument, of which the account preceded; then fix the hook into the uvula and pull it downwards; and cut it with one of the two instruments which I said in the incision of the two tonsils.
And it is necessary that you do not cut from it, except that which is added beyond the natural limit without addition.
For if you cut from it more, you will harm the voice and speech.
Then after the incision, use that which we have narrated in the incision of the two tonsils, and treat it until it is healed.
If, however, the sick person is frightened by the incision, then it is necessary that you use skill in cauterizing it without fear or terror.
And the method of cautery in it is not, except with sharp water; and it is, that you place the head of the sick person in your bosom. Then press his tongue with the instrument that we have said. Then take from the water that I have said to you in the chapter on cauterization, and make with it quicklime that is not extinguished, and place it neither thick nor thin, and fill from it the concavity of this instrument, of which this is the form.
Let the concavity in which the medicine is placed be at its end, like the concavity of a spoon's radius.
And place the instrument with the medicine upon the uvula itself; and let the sick person lie on his side, so that saliva may run from his mouth from the internal medicine, so that nothing descends from it to his throat and harms him.
Then hold your hand with the medicine. And you should hold it over the uvula for about half an hour, until you see it blackened and the biting of the medicine calmed.
And if you wish to take a cottons swab, and wrap it around the tip of the radius, and infuse the cottons swab with the medicine, and insert the radius with the cottons swab into the cannula from above, until the cotton adheres to the uvula.
Do this many times, until you achieve what you want from the cauterization of the uvula. Then leave it; for it dries and falls off after three or four days.
If you need to repeat the medicine, repeat it: and after the cauterization, wipe around the uvula with a cotton swab soaked in butter, and cleanse whatever is around it of the medicine; and rinse it with cold water.
And be treated externally with embrocations, and internally with gargles, until it is healed.
This method of treatment is therefore healthier and further from the danger of incision.
And sometimes the uvula is treated with something finer than cauterization and incision; for it is treated with fumigation, according to this method. Take calamus, hyssop, oregano, rue, sseah, chamomile, and rue, and similar herbs: and gather them all, or some of them, in a pot, and soak them with vinegar, and boil them; and let the pot be well-baked with mud; and let there be a hole in the middle of the pot, upon which a concave instrument shall be placed according to this manner.
Let it be made of silver or bronze; and let the end, on which is the pomegranate, be introduced into the sick person's mouth, until the vapor ascends to the uvula over the cannula, until the uvula is greatly darkened.
Then repeat this many times over it, until it dries.
And beware, lest you perform this treatment at the beginning of the abscess's arrival. For it usually adds to the abscess; and it is not appropriate for you to do it except at the decline of its hot abscess.
If however this instrument is not present for you, then take a tube, and place a piece of egg shell on its end, so that the sick person's mouth is not burned; because the egg shell prevents the vapor from burning the mouth; and this is from good treatment with health.
Concerning the extraction of thorns and bones from the throat.
Chapter Thirty-Eight.
Often a bone, or a fishbone, or something else adheres in the throat.
Therefore, it is necessary to extract that which is apparent to the sight, after pressing the tongue with an instrument in the sun, and it is made clear to you what is in the throat.
And that which does not appear to you and is hidden in the throat, it is necessary to make the vomit weak before the food is digested in the stomach. For perhaps the attached thing will come out through vomiting.
Or let the weak person swallow a piece of turnip, or a root of lettuce, or swallow a piece of dry bread; or let him take a piece of soft dry sea sponge: and tie it to a thread, then let him swallow it. When therefore it reaches the place where there are spines, or bone, and is extracted, then it comes out.
If therefore it does not come out with what we have said, then use an instrument of lead according to this form.
Let it be thicker than a radius a little, and at its extremity let there be a curve, which he should introduce into his throat with ease, and let him have his head raised upward.
And let him beware of touching his epiglottis, so that coughing does not befall him, and let him expel with it the bone, or spine, or let the physician introduce it with his hand.
But that the patient introduce it is better, on account of his knowledge of the place of the attached thing; and let him push it downwards, or pull his hand with the instrument upwards, all of it according to the quantity which is prepared for him, until it comes out.
Concerning the extraction of a leech.
Chapter Thirty-Nine.
When a leech is medicated with what I have said in the division of treatment with medicines, and it does not help, then consider in the throat of the sick person before the sun, or in the sun after you have pressed his tongue with the instrument that I have told you.
If therefore your sight falls upon the leech, extract it with a small hook, or with fine tweezers properly.
If it is not possible with them, then take a concave cannula, and insert it into the throat of the sick person until near the throat; then insert into the concavity of the cannula a heated iron with fire. Do that many times, and let the sick person abstain from water all day.
Then let him take a bowl full of cold water, and open his mouth in it; and wash his mouth with it, and not swallow anything from it; and move the water one hour after another with his hand. For the leech immediately, when it feels the water, falls.
However, if it does not come out with what we have recounted, then fumigate the sick person with leeches, or with asa with the instrument that I have described in the fumigation of the uvula.
Do that many times, because it will fall itself.
And the method of operation in fumigation is that you take a pot in which there are live prunæ with fire; and the pot is covered with a cover, in the middle of which is a hole; and the end of the instrument is placed in that hole.
Then project the fumigation, and let the sick person place his mouth at the end of the cannula, and close his mouth so that the fumigation does not escape, until you know that the fumigation has already reached the leech. For the leech will fall immediately.
If it does not fall, then repeat the fumigation many times.
And let the sick person endure thirst, and eat salty things, and garlic, and not drink water.
Therefore, it is necessary that it depart with this regimen.
And this is the form of the instrument with which the leech is extracted from the throat, when it falls upon it, visible.
It is similar to forceps, as you see: except that it has this curvature which is inserted into the throat. And its extremity is similar to the mouth of a bird called a stork, in which there is the roughness of a file: when it grasps something, it does not release it at all.
Concerning the opening of abscesses, and their incision, and their general care.
Chapter XL.
Abscesses have many species, of many kinds, according to the recollection of them, that is, of one, and of one in this book; and they differ in their perforation and incision in two ways.
One of which is from a species of abscess in itself, and because it is comprised of humors.
And the second mode is because of the locations in which it occurs in the body.
Because an abscess occurring in the head is different from an abscess occurring in the anus; and an abscess occurring in a fleshy location is different from an abscess occurring in the joints; and each of them has its own judgment from the operation.
And of abscesses, some should not be perforated unless after the maturation of the pus in them, and its completion; and some of them should be perforated while they are raw and do not mature to completion.
Such as abscesses that are near the joints.
Because when an abscess occurs near a joint, and its duration is prolonged until what is around it rots, perhaps the ligaments or nerves of that joint are corrupted: therefore, it is a more proximate cause of contraction of that limb.
Or it is an abscess near the main limb: because if you delay its perforation until it matures, it will harm either it or the main limb through it.
Or it is near the anus, therefore pierce it immaturely; and it is not fitting to perforate a raw abscess, not fully digested, except that which is near the anus, so that it does not rot deeply, and extend to the interior of the anus, and become a fistula, or become incurable at its end.
You should know the time of perforation of an abscess which is mature according to the fullness: and it is by the cessation of pain, recession of fever, and diminution of redness, and pulsation, and acuteness of the abscess's head, and the other signs.
And it is necessary to make the same perforation in the lower place of the abscess, and of greater prominence; and let the perforation extend in the length of the body, if the abscesses are in the parts of the hands and feet, and places of tendons, and cords, and nerves, and arteries, and finally in all equal places where there is no reflection.
In those, however, which are reflected, proceed with the perforation according to that limb.
However, when the abscess is in fleshy places, then it is better to leave its perforation until its digestion is confirmed, as we said according to completion.
Because if you perforate it before that, the course of the virus from it is prolonged, and there is much filth and nastiness, and perhaps its lips will harden, and its depth.
And some abscesses are perforated according to the width of the body, where necessary, or according to what the limb requires.
And it is necessary that you administer a small perforation in small abscesses, and a large perforation in large abscesses, or multiple incisions according to the size of the abscess.
And there are abscesses in which the skin must be hollowed out, and incised when it has already become like a rag, and is at its end, so that it is already dead; like what happens in many, such as buboes, and carbuncles.
And some of them are incised with a three-angled incision. And some of them are incised like the shape of a myrtle leaf.
Like a groin abscess.
And among them are some in which a round incision and a crescent-shaped incision are given, and similar incisions to them.
But that which does not have a head, such as mild superficial abscesses, should be pierced with a superficial or simply flat perforation.
And when the abscess is large, and has already collected much pus, and you pierce it, you should not hurry and remove all the pus at that time; rather, remove a part of it. Then, cover the abscess until another day. Then remove some more of the pus.
Do this repeatedly in stages, until it exits; especially if the patient is weak, or a pregnant woman, or a young child, or an aged invalid, for the animal spirit is often resolved by the expulsion of the pus, and perhaps the patient dies, and you do not notice.
Therefore, observe this chapter with great caution.
And after your perforation, with which you perforate these abscesses, it is necessary to cleanse the wound and consider it. If therefore the wound of the abscess is small, or is a flat incision, then apply bandages of linen or old cloth. And if the abscess is large, and the perforations are many, then it is necessary to insert a bandage into every fissure until they meet.
And if in the abscess you have cut away part of the skin, or hollowed it out in the surrounding area: then it is necessary to fill it with old cloth, or as when a cloth is woven, or the fluff of linen is shaved off without moisture: and bind it until the third day.
Then remove it, and treat it with whatever ointment is appropriate, until it is healed.
If, however, bleeding occurs during your operation, then apply cold water and vinegar, after soaking a cloth in them: and place it repeatedly over the bleeding site.
If, however, the bleeding persists, then it is necessary to administer the regimen, and treatment, and powders, which I have described in many places in this book of ours, and in detail.
If, however, it is wintertime, and the site of the abscess is close to many nerves: then it is necessary to soak pledgets with warm wine and oil, and place them over the area.
And if it is summer, and the areas are fleshy: then it is necessary to place pledgets soaked in water and oil, or in wine and oil, and altogether cold. Until when it will be the third day, as we have said, then it is necessary to open the abscess and cleanse it, and apply the appropriate treatment until it is healed.
This, therefore, is what is necessary from the knowledge of treating abscesses universally.
Following the path of distinction, however, I have already spoken of every abscess, how there is a way to cure it in a chapter, individually.
Chapter XLI.
Concerning the incision of abscesses that occur in the scalp.
Small abscesses occur in the scalp, and they are of the kind of nodules, which are contained by coverings that are like sacs; as if they were the eggs of a hen.
And their varieties are many.
Some are fatty; and some contain moisture like mud; and some contain moisture like fritters and gruel; and similar to these, and some are hard, stony ones.
And in the incision of all of them, and their extraction, there is no fear, as long as their incision does not oppose an artery.
And the procedure in their incision is to probe them with an instrument, whose form will come after this, which is called an Intromissum, until you know what they contain.
For if it contains moisture, then cut it with a superficial longitudinal incision, according to this form.
And begin with an incision from line b to line g. When the moisture is evacuated, then scrape the cyst that contains it, and do not leave any of it at all.
For it often returns if anything remains of it.
Then immerse the wound in Egyptian ointment, if present; and if not, in water and salt; and fill the wound with it, and leave it until another day. For it will corrode what remained of the cyst.
Then repeat over the wound with Egyptian ointment a second, and a third time, and if you need it, until you are sure that nothing remains of the cyst; for then treat the wound with the ointment until it heals.
If the abscess contains a fatty tumor, then cut it in this shape, the incision having the form of a cross; and insert hooks between the two sides of the incision, and scrape it from every side; and exert your skill in extracting the tunic that contains it.
If however the artery resists you, then do what I will tell you later.
And likewise do in an abscess, if it is stony, by the same incision and treatment, according to what we have said.
And the incision over a stony abscess is easier, because it has little blood and moisture. And sometimes there are some of these abscesses which are in the head of certain people, in which there is no moisture at all.
That is: because I incised an abscess in the head of an old woman, and found the abscess like a solid and hard stone, rough, white, which could not be broken; and if anyone struck it with something while throwing it, it would injure him.
In other abscesses, however, occurring on the head, apart from these abscesses which occur on the heads of children, and at the roots of the ears, the incision of all of them should be a superficial incision.
Or make a perforation of them always from their lower part, so that the course of pus is relieved downwards.
Then treat it with what is suitable for them from the treatment.
Concerning the incision of tumors on the neck.
Chapter XLII.
Often these abscesses occur on the neck, and under the armpits, and in the groins; and sometimes they occur in the rest of the body.
And those which arise from them on the neck, are single, and multiple; and they are generated from one another; and every tumor among them is within its own proper membrane, just as is the case with the knots and abscesses of the head, as we have described.
And the types of these tumors are numerous. And some of them are stony; and some of them contain moistures; and some of them are malignant, which do not obey treatment.
Therefore, in that one among them which you see to be well-disposed to the touch, whose proximity to the skin color is evident, and which moves in every direction, and is not connected with the neck nerves, nor with the veins, nor with the artery, nor is deep: you should separate it by a superficial incision from the upper part to the lower part of the body along a line, b, to a line, g, and scrape it from every side; and spread the two lips of skin with a hook, or with many hooks, if you need it, as we said in the case of head abscesses, and extract it gradually.
And it is a precaution not to cut a vein or a nerve.
And let the scalpel be not very sharp, so that it does not harm your hand with the incision, or disturb the sick person, and you cut what is not necessary.
However, if you cut a vein or an artery, or it prevents you from the operation, then place in the wound ground zegi, or certain powders which cut off blood, and bind the wound, and leave it until the sharpness of the abscess subsides, and the wound softens; or it begins to putrefy.
For the blood is then cut off.  Therefore, return to your operation, until you complete it.
Then investigate with your index finger if any of the other small scabs remain; therefore, cut them off, and clean them.
If there is a large vein at the base of the scab, then it is necessary not to cut that scab from its base; rather, it is necessary to tie it with a strong double thread, and leave it until it falls off by itself without harm.
Then fill the wound with old lint, which you have already soaked in Egyptian ointment. Then care for it.
If the scab is cut entirely, then it is necessary to bring together the two wound edges and close it immediately, after you see that there is no excess remaining.
However, if you see that excess remains due to the size of the scab, then it is necessary to attempt to cut its root and pull it out, and administer the suture we mentioned.
And whatever humours the scabs contain, pierce it with a superficial puncture, so that the place of its maturity is apparent to you, and place the puncture from that which follows below the body, as we said.
Then, after the puncture, apply bandages with ointment, and similar things, to corrode what remains of the corruption, until when you see the wound already cleansed: then treat it with a regenerative flesh ointment until it heals.
Chapter XLIII.
Concerning the section of the epiglottis from an abscess occurring within the throat.
The ancients said this section in the epiglottis, and I have not seen anyone in our land who has done it, and this is the writing of their words.
However, in those with synocha, the section of the epiglottis should be avoided, as it does not provide benefit due to the weakness of all the veins and lungs.
However, in those who have a hot abscess in the mouth, or throat, or the two tonsils, when there is no illness in the canal, the section of the epiglottis should be used to avoid the harm that comes from suffocation.
Therefore, when we wish to do it, we should cut the epiglottis beneath three or four circles of the epiglottis, with a small cut across the width, in the space between two circles, so the cut is on the sifac, not on the cartilage.  This place is suitable for the incision because it is devoid of flesh, and blood vessels are distant from it.
If the physician is timid, then it is necessary to extend the skin of the throat with a hook. Then he should split the skin until he reaches the canal, avoiding any blood vessels if he sees any.
Then he should cut the sifac that we have described, and the incision of the canal is shown by the phlegm that flows out of it when it is ruptured, and the loss of voice.
And leave the wound open for a time.
When therefore the time of feared suffocation is past, bring together the two lips of the wound of the skin, and stitch them together alone, without cartilage.
Then administer medicines that cause flesh to grow, until it is healed.
Says Albucasis. The summary of this account we have given is not a description, unless when they see that some of these abscesses have already blocked the patient's throat; and the patient is raised above death, and his breath wishes to be severed; they go to cut the epiglottis, so that the patient may breathe over the place of the wound with some respiration, and be saved from death: and for this reason they have prescribed to leave the wound open until the malice of the sickness is consumed, and its malice is for three days, and as much itself; then they prescribed to stitch the wound, and to treat it, until it is healed.
And that which I saw through myself is that a certain maidservant took a knife and inserted it into her throat, and with it cut a part of the lung canal.
And I was called to treat her, and I found her moaning, like the moan of a dead person.
Therefore, I examined the wound, and found blood that had flowed from the small wound. Therefore, I concluded that she had not cut a vein, nor a tendon; and wind was emerging from the wound.
I hastened, therefore, and treated the wound, until it was healed; and nothing happened to the maidservant except a hoarseness in her voice; and she returned after a few days to her better state of health.
Therefore, I say that there is no fear in the section of the epiglottis.
Concerning the incision of a throat hernia.
Chapter XLIV.
This abscess, called a throat hernia, is a large abscess, according to the color of the body; and it is very common in women.
And it is according to two modes: either it is natural or it is accidental.
In the natural one, certainly, there is no ingenuity.
The accidental one, however, is according to two modes: of which one is similar to fatty nodes. And the other mode is similar to an abscess that forms from the knotting of an artery. And in it there is fear, and it is not fitting that presumption be made to it with iron, unless it is small; and you should probe it, and investigate it with an instrument. If therefore you find it similar to a fatty node, and it is not attached to any vein, then cut it, as nodes are cut, and extract it with what it contains from the cyst, if it is in a cyst; and if not, then explore its entire extent. Then treat the place with what is appropriate for healing, until it is healed.
Chapter XLV.
Concerning the incision over the types of Nodes.
There are many types of nodes, and I have already stated all their types in the division; and it is necessary to explain here the difference between a node and an issue, since it causes doubt.
Therefore, when an issue is present, there is heat, fever, and intense pain until the ebullition of the superfluity subsides and putrefaction is complete; then fever and heat cease.
However, with a node, there is no heat, no fever, no pain, and it contains those small cysts, which are their own sac, and is according to the color of the body; and their beginning is like a chickpea, and it becomes like a batecha, and larger and smaller.
And they are of two types: either fatty or containing moisture. And the colors of the moisture are many, according to what I have said in the division.
Therefore, when you reach the treatment of a node, you should examine it and inquire into its form first with an instrument called an Intromissum, as its form will be described in the chapter after this one.
And the method of investigating abscesses and all nodes is that you should take this instrument and insert it into the moistest place that you find in the abscess; and you should slowly rotate your fingers with it, until you know that the instrument has already penetrated the skin. Then place your hand, according to the size of the abscess.
Then withdraw the Intromissum and consider what comes out in its wake.
If, therefore, running moisture of any color comes out, then cut it with a superficial incision, as I have said in other abscesses.
And if no moisture comes out in the wake of the Intromissum, then know that it is fatty; therefore, cut above it with a cross-shaped incision, according to this form, as I have taught you; and suspend it with hooks, and carefully scrape the skin from all sides; and if possible, keep a kist on it, so that you can extract the healthy part with the Node.
If, however, the kist is broken during the operation, and it is not possible to extract the healthy part—for this often happens—then extract it piece by piece until nothing remains of it.
For if a little or a lot remains of it, the Node returns most often.
If, however, it overcomes you and a little remains, then fill the wound at its completion with certain sharp corrosive powders; and constrict the wound, and place on it whatever is fitting for a hot abscess, and treat it with the rest of the treatment until it is healed.
If therefore the Node is large, then sew its two lips together, and treat it with that which causes it to become flesh.
If, however, there is a pulsating or non-pulsating vein in opposition, then hurry and fill the place with crushed zegi, and leave it constricted for two or three days until the wound putrefies and the boiling of blood subsides; then return to cutting away what remains of the Node.
Chapter XLVI.
Concerning instruments for incision and perforation.
Among them are the forms of instruments, and there are three kinds.
For among them there is a large one, and among them there is a medium one, and among them there is a small one.
Let them be made of iron, with square ends, well-formed, so that they quickly enter the abscess.
And these are the forms of instruments, and no, that is, perforating ones: and one of them is called a *beris*, that is, a piercer: and they are of three kinds.
For among them there is a large one, and a medium one, and a small one.
They are suitable for investigating abscesses, issues, fistulas, and buboes, as well as that which is between the bones in them.
And others are made round, smooth, and gentle, like large needles with which saddles are sewn, made of amber, or bronze, or iron, or silver.
And the best of them are those made of amber.
And experience is also made of black lead, which are suitable for testing fistulas, whose concavity has reflections, so that they may be reflected with their gentleness along those reflections.
And they are again of three kinds. Because from them are long, from them middle, and from them short, according to the quantity of the fistula's width and its stricture.
The shape of Hooks, and they are of many kinds.
Because from them are simple, namely those that have one curvature: and they are of three kinds as you see, Large, Medium, and Small.
And from them are blind hooks: and they are of three kinds.
And from them are hooks having two curvatures, and they are of three kinds: and from them are hooks having three curvatures, and they are of three kinds: and all these are necessary in their place.
And these are the forms of scalpels, with which knots and abscesses are cut and scraped: and they are of three kinds: because from them is large, medium, and small.
Their cutting edges are sharp, the other edges are not sharp: and they are not placed like this, unless so that with them help is given in scraping knots, for fear of cutting a vein or nerve: and so that the sick person may be healed by them, and may find some peace from the burning that he finds in scraping an abscess.
They are made of air, and are similar to a Ray, with which alcohol is made: and on the broad end a hidden Spatula point is present: and on it runs inwards and outwards when you wish, as you see.
The shapes of spatulas that are hidden between the fingers during the perforation of abscesses: and the sick do not perceive them: and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
The forms of cupping glasses with which the flow of blood is cut off: and they are of three kinds, large, medium, and small.
They are made of air, or of amber, round, slightly elongated, as you see: and let them be to a fine degree.
And it is necessary that these kinds of cupping glasses be present, large and small, so that you can cut off blood with speed when necessary, and then when there is no medicine available to you.
However, do not use them for the cutting of blood in every part of the body, and do not use them except on fleshy areas: such as the calf of the leg, and the thigh, and the calf of the arm, and the nipples, and the belly, and the groin, and similar fleshy, moist parts.
And sometimes they are made from them other small instruments similar to hazelnuts, according to their shape: with which blood is cut off when it flows from a phlebotomy site, or at the incision of a vein, or artery: and sometimes they are made round according to this form.
Chapter XLVII.
Concerning the care of the nipples of men that resemble the nipples of women.
Sometimes the nipples of certain men swell at the completion of sleep (that is, when they begin to be polluted in sleep) until they resemble the nipples of women: and they remain festering and unsightly.
Therefore, whoever abhors that, must cut above the nipples with a crescent-shaped incision, according to this form, from line b to line g. Then scrape away all the fat. Afterwards, fill the wound with a flesh-forming medicine. Then bring together the two edges of the wound with sutures, and care for it until it heals.
However, if the nipple descends and softens because of its size, as happens in women, then it must be cut open on its two upper sides with two crescent-shaped incisions, each one joined to the other at the ends of both, until there is a larger line encompassing a smaller one, according to this form, from line b to line g.  Then scrape the skin that is between the two edges, and remove the fat, and apply what we said about sutures and powders, what is necessary for treatment, until it heals.
However, if you do not complete the incision that you ought to make, because of the patient's discomfort or due to blood flow, then you must fill the wound with a cloth soaked in a corrosive ointment, and leave it until it corrodes the remaining fat. Then treat it until it heals.
Concerning the perforation of an abscess occurring under the nipple.
Chapter XLVIII.
These abscesses that occur under the nipple are of the hard Strophulus type, containing cysts, and some contain moisture.
Therefore, those containing moisture should be incised with a crescent-shaped cut, following the form from line b to line g.  When all the pus in the abscess is evacuated, then fill it with old lint and leave it until the following day.
Then the lint should be removed, and treated with a suitable ointment until it heals.
If the abscess is of the knotted Strophulus type, then cut over it, as we stated in the section on Strophulus, equally.
If the wound extends to emit pus, then apply a cautery to it, as we have stated in what has gone before.
Chapter XLIX.
Of the incision of an abscess occurring due to artery and vein.
When an artery bursts and the skin over it heals, then often an abscess occurs from this: and similarly it happens to a vein, so that an abscess and swelling occur in it.
And the signs by which it is known that the abscess and swelling are due to artery and vein are: because, when the abscess is due to an artery, it is long, gathered deep in the body: and when you press the abscess with your fingers, you feel as if there is a creaking sound.
And that which is due to the vein is round on the surface of the body.
And a section upon this abscess is an error, and especially that which is under the armpits, and the groin, and the neck, and in many places of the body: and it is very large. Therefore, its treatment with iron should be avoided, and that which is also in the extremities, or in the head, should be avoided.
Therefore, for that which is from them from the swelling of the artery's opening, make an incision in the skin lengthwise. Then open the incision with hooks, afterward the Artery: and free it from its sheaths, until it is exposed.
Then insert a needle beneath it, and make it penetrate to the other side, and tie the Artery with a double thread in two places, according to what I taught you in the extraction of the two Arteries that are in the temples.
Then puncture the place that is between the two ligatures with a lancet, until all the blood within it is discharged, and the abscess is resolved.
Then apply a treatment that produces pus until the ligatures fall off; afterward treat it with suitable ointments until it heals.
If it be an abscess due to the incision of a Vein, then you should hold with your hand what you can of the abscess with the skin. Then introduce a needle beneath the place which you held with your hand, and make it penetrate; and let there be a double thread in it, until you extract it from the other side. Then tie the abscess with a good ligature, according to what I told you in the ligature of a grape, with two ends of the thread at two sides.
If however you fear that the thread will be pulled out, then introduce another needle with another thread beneath the entire abscess at the incision of the needle; press, and tie your threads in four places.
Then cut the abscess in its middle, until that which is within it comes out; then cut the superfluous skin, and leave that which is tied from it.
Then place upon it the poultice which you have soaked in wine and oil. Then administer treatment, which is done with bandages and ointments, until it is healed.
Chapter L.
Concerning the abscess that arises from the twisting of nerves.
Just as an abscess occurs in arteries and veins, so too does an abscess occur in nerves: which occurs in them through a blow, or occurs from excessive labor, and similar things; and it happens most frequently in the places of the helpers, or the heel, and in every place where the joints move; and it is a serious abscess.
Its color is similar to the rest of the body, and it is in many dispositions without pain; and when it is squeezed with force, the weak person feels a similar numbness in it.
And it is not a deep-seated, aggregated abscess, but rather under the skin, and it moves to every part, and does not go forward, or in front, nor back to the rear.
Therefore, those that are in the joints should not be incised with iron. For perhaps a contraction will result.
However, in that which is in the head or forehead, slit the skin with a scalpel. If therefore it is a small abscess, then hold it with forceps, and cut it away from its root.
And if it is large, then suspend it with a hook, and scrape it away: then remove it, and stitch the wound: and care for it until it is healed.
Chapter LI.
Concerning the incision of an abscess occurring in the belly.
Some people frequently get, in their bellies and other parts of their bodies, growths called Fungalia, by reason of their similarity to fungi.
Their root is subtle, and their head is large, and their lips are turned inward. And some of them are small; and some of them become very large.
And indeed I have already seen a man who had two growths in his belly which were more similar to fungi than other things: there was no difference between them, white, with tiny roots, and their lips turned inward, and they split open, and moisture always flowed from them.
Therefore, I cut them, and found in the weight of one about ten and eight ounces, and in the other about six ounces.  And the operation in cutting them is, that you should consider then, if the diseased part is moist, and the color of the growth is white, moist, with a subtle root: then cut it with a broad spatula, and have cauteries ready in the fire.
For often much blood is expelled during its section; therefore, hurry if blood overcomes you, then cauterize it.
If you see the patient fearful and timid of the incision with iron, then take a thread of lead well-made, and tie the growth with it: using the method we have described, and leave it for two days.
Then add to the lead ligature. Therefore do not cease to tighten the lead, as often as it presses on the growth, until it is severed and falls away by itself without effort.
If however the growth is large in its root, then in its incision there is something of fear and dread; especially that which is in the belly.
However, it is possible to cut half of it. Then cauterize it, so that it does not return.
Then treat it until the wound is healed.
And beware lest you presume to cut a boil that is of a dark color, with few sensations, and foul appearance: for it is indeed a cancerous abscess; and I will come with a reminder of Cancer after this.
Chapter LII.
On the treatment of the navel eminence.
The navel eminence arises from many causes. Or from fissures of the skin that is upon the belly; and from it comes forth a part of the bowels or an intestine, according as happens in other ruptures. Or from blood which flows from a vein or from an artery, according to what has been said before. Or from a constriction of wind in it.
If however it is because of a fissure of the skin, and an issue of pus, then its color is similar to the color of the body, and it is soft, without pain, and appears in various locations. And if it is because of the emergence of an intestine, then its position, with the one that we have described, is of more varied location. When therefore you press it with your fingers, it disappears; then it returns, and perhaps there is a sound with it; and it greatly increases at the entrance of the bath, and with great exertion.
If however it is due to moisture, it is soft, then it does not disappear when you press it with your hand, nor does it contract, nor does it increase.
If however it is because of blood, then with these signs the abscess appears black from the bottom.
If however it is due to generated flesh: then the abscess is severe, large, hard, and fixed in a single quantity.
If however it is due to wind, then its touch is soft.
And the operation on it is, that you should then consider, if the prominence of the navel is because of blood of the artery, or vein, or wind: then you ought to refrain from its treatment. For in it is fear and terror, as I taught you in the chapter, in which I said abscesses because of the artery and vein.
If the prominence of the navel is because of the intestine, or the zirb: then it is necessary that you order the sick person to hold his breath and stand extended. Then mark with ink around the entire navel. Afterwards, order him to lie on his back between your hands. Then surround with a broad spatula the circumference of the navel over the place you marked with ink. Then extend the middle of the abscess upward with a large hook. Then tie him around strongly with thread, or with silk cord, a firm ligament: and let the knot of the ligament be tied.
Then open the middle of the abscess extended above the ligament. If therefore you find it already grasped by the ligament, then pull out the tied portion, and insert the index finger into it, and push the intestine towards the interior of the belly: and if you find a hernia, then take it with a hook, and cut away its excess.
If however it is an artery or vein, then surround it greatly. Then return to your operation. And take two needles, and insert in them two strong threads; and insert the two needles into the circumference you have made around the abscess in the manner of a cross, making them penetrate.
Then tie the abscess in four places above the needles; and if you wish, remove the needles, and leave the place until the stretched flesh rots: and falls off by itself; or cut it when it becomes thin and rotten.
Then treat it with whatever is necessary from the ointments, until it is healed.
If the protuberance of the navel is from flesh there or from moisture, then it is necessary to concavate the abscess, as we said. And draw out the blood or moisture that you find in it.
Then treat it with that which solidifies the place.
Chapter LIII.
Concerning the treatment of CANCER.
We have already spoken in the division of the species of Cancer, and how there is a way to treat it with medicines; and caution is to be observed in treating it with iron, lest it be ulcerated; and we have spoken of Cancer generated in the womb, and the precautions in its treatment.
And the ancients said that sometimes Cancer is in a place where its eradication is possible, such as Cancer in the breasts, or in the thigh, and similar parts, by removing the whole of it, especially when it is small and just beginning.
However, when it is old and large, one should not approach it.
For I myself could not cure anyone of it, nor have I seen anyone before me who has reached that point.
And the operation on it, when it is located as we have said, is to proceed and to release the sick person from the black cholera at times. Then phlebotomize it if you find it with clear fullness.
Then prepare the sick person with the preparation in which it will be placed with the operation.
Then cast into the Cancer the hooks which are suitable for it. Afterwards, hollow it out from every side with the skin, according to the examination, until nothing remains of its roots; and let the blood run, and do not sever it quickly, rather, press the places, and wipe away all the thick blood with your hand, or with what is possible there from the instruments.
If, however, a contrary effect is made to you by the flow of thick blood from the incision of an artery, or vein: then cauterize the vein, until the blood is severed. Then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment of Dropsy.
Chapter LIII. We have already described in the division of the species of Dropsy, and how the collection of water takes place; and the signs of every species, and its treatment with medicines.
And that which is cured with iron is only the species of ascites alone; and it is not applied with iron to the species of tympanites; nor to the species of hyposarca at all; for that is fatal.
When you have already treated this species of dropsy, tympanites, with medicines, and your treatment has not been successful, then consider. For if weakness has already resulted in the patient, or there is another illness with him besides dropsy, such as if there is a cough with him, or looseness of the bowels, and things similar to those; then beware that you do not treat him with iron, because there is a fear of it.
If however you see the patient of exuberant strength, and there is no illness with him besides dropsy alone; and he is not a child, nor an old man; then the method of operation in him is that you raise the patient standing between your hands, and a servant behind him will express his belly with his hand, pushing the water towards the lower part of the chest.
Then take a pointed, thorny spatula, according to this form. Let it be of two pointed parts, long, with sharp extremities like a spatula. However, let it have some degree of shortness gradually, so that you do not proceed with it in the operation all the way to the intestines and injure him.
Then consider. For if the generation of dropsy is from the intestines, then it is necessary to extend the incision from the navel by the quantity of three fingers, downward in a straight line above the pubic bone.
If, however, the generation of dropsy is due to disease of the liver, then your incision should be to the left of the navel by three fingers.
And if its generation is due to the spleen, then the incision should be on the right side; and it is also not necessary that the incision be on the side where the sick person wishes to lie, so that superfluities do not flow to that weak place.
Then perforate the entire skin with an instrument. Then introduce it into that incision, and raise your hand with a spatula between the skin, and if you do this, as if you are scraping it: and let the amount scraped be the quantity of a fingernail, or approximately that.
Then perforate in this way, until the spatula reaches the empty space: and it is the space of the water. Then extract it, and introduce into the hole an instrument of this shape. And it is similar to a cannula: it is made of silver, or bronze, or brass, smooth, polished; on its lower part is a small hole, and on its sides are three holes, two on one side and one on the other side; and its end is cut, according to the manner of cutting a reed, as you see; on its upper end is a ring.
For when the instrument reaches the water, then it descends immediately upon the instrument, therefore evacuate a moderate amount from the water in an hour.
Because if you evacuate from it more than is necessary, perhaps the sick person dies in an hour through the resolution of his animal spirit, or a faint occurs to him which approaches death.
But evacuate from it according to the amount of its virtue, and according to what the disposition of the sick person signifies to you from the strength of his pulse, and from his condition.
Then withdraw the instrument, and retain the water.
Because it is retained immediately due to the skin that holds the opening that is above the sifac: concerning which I have spoken, so that you pierce it according to that manner.
Then again use the instrument on the next day, if you see the sick person tolerating it; and also withdraw a small amount from the water.
Do this for several days without fear, and guard yourself from error, until there remains little of the water.
If, however, you fear for the sick person, and you remove much from the water: then treat him with burial in warm sand, and with much sweat in a bath and the sun, and let him endure thirst; and treat him with drying medicines, until he is healed; and cauterize above the stomach, and liver, and spleen, after the extraction of the water, according to what I have described previously.
Concerning the treatment of infants born in places where their urinary openings are not perforated.
Chapter LV.
When any infant emerges from its mother's womb, and the opening of its urethra is not perforated, or is a narrow opening, or is in the wrong place.
Therefore, it is necessary to assist with its perforation immediately when it is born, using a very thin spatula, according to this form.
Then place a thin leaden nail in the opening, and bind it: and hold it for three days, or four.
When, therefore, it wishes to emit urine, let it be removed, and let it urinate. Then return it.
And if you do not place the lead, it is possible that the urine that flows over the place will not allow it to close.
However, he whose opening is constricted is treated with lead, as we have said, for many days, until it is widened.
However, those in whom the opening is in an improper place are injured. That is because, in those who are born thus, the opening is at the end of the penis: wherefore they cannot urinate forward until they elevate the penis with their hand upward. Nor is there generation for him, therefore, because the semen cannot reach the womb in a straight line: and it is a very foul disease.
And the method of operation in him is that the sick person be laid on his back. Then extend his penis with your left hand by forceful extension, and cut the head of the penis from the place of the glans, with a sharp blade, or a sharp spatumil; as is the incision of a reed, or as if you were shaving something, so that its middle comes similar to the head of the penis; and so that the opening is in the middle as it ought to be.
And beware of blood flow during your operation. For that often happens. Therefore, apply to it what stops the blood, and treat the wound until it is healed.
Concerning Albucasis, what happens in the foreskin and head of the penis, and corruption, blackness, and adhesion.
Chapter LVI.
These things, according to Albucasis, happen frequently in the penis, and there is a prominence of foul flesh.
And from it there is another that is malignant, and another that is not malignant.
Therefore, the non-malignant one should be suspended with a fine hook and cut off, until the whole of it is cleansed. Then place upon it a compress soaked in Egyptian ointment. Then treat it afterward with the ointment Dyafinicon, until it heals.
If it is malignant, of foul color, then one should apply a cautery to it after its incision and scraping.
If the Albather is on the foreskin of an uncircumcised person, and part of it is within the foreskin and part outside, then one should remove the Albather that is inside first, until it consolidates, then treat the outside part; for if you treat both at the same time, the foreskin will not be safe from being perforated.
And similar blackening and corruption also occur in the testicles and foreskin. Therefore, one should cauterize all that is already blackened and that is about to corrupt, or that is already corrupted.
Then apply a poultice over it afterward with honey that has been mixed with the flour of ground pomegranates' bark and ground, sifted herbs.
Then treat it with the rest of the treatment until it heals.
If, however, bleeding occurs, then apply a cautery with a lunar cautery, according to this form.
For cautery is a remedy for two conditions at once, namely, for bleeding and for a wound when it becomes corrupted.
If the head of the penis is gnawed away and the whole thing is destroyed within an hour, then it is necessary to insert a lead cannula into the urethra so that the sick person urinates over it.
However, in the adherence of the foreskin to the head of the penis, and this adherence does not occur unless the foreskin is healthy, and circumcision is not required upon it; and sometimes adherence occurs because of a wound or an abscess, therefore it is necessary to scrape it with a blunt spatula until the ligament is loosened and the head of the penis is freed on all sides.
If it is difficult to discern them exquisitely, then it is necessary to scrape away something from the head of the penis and from the foreskin. This is because the foreskin, when it is thin, is perhaps perforated quickly because of its thinness.
Then separate the foreskin and the head of the penis with a thin linen cloth that has already been moistened in cold water, so that they do not adhere again.
Then treat it with astringent wine until it is solidified.
Chapter LVII.
Concerning the cleansing of infants and the treatment of errors that befall them.
Circumcision is not a solution of continuity, like other wounds; however, since it is performed by our operation, of our own will, and its use is proper to boys, it is necessary that we mark it with a better operation and a gentler path leading to health.
Therefore I say that the ancients did not speak of circumcision in any of their books, because they do not use it in their laws, and it is nothing but what we have acquired through experience.
This is because I have found most craftsmen and phlebotomists using cleansing, circumcising with a razor and with scissors; and the skilled use ligatures with thread and incisions with a nail.
And indeed I have already tested all these methods, and have not found a better cleansing than with scissors and a ligature with thread.
Because in cleansing with a razor, the skin often escapes. Because the foreskin has two layers; therefore, perhaps the upper skin is cut, and the lower layer remains; therefore, it is necessary to make an incision to cut the other, and future pain will result. And with cleansing done by means of alfecati, that is, digital, which the surgeons use, the incision of the male member's extremity is not secured; because perhaps it enters its opening.
In cleansing with a nail, perhaps the iron slips out and spoils your operation; and indeed the boy's skin is of short nature, and they are often born in such a way that they do not require cleansing; and I have already seen that.
However, cleansing with shears, and ligature with thread, proved a good test for me; because shears are proportional to the incision; because the sharpness that is above is like the sharpness that is below. Therefore, when you press your hand, the two sharpnesses are proportional, and they cut according to one operation at one time; and a similar figure of compression is made, enclosing the skin of the penis on all sides, with which no error falls at all.
And the proper method of operation is that you should cause the boy, especially if he is one of those who understand a little, to be aware that you are not doing anything except tying a thread on his penis; and leave him until another day.
Then cause him to rejoice and be joyful in every way possible to you, from that which he receives with his understanding. Then have him stand between your prepared hands, and let him not be seated; and conceal the shears in your sleeve, or beneath your foot, so that the boy sees them not at all, nor anything of the instruments.
Then insert your hand into his penis, and blow on the skin, and pull it upwards until the head of the penis emerges. Then cleanse it from the dirt that gathers on it.
Then tie the marked place with a double thread. Afterwards, tie a second ligature slightly below that. Then hold with your index finger and thumb the place of the lower ligature with good retention, and cut between the two ligatures. Then lift the skin quickly upward and pull out the head of the rod. Then let the blood run slowly (for this is better) and place powder on top; and on top of the powder a cloth on which is spread the yolk of a hard-boiled egg in rosewater, mixed with good fresh rose oil; and leave it on top until the following day; and treat it with the remaining treatment until healed.
The form of the scissors for cleansing is this.
Let the two ends be blunt, and let them be meeting; so that a nail's head does not emerge from them; and let the length of the two sharpened parts be the same as the length of the two handles, equally.
An error, however, occurring in cleansing is that perhaps the inner skin is entirely escaped, or a part of it near the incision.
Therefore, it is necessary that you extend it from your hour with your fingernail before the inflammation of the place: and cut it according to equality.
If you cannot hold it with your fingernail, then pull it out with a hook and cut it.
However, if three days have passed it, and what is under the rod remains swollen, suppurated: then leave it until the boil settles hot, and carefully remove its covering, and cut it as directed; and be cautious of the head of the rod. For that will not harm it.
Therefore, treat it with what consolidates wounds from the powders we have described in the treatise on powders: and if it is cut from the skin beyond measure, and extends upward, that will not harm it either.
Therefore treat it with what we said, until it is healed.
Chapter LVIII.
Of the extraction of retained urine in the bladder.
Retention of urine in the bladder occurs from an obstruction caused by a stone, or congealed blood, or pus, or newly formed flesh, and similar things to those which we have mentioned in the methods of treatment in the division.
And if the urine is not released, and you see that its retention is due to a stone which is now in the neck of the bladder, then it is necessary that he be weak, bent over on his knees. Then let his feet be placed on his back, and let him extend his abdomen from the upper part of his back, and let the weak man strain his breath as much as possible. Thus, it is forced with the neck of the bladder towards expulsion of the stone to the rear: wherefore the urine is released.
If, however, it is not released with what we have said, and the matter is severe upon the sick man, then it is necessary that you administer its extraction with an instrument, which is called a syringe, whose form is this: Let it be of silver, and let it be fine, smooth, concave like the quill of a bird's feather in thinness, and long in the likeness of one and a half palms; to which let a fine tube be on its head.
And the method of the urine's exit through it is, that you take a doubled thread, and tie to its end wool, or tow, a good ligature; and introduce the end of the thread into the lower part of the Syringe; and cut with scissors if any superfluity of wool remains, but introduce it into the tube with force, as if to raise it.
Then anoint the syringe with oil, or with butter, and the white of an egg; and make the sick man sit upon a seat, and embrocate the bladder, and its member with moist oils, and olive oil, and lukewarm water.
Then introduce the syringe into the member with ease, until it reaches the root of the member.
Then double it above, from the part of the navel.
Then force the syringe within it, until it reaches the Bladder, and let the sick person feel it has now reached the empty place: and this is not done according to this order, unless because the passage, in which it goes, is one in which there is winding.
Then extend the thread with wool gradually. For urine will follow the wool.
Then draw it out, and cause the urine to run through the urinary way: and again insert the syringe, and do not cease to do this, until the bladder is emptied, and the sick person finds relief.
Concerning the quality of bladder irrigation with a Clepsydra.
Chapter LIX.
When an ulcer occurs in the bladder, or blood clots in it, or pus collects in it, and force is used to pour water and medicines into it: then it should be done with the instrument called a Clepsydra, and this is its form.
It is indeed deaf, that is, closed. And in it are three openings, two on one side, and one on the other side, as you see.
And the hollow place, in which that which propels it is, should be according to the quantity required to propel it without addition, until when something is drawn with it, the humors are drawn out: and when it is propelled, it expels it to a distance, according to how the clepsydra projects naphtha, that is, Greek fire in naval battles.
Therefore, when the intention is to expel the humors in the bladder, introduce the end of the clepsydra into the moisture, and draw up with that which propels it to the upper part, for the moisture is drawn into the concavity of the clepsydra.
Then introduce its end into the tube, as we described in the syringe. Then propel the moisture with that which propels it, for that moisture reaches the bladder immediately, so that the sick person feels it.
A subtle form of clyster with which the bladder is clysterized.
Let it be made of silver, or brass: its head be higher, similar to a small alembic, and below it a sinus into which a ligament should fall.
Then take the bladder of a ram, and place in it the moisture with which you wish to clysterize the bladder. Then tie it between two sinuses with a strong ligature made with doubled thread: and heat that moisture a little while over the fire.
Then introduce the extremity of the clyster into the tube, afterward squeeze your hand over the bladder containing the moisture with a suitable constriction, until the weak person feels that the moisture has now reached the bladder.
If however you do not have a bladder present, then take a piece of parchment, and make a circle from it, and pierce it all around its circumference. Then introduce a strong thread into those holes, as they revolve. Then join the circle, as a bandage is joined: after you have placed on it that which you wish from the moistures, and oils, and waters.
Then tie it to the instrument: and do with it as you did with the bladder by pressing with your hand, until the moisture reaches the bladder.
Chapter LX.
On the extraction of the Stone.
We have already discussed the species of stone, and its treatment with medicines: and I have explained the difference between a stone generated in the kidneys, and the bladder, and the signs of each: and I have described the stone in which operation with the hand and incision is undertaken: and the stone generated in the bladder is proper: and that which is fixed in the urethra: and I am relating it with clear explanation and abbreviation.
I say, therefore, that a stone generated in the bladder happens most often to children.
And from its signs is that the urine issues from the bladder similar to water in its subtlety: and white sand appears in it, and the weak person rubs his urethra, and suffers with it, and often hangs down. Then it is erected, and the anus issues with it in many of them.
And the cure is easy for children, as long as they reach fourteen years. And it is difficult in the elderly. However, in young people it is intermediate, in that which is between those: and that whose stone is larger, the cure is easier; and if small, its cure is the opposite.
Therefore, when we come to the treatment, it is necessary first to cleanse the sick person with an enema, which will remove all the stool that is in their intestines; for it sometimes hinders the discovery of the stone during examination.
Then let the sick person be taken up by their feet and shaken and moved downwards, so that the stone descends to the bottom of the bladder, or jumps from a higher place sometimes.
Then have him sit between your hands, prepared, and let his hands be under his thighs, so that the entire bladder is inclined downwards.
Then examine him and touch him externally.
If therefore you feel a stone in the space, then quickly proceed with incision upon it.
If, however, it does not fall under your touch at all, then it is necessary that you wipe your index finger with the oil of your left hand, if the sick person is a child, or the middle finger if he is a full-grown youth, and insert it into his anus, and examine for the stone until it lies under your finger, and turn it gradually towards the neck of the bladder.
Then press upon it with your finger, and drive it outward against the place whose incision you wish, and order a servant to press the bladder with his hand, and order another servant to extend the testicles upward with his right hand, and with his other hand to remove the skin that is beneath the testicles on the side of the place where the incision is.
Then introduce the spatule, cutting, whose form is this. And cut into that which is between the anus and the testicles, and not in the middle, to the side of the left hip, and let the incision be upon the stone, and your finger be in the anus, and let the incision be transverse, so that the incision be wide externally and narrow internally, according to the quantity that the exit of the stone from it may not be greater.
For perhaps the finger, which is in the anus, compresses the stone near the incision, and it emerges without difficulty.
And know that the stone is one which has angles and edges, wherefore its exit is made difficult by that.
And of it is smooth, similar to a gland, and round, and its exit is made easy.
Therefore, to the one which has angles and edges, add in the fissure.
If it does not emerge thus, then it is necessary that you devise something above it, or that you tie over it with a fitting instrument, whose extremity is like the moon, which shall tie above the stone, and not escape from it; or that you introduce a slender, curved instrument underneath it.
If however you cannot above it, then enlarge the opening a little. If anything from the blood overcomes you, cut it away with a zegi.
If however the stone is more than one, then drive the large one first to the opening of the bladder. Then cut above it. Afterwards drive the small one after that: and likewise do if there are more than two.
If it is very large, then ignorance is to cut above it with a large incision. Because it happens to the sick person one of two things, either that he dies, or that a constant dripping of urine happens to him: because the place is not entirely consolidated.
But administer its expulsion, until it comes out; or make incisions in its fracture with forceps, until you extract it piece by piece.
When you have completed your operation, then fill the wound with frankincense, and aloe, and dragon's blood: and compress it, and place over it a cloth steeped in oil and wine, or rose oil, and cold water: as a hot abscess is seated.
Then lay the sick person on their back, and do not loosen the bandage, until the third day. When you have then loosened it, embrocate the place with water and much oil.
Then treat it with diachylon ointment, and basilicon ointment, until it is healed.
If however, a hot abscess, added to the wound, and corrosive, and similar to it should occur; such as if blood congeals in the bladder, and prevents urine from leaving; and this is known by the blood's discharge with the urine. Therefore, insert your finger into the wound, and extract that blood. For if it remains, it provokes the corruption of the bladder, and its putrefaction.
Then wash the wound with vinegar, water, and salt, and apply every kind of suitable remedy for healing, until it is healed.
And it is necessary during the entire period of treatment, that you tie the two hips together and compress, for the sake of the medicines that are placed over the area.
If the stone is small, and is in the urethra, and it is fixed there, and it prevents the exit of urine, then treat it with what I have described before you reach the stage of cutting. For many times you will be spared from cutting by this treatment, for I have already proven that.
And it is necessary that you take a trocar made of iron, of this form, triangular at the end, sharp, fixed to wood.
Then take a thread, and tie the urethra with it under the stone so it does not return to the bladder.
Then introduce the piercing iron into the urethra with ease, until the trocar reaches the stone itself; and revolve the trocar with your hand on the stone gradually; and try to pierce it, until you make it penetrate from the other side.
For the urine is discharged immediately.
Then press your hand upon that which remains of the stone from the outside of the urethra. For it breaks itself and emerges with the urine, and the sick person is healed.
However, if this treatment is not prepared for you, due to something that prevents it, then tie a thread below the stone, and another thread above the stone. Then cut above the stone in the urethra itself between the two ligatures. Then extract it. Afterwards, loosen the ligature, and cleanse the congealed blood that forms in the wound.
And a ligature of thread below the stone is not necessary, unless so that it does not return to the bladder; and another ligature above, unless so that when the thread is loosened after the stone's expulsion, the skin returns to its place and covers the wound; and for that reason it is necessary for you, when you tie the upper thread, to lift the skin upwards, so that it returns to its position, and covers the wound, as we said.
Chapter LXI.
Concerning the extraction of a stone in women.
Little stone is generated in women.
If however a stone happens to any of them, then the cure is difficult, and it is prohibited on account of many methods.
One of them is: that the woman is perhaps a virgin.
And the second is, because you do not find a woman who reveals herself to a physician, when she is chaste, or having husbands.
The third is, because you do not find a woman well knowing this art, especially the operation with the hand.
And the fourth is the distance from the location of the stone: wherefore it needs a deep incision, and there is fear in that.
If necessity provokes to that, then it is necessary that you accept a female physician, well-knowing: and little is found.
If however you are private with her, then seek a chaste, skillful physician, and present a knowledgeable midwife skilled in women's matters, or a woman who hints at a part of the art in this field: so make her present, and particularly to her, that she do all that you order her concerning the inquiry about the stone.
First of all, it is that she should look if the woman is a virgin: then it is necessary that she insert her finger into her anus, and seek for the stone.
If therefore she finds it, she should constrict it under her finger, and then order the midwife to insert her finger into the vagina of the sick woman, and inquire about the stone, after she places her left hand on the bladder, and compresses it with a good compression.
If therefore she finds the stone, then it is necessary that she gradually move it from the opening of the bladder downwards with all her skill, until she reaches it with the root of the thigh.
Then she should cut upon it at the opposition of the middle of the vulva at the root of the thigh, from whichever side it is prepared for her, and she feels the stone in that part: and her finger should not be removed from the stone, constricting it underneath: and the incision should be small at first. Then she should insert a rod over that incision.
Then she should insert a rod over that incision. If therefore she feels the stone, then she should add to the incision according to the proportionate amount that the stone emerges from it.
And know that the species of stone are many. For from these there is a small one, and a large one, smooth and rough, long, and round having branches.
Therefore know its species, so that this may signify to you concerning that which you wish.
If therefore blood overcomes you, then add to the place ground zegi, and hold it for one hour, until the blood is severed.
Then return to your operation, until the stone emerges: and do so, preparing with you from the instruments, which I said in the extraction of the stone in men, so that you may assist with them in your operation.
If, however, a flow of blood overcomes you, and you know that the expulsion of blood is from an artery which is cut: then place powder on the place, and bind it with bandages of fitting constriction; and do not alter it; and leave the stone, and do not extract it. Perhaps, indeed, the weak woman will die. Then treat the wound.
When therefore the sharpness of the blood subsides after days, and the place putrefies, then return to your operation, until the stone emerges.
Chapter LXII.
Concerning the incision of watery hernia.
A watery hernia is not, unless an aggregation of moisture in a white sifac, which is under the skin of the testicle containing the egg: and is called sifac.
And sometimes it is in its own membrane, which nature prepared for it in part of the egg, until it is rotten: which is another egg, and is the skin of the testicle between the white sifac, which we said: and that does not happen except rarely.
And indeed the generation of this hernia is from the weakness that occurs in the testicles: wherefore this matter is poured out upon them. And sometimes it happens from a blow upon the testicles.
And this moisture has many colours. For either its color is inclined towards a yellowness, or it is a blood-red, or it is a foul, black, or it is a watery white, and that is what is more.
And the signs by which it is known where the aggregation of water is, are these. For if it is in the white sifac that we mentioned, then the abscess is round to a slight length like the shape of an egg: and the testicle does not appear, because the moisture contains it from every side.
And if the moisture is in the proper tissue to itself, then the abscess is round with part of an egg, and because of this the man thinks that it is another egg.
And if the moisture is between the skin of the testicles and the white sifac, then it falls under sense.
Therefore, when you wish knowledge of the color of the moisture, then experience the color of the moisture in the abscess with the inserted square, whose form preceded. Therefore, according to that which emerges in the track of the Intromiss, judge of that which is between it.
When, therefore, we come to treatment with iron, then it is necessary that you command the sick person to be bled, if it is possible for him, and you see his body full.
Then let him be laid on his back upon a slightly raised thing, and place many cloths under him.
Then sit you upon his left side, and command the attendant to sit on his right side, who should extend his rod to one of the two sides of the skin of the testicles, and to the part of the navel of the belly.
Then take a wide spatula, and cut the skin of the testicles from the middle lengthwise, as far as near the pubic bone; and let the incision be according to the straightness of an equidistant line that divides the skin of the testicles, until it reaches the white sifac containing it; and scrape it and observe it, or beware of this, that you separate it; and let your scraping, with which you scrape it, be from the part that hangs with the egg more; and subtly examine it with the scraping according to the amount that is possible for you.
Then pierce the sifac full of water with a wide perforation, and extract all the water.
Then separate the two lips of the incision with hooks, and extend the sifac upwards, and do not touch the skin of the testicles containing it; and cut the sifac however it is possible for you to cut it, either with its totality, or in fragments, especially its thin side. For if you do not thoroughly examine it, there is no security in its incision, that the water will not return.
If, however, the egg proceeds outward from its skin during your operation, then when you have completed the incision of the sifac, return it. Then join the two lips of the scrotal skin with sutures.
Then treat it like the other wounds until it heals.
If you find a corrupted egg with another blackness, then it is necessary to tie the vessels, which are the suspensory ligaments, fearing the flow of blood.
Then cut off the testicle with the suspensory ligament, and remove the egg; afterwards treat it with what we have said.
If, however, water is collected in two parts, then know that there are two branches: therefore, cut the other part according to what you have done to the other equally.
And if it is equal to you, so that it is one operation, then do it; then wipe away the blood. And introduce into the sections wool immersed in oil, or in rose oil; and place outside another wool which you have immersed in wine and oil, and spread it over the two testicles, and the cavity of the belly; and place over it doubled cloths, and these are pillows; and tie them over it with a bandage, which is having six according to this form.
Then take two cloths, and sew them together, and fill them with dyed wool, according to this likeness; and let the edges with which it is pressed be made of cotton, or damp wool; and let them be joined with a constriction at the lumbar region of the breeches; and one of the edges should extend over the anus up to the back, and the second should extend to the side of the pubic area; and two edges over the two thighs below, and two other edges over the two groins, and the whole constriction should be joined at the lumbar region of the breeches.
And sometimes this branch is artificially treated with a cautery at the place of the incision with iron: and it is like taking a delicate knife cautery, and cutting with it the skin of the testicles; and it is heated, as we have described; until when a white substance containing water is revealed; then take the cautery, according to this form.
It is similar to the Greek letter.
Then pierce it with that, and let it be heated, until all the moisture comes out.
Then extend the sifac with hooks, and scrape it with a heated fine cautery, and cut it as best you can until you eradicate all of it.
And beware, lest the fire touch it.
And know that this operation is safer, and further from blood flow, and better than an operation with a section.
If, however, a contrary thing happens to you at the time of the section, or at the time of the cautery, a hot abscess, or a flow of blood, or anything else: then it is necessary that you treat whatever contrary thing happens to you from it with the treatment that suits it, as has been previously stated.
If, however, the patient is timid and does not expect this operation because of its horror, then perform the perforation as I describe it; and it is so.
When it is certified to you by what we have said about the signs that the limb is watery, then it is necessary that you have the patient sit upon a raised seat.
Then perforate the abscess in its lower part with a wide spatula; and place the perforation along the length of the body, and let the opening be wide until all the water runs out.
Then place a poultice on the wound, and bandage it, and let it run until the remaining water is drained; then treat it until the wound heals.
If, however, there is opposition to the escape of water at the time you perforate it, and this is only because a white membrane creates opposition in the wound opening, and prevents the water's exit, then it is necessary to insert a pen into the wound, or an instrument with which the water of the hydrops is drawn out, whose form has preceded, or add a little to the wound treatment.
And know that the water returns, and collects after six months, or about that time. When it collects again, then it is to be pierced according to the same method, and the patient will pass their days.
Chapter LXIII.
Concerning the treatment of fleshy tumors.
Know that in the section concerning this tumor, there is fear leading to harm in many dispositions. Therefore, it seems to me that its dismissal is to be saved from it.
And I am recalling the operation on it, and the appearance of the abscess.
I say, therefore, that many abscesses occur in the bodies from which the testicles are composed: and that happens from many causes, namely.
