Or from an acute or acrid superfluity, which is poured onto the testicles. Or from a blow.
And the color of the abscess is according to the color of the body, and no pain is found in it: and it is a serious abscess.
And perhaps it is a stony abscess, whose color is dark, and there is no feeling in it.
And sometimes it arises from the knotting of the arteries, and their inflation: as its previous mention has already shown: or from the inflation of the veins.
Therefore, what arises from them, from the inflation of the arteries, is recognized by the disposition of the abscess, when you press it with your fingers. And you should not touch it completely.
However, that which arises from the inflation of the veins does not disperse anything from it upon compression with the fingers.
And it is now possible that a section be made upon this type, and upon the other flesh: and it is as if you were to cut the skin of the testicles. Then, extend the egg upwards, and extract it with a white sifach: and free the suspensory from the vessels, and tie the vessels: and cut the suspensory after you have freed it from every part of the parts of the egg.
If therefore the egg is consolidated with those born flesh, then it is necessary that you pull the egg, and cut it.
And if there is an adhesion between something of the siphaket, or in that which is between the vessels: then it is necessary that you free all that adhesion, and cut it with a round incision.
If however there is a birth of flesh in the place of adhesion which is behind: then it is necessary that you cut all of it, and extract the egg, as we said.
When therefore your operation is completed, then fill the wound with wool infused in rose-oil and wine: then treat it with the rest of the treatment, until it is healed.
Chapter LXIII.
On the treatment of Ramicis, which is with the Vite.
Uitis is an abscess enclosed by some wrapping, similar to a bunch of grapes, with a softening of the testicles: and it makes difficult movement and exercise, and walking, for the infirm person.
And in the treatment of this illness there is fear, because of that which preceded it's recollection.
But it is necessary, that we say the operation on it according to what the first ones did.
Therefore I say that it is necessary to make the infirm person sit on an elevated seat. Then push the suspensory of the testicles downwards. Then grasp the skin of the testicles with your fingers, near the vessels which are near the penis; and let another servant hold them, and stretch them with a forceful extension.
Then, with a broad, sharp spatula, make a transverse incision directly over the vessels, until the vessels are exposed.
Then scrape from every side, as we told you in the extraction of the arteries which are in the temples.
Afterwards, insert a needle with doubled thread into them, and tie it at the first of the places where the veins occur; and tie it again at their last. Then cut it in the middle, straight along the length of the body, and extract what is gathered from putrid, foul humors.
Then treat the wound with the treatment of the other wounds, in which pus is generated, so that the vessels which nourish one testicle fall. For there is no evil through it.
If, however, it happens to all the vessels, then it is necessary that the testicles be extracted with the vessels, so that the egg is not deprived of nourishment because of the incision, and it does not waste away or dry up, and there is no benefit with them.
Chapter LXV.
Concerning the treatment of intestinal hernia.
The outcome of this hernia is from a section which happens in a sifach extended over the belly in the groin area from the mirach of the belly.
Why do the intestines descend from that rupture to one of the testicles? And this rupture is either from sifach, or from its extension.
And these two types occur from many causes: either from a blow, or a jump, or a cry, or the lifting of a heavy object, and similar things.
And a sign, when it is from the extension of sifach. It is that which happens gradually over a long time, and does not happen suddenly; and it is an abscess equal in depth; because the sifach compresses the intestine.
And a sign of it when it is from a section of sifach, because it causes a sudden intense pain from the beginning; and it is a different abscess, appearing under the skin nearby; and that is because of the exit of the intestine, and its course to the exterior of the sifach.
And sometimes it exits with the intestine in a coil; and this is called a 'intestinal Ramex', and 'zirbia'; and sometimes it occurs with wind.
And sometimes the faeces runs into the intestine and is retained there; and with it is the harm of the sick person, because it causes mild pain, and especially when it is pressed.
And in the treatment of these types of illnesses there is fear with iron; therefore, care must be taken in that case.
And the method of operation is that you should instruct the sick person to reduce the intestine with their hand to the interior concavity of their body, if it yields to the reduction. Then let him be placed supine on his back between your hands; and raise his legs.
Then extend the skin that follows the skin upwards, and cut the skin of the testicles entirely along its length. Then fix hooks in the two lips of the incision, according to your need for opening the incision with them; and let the incision be according to the amount, so that it is possible for the egg to exit from it.
Then scrape the siphak, and what is under the skin of the testicles, until when the white, hard siphak is revealed on all sides; then insert your index finger into that which follows the testicle, into that which is between the white siphak, which is under the skin of the egg, and the second siphak, and separate with it the adhesion that is on the posterior part of the egg.
Then move the skin of the testicles inwards with your right hand; and with this, extend the white siphak upwards with your left hand; and lift the egg with the siphak towards the incision; and command the servant to extend the egg upwards; and you separate the adhesion, which is behind, upon complete separation.
And search with your fingers, so that there is nothing there of the intestine wrapped in the hard white siphak. If however you find something of it: then push it towards the lower belly.
Then take a needle, in which there is a thick thread, made of linen from ten threads; and insert it at the last siphak, that is, the tunic, which is under the skin of the testicles which follows the incision; then cut the ends which project from the thread, until there are four threads. Then arrange some of them over some others with a figure that is in the form of a cross; and tie with them the alsifach, of which we spoke, because it is under the skin of the testicles with a strong ligament, until no vessel, which nourishes them, can send anything to them; so that a hot abscess does not occur from it. And place again a ligament second, distant from the first ligament by less than two fingers. And after these two ligaments, leave from the sifach, which is under the skin of the testicles, a quantity the size of a finger; and cut the rest entirely along the circumference, and remove the egg from it.
Then cut in the lower part of the skin of the testicles the incision, from which blood and pus runs, as we have narrated in what has been said before.
Then apply wool soaked in oil, and place it in the wound; and apply the ligament that we have narrated. And sometimes the white sifach that we mentioned is cauterized, after its incision, fearing a flow of blood. For this often happens. And leave the ligament until it falls off by itself.
If however its falling is delayed, then it should be soaked with hot water; and place upon it that which we have mentioned, until it falls off.
Then treat the wound with the rest of the treatment for wounds, until it is healed.
Chapter LXVI.
Concerning the windy ram.
In this ram, I have not seen anyone who dared to use iron for its treatment.
And indeed the first ones said that it happens as we have described in the ram that is with the vine. That is, tie the vessels after the incision, apply pressure on them from below with ease; then cut in the middle, and finally treat with what opens it, until the vessels fall away; and treat the wound, as we said, until it heals.
Chapter LXVII.
Of the rupture that occurs in the groin.
When a rupture occurs in the groin, as we have said, and the area protrudes, and nothing from the intestines descends to the testicles: and if it does descend, it is little, and returns every hour; but if the condition continues with that time, a fissure is added in the scrotum, until the intestines descend into the scrotum.
And that happens from the stretching of the scrotum, which is in the groin, as we said. That is, because the scrotum is stretched; then the area softens, and an abscess forms, and it protrudes.
And its treatment is with a cautery, as its previous description stated.
And sometimes it is treated with an iron, according to this method: and it is that the sick person lies on their back between your hands. Then cut the location of the abscessed and protruding rupture, cutting across its width, according to the size of three fingers.
Then press the scrotum and what is beneath the skin until when the white scrotum is exposed, which is beneath the skin that follows it.
Then take a rod, and place it over the protruding location of the scrotum, and press it to the depth of the belly.
Then sew the two protruding locations above the end of the rod from the scrotum, and connect one of them with the other with sutures: then pull out the end of the rod.
And do not cut the scrotum at all, nor stretch the egg, nor anything else, just as I have made you know in the treatment of intestinal rami.
Then treat it with the treatment of wounds. When therefore you cut the threads, cleanse it, and restore the wound, until it is consolidated.
For the scrotum contracts, and no protrusion of the rupture is added.
And a cautery is better in this place, because it is closer to health.
Chapter LXVIII.
Concerning the softening of the skin of the testicles.
Often the skin of the testicles in some men softens, because of which the flesh between them softens, and its appearance is disfigured.
Therefore, it is necessary for whoever is asked to treat it, to have the sick man lie on his back, and to cut away all the skin that has softened, over the natural skin.
Then, he should bring together the two folds with sutures: and if you wish, first suture the excess of softened skin with three or four sutures, and make the suture firm.
Then cut between the sutures.
Then treat the remaining wounds until they heal and the threads fall out.
Chapter LXIX.
On Castration.
Know that castration is forbidden in our law; and because of that, I would be permitted to not mention it in this book of mine.  Its mention is not necessary except for two methods.
One of which is that it should be included in the physician's knowledge when questioned about it, and so that he may know the treatment for the one to whom it has happened.
And the other method is that we often need the castration of certain animals, for our benefit by it, such as rams, boars, and cats, and similar animals.
Therefore, I say that castration is done according to two methods. Or by attrition. Or by fissure and section.
In that which is done by attrition, the way of the operation is: that we make the animal sit in hot water, until its testicles are softened and relaxed, and hang down; then rub them with your hand until they are dissolved and do not appear under touch.
In castration by fissure and section, it is necessary that you hold the animal, and press the skin of its testicles with your left hand.
Then tie the suspensory, and cut above each testicle with one cut, until when two testicles emerge; then cut them, after you have scraped each, and do not leave on them from sifaket anything except the thin sifak that is over the vessels.
And this method of castration is better than that which is done by attrition, because attrition perhaps leaves something of the testicles, wherefore the animal desires intercourse.
Then treat the wound until it is healed.
Chapter LXX.
Concerning the treatment of a hermaphrodite, two methods.
A hermaphrodite is from males in two ways. One of them is that there appears in it, in the space that follows, or in the skin of the testicles, in the area between the two testicles, a figure like a woman's vagina, in which there are hairs; and sometimes urine flows from what is in the skin of the testicles.
In women, however, there is one type, and it is a woman's vagina above the pubic bone, just as small male testicles, protruding outwards; one of which is like a man's penis, and two like two testicles.
And the treatment of three types, two from men, and one type from women, is: because it is necessary to cut away the added flesh until their impression is released. Then treat them with the treatment of the other wounds.
In the second type of men, however, from which urine emerges, which is in the skin of the testicles, there is no operation, nor is there any treatment of it at all.
Chapter LXXI.
Concerning the incision of the labia and protruding flesh in women's vulvas.
A swelling may perhaps be added to the natural parts, until its appearance is disfigured and unsightly: and sometimes in some women it is so magnified that it expands like in men, and reaches the point of intercourse.
Therefore, it is necessary to hold the excess swelling with your hand, or with a hook, and cut it; and do not cut too deeply, especially into the deep root, so that a flow of blood does not occur.
Then treat it with the treatment for wounds until it heals.
However, a flesh that arises in the opening of the womb, until it fills it, and perhaps protrudes outward in the likeness of a tail, and because of that some of the ancients call the illness a tailed illness. Therefore, it is necessary to cut it as we cut the swelling, and treat it until it heals.
Concerning the treatment of Alratica, which is a non-perforated vulva of a woman.
Chapter LXXII.
Alratica is, that the vulva of a woman is not perforated, or is a small opening. And it is either natural or accidental.
Accidental is from an illness that preceded: and indeed it is made either from added flesh growth, or by a subtle or thick sifach: and it is made either deep within the womb, or on its sides, or at its upper or lower part: and it prevents intercourse and impregnation, and childbirth, and perhaps prevents menstruation.
And its knowledge is made through the observation of the midwife, if an apparent obstruction is nearby.
If however it is not apparent, then seek it with your fingers, or with a probe.
If however the obstruction is from a subtle sifach, and is near the two lips: then hurry, and break it open.
And it is, that you place pads upon the two lips.
Then place your index fingers of both hands together, and let the woman be on her back with her legs spread out. Then extend the two lips with strength, until the subtle sifach is broken, and the obstruction is opened.
Then take wool, and soak it in oil, and place it over the place: and let the woman apply it every day, so that the place may not be consolidated again.
If however the sifach is thick, dense: then it is necessary to cut it with a spatula wide like a myrtle leaf.
If however the obstruction is from a flesh growth, then suspend it with hooks, and cut it: and have with you medicines that restrain the flow of blood without causing bruising, such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense mixed with egg white.
Then administer a wide lead cannula so that the wound does not heal quickly, hold it for several days: and apply a linen bandage, then treat the rest with the treatment, until it is healed.
And sometimes there is a birth of other flesh growths in the womb: therefore it must be cut in this way: as long as it is not a cancerous abscess; for a cancerous abscess which is in the womb, should not be opposed by iron at all.
Chapter LXXIII.
Concerning the treatment of hemorrhoids, Athehelil, and red Bothor in the vulvas of women.
Hemorrhoids are indeed the opening of the veins, until much blood flows continuously from them.
When hemorrhoids become chronic, they become thelil. And I have already spoken about their various types and symptoms.  And I will say here which of them receive treatment, and which do not.
I say therefore, that hemorrhoids which are in the orifice of the womb, and do not appear to the senses, then there is no treatment with iron for them.
However, those of them that are in the orifice of the womb, upon which sense falls, are those that are treated.
Therefore, it is necessary to place the woman in a cool room. Then, cut the thelil with shears, or with a rough cloth, and sever them from their roots.
Then powder over them, during the flow of blood, from one of the powders of the incisions, without modification; such as acacia, and dragon's blood, and frankincense, and similar to them.
Then, press her feet against the wall for one hour. Then place her in cold water.
If the flow of blood persists, then make her sit in a decoction of sumac, and pomegranate bark, and gallnuts, and similar things.
Then take barley flour, and mix it with honey and vinegar, and make a plaster with it on her back.
If, therefore, blood is drawn out with it, it is good; and if not, apply cupping glasses to the back and armpits, without incision.
Then take wool, and soak it in the juice of tarathit, or the juice of arnoglossa, or the juice of alaulat branches, that is, aglanter, or Pontic wine, and apply it to the place.
When, therefore, the abscess subsides, then treat the place with ointments, until it heals.
Albarth, however, is red, similar to alhaasse heads, and is of a rough appearance.
Therefore, you should cut what appears from it, according to what I said in the thelil equally, and treat it until it is healed.
Chapter LXXIV.
Concerning the perforation of the exit passage which occurs in the womb.
Many kinds of abscesses occur in the womb, such as cancer, and stony abscess, and ulcers, and contusions, and abrasions, that is, herpes, erysipelas, and fistulas, and hemorrhoids, and fissures, and thelil, and a hot abscess.
And I have already described all these illnesses and their types, and their signs, and their treatments in the division: it is necessary to discuss in this treatise the hot abscess which occurs in the womb, when it is from the abscesses that collect pus, and you wish to know how its opening is done with an iron.
Therefore, it is necessary to consider. If, therefore, the pain of the abscess in its beginning is acute with its blow, and inflammation, and fever; and you see it reddened; if sensation falls upon it, then do not hurry with its perforation; and treat it with that which helps in its maturation, until when the sharpness of the occurrences ceases.
Then it is necessary to make the woman sit on a seat that slants two seats, and reclines, or is supported on her back, and bring her feet to the lower part of her abdomen; and let her thighs be spread out, and let her place her arms under her knees, and tie each with a suitable bandage to it.
Then the midwife should sit on the right side, and administer the instrument with which the opening of the womb is made.
And it is necessary when you wish to administer this instrument, that you fix with the radio the depth of the woman's womb; so that it is not that which enters from the instrument more than the depth of the womb, and hurts the weak one.
However, if the instrument is more than the depth, then it is necessary to place cushions over the covering of the depth of the womb; so that the entire entry of the instrument into the depth of the womb may be prevented; and it is necessary that it put the rotation, which runs on the instrument, from the upper side; and that it holds the instrument, and that the minister revolves the rotation until the depth of the womb is opened.
When therefore the exit appears, and it is touched by the hand, and it is smooth and soft, then it is necessary to separate it at its head with a wide spatula.
When therefore the pus is evacuated, then it is necessary to place on the wound a linen cloth soaked in rose oil; or green oil, in which there is some astringency; and place the linen cloth, and extract the section in the depth of the womb.
And place externally over the womb, and over the pubis, clean wool soaked in water in which mulberries have been boiled.
Then be treated after two days with an ointment suitable for it, until it is healed.
And sometimes the womb and the wound are washed, so that they are cleansed with water and honey; then with water in which the lily root or long aristolochia has been boiled; then return to the ointment.
If the wound is hidden inside the womb, then it is necessary to refrain from treating it with iron; indeed, treat it with what we have said in the division.
Chapter 70.
Doctrines of Midwives, how living embryos should be treated, emerging in a non-natural form.
It is necessary for the midwife to know first the form of natural childbirth.
Therefore, from its signs, when you see a woman pressing downwards, desiring to smell the air, and relieved by what is upon her concerning childbirth, and the exit of the embryo is hastened: then know that this childbirth is according to a natural form, and its exit is over her head, and the afterbirth with it: or hanging, or dependent from the navel.
Therefore, when you see these signs, then it is necessary that her belly be pressed, so that the embryo descends quickly.
For when it descends over her head, the afterbirth descends with it, and it is cleansed from those superfluities by a good cleansing.
However, whatever is beyond this childbirth is unnatural, blameworthy.
For sometimes the fetus emerges upon its feet, and its body emerges before its head, and its feet and hand together, or one foot; or its head and hand emerge; or the abdomen emerges; and perhaps it is turned upon its neck, and similar figures are blameworthy.
Therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to be prudent, astute in all these matters, and to beware of error and deception; and I am showing every figure among them, and how its nature is in it, and what is signified by it, and what should be done about it.
When the fetus emerges above its head in its natural emergence, and it becomes violent upon the woman, and childbirth becomes difficult for her, and you see her strength already weakened, then have her sit upon a seat, or let women hold her; and apply vapors to her vulva with decocted fenugreek and with moist oils.
Then let the midwife insert a small spatula between her fingers, and with it split the afterbirth, and cut it with her nail until whatever moisture is in it runs out; and press the woman's abdomen until the fetus descends.
If it does not descend, then it is necessary that the woman be given an enema of fenugreek mucilage with sesame oil.
Then command her, after the enema, to compress herself; and make her sneeze with condiments, and hold her mouth and nose for one hour. For the fetus will emerge immediately.
If two hands of the fetus emerge first, it is necessary to gently draw them back gradually. If, however, it does not obey in entering, then place the woman on a seat, and lift her feet up; then shake the seat on the ground; and let the woman be held so that she does not fall during the shaking.
If therefore the hands do not emerge, and the fetus dies, then cut them, and extract the rest of the fetus; and tie its hand with a cloth, and extract it with equality; for it will emerge.
The exit of the embryo is above its feet. When the fetus emerges above its feet, then it is necessary to push them upwards; then turn the fetus gradually; afterwards, straighten it.
Then take one of its feet, and extend it gradually.
When therefore they emerge up to the hips, then command that it be compressed, and make it sneeze with a condiment; for it will emerge, if God wills.
And if it does not emerge with what we have narrated, then reduce the fetus gradually, until you place it in its natural form. For it will then emerge with ease.
If it is prevented with all that we have narrated, then take from marsh-mallow mucilage, and sesame oil, and dissolved gum; and pound it all in a mortar with good pounding; then line with it the woman's vulva and the lower part of her abdomen. Then make her sit in warm water until the hypochondria are relieved.
When therefore you see that her lower parts are now heated, then make her a poultice of myrrh and apply it. When therefore the poultice has been held for one hour, then make her sit on a seat. Then make her sneeze; and pinch her nose and mouth; and press the lower part of her abdomen with gentle pressure. For the foetus will be expelled immediately.
The exit of the foetus, upon the knees and hands. When it emerges according to this form, then gently insert her hands. Then the woman will be laid on her back, and her feet hang downwards; and she lie on the bed, and fold her hands. Then press gently on her abdomen. Then pull out what is under her feet from the pillows, until it is prepared at the lower part. If the fetus does not emerge, then take her feet and move them with a forceful motion. Then press gently on what is above the hypochondria, until the fetus ascends upwards. Then let her introduce her hands, and gently align the fetus: and instruct the woman to compress, until the fetus emerges.
The exit of the fetus hanging sideways from the hands. A hand be introduced in the reduction. If however she cannot reduce it, then raise the woman, and make her walk.
If however she cannot walk, then make her lie down on a bed: and shake her legs with a violent shaking.
Then administer the liniment which you prepared from mucilages.
If indeed she receives it (the hand) from childbirth, then make her sit on a seat. Then strive in the reduction of the hand, and straighten the fetus according to its natural form; and make the woman sneeze, and order her to compress herself, until it emerges.
The exit of the fetus is upon her neck, with outstretched hands, and its face is towards its mother's back. The midwife ought to hold the fetus's hand, then turn it gradually, and straighten it with ease.
When it is straightened, then place upon its vulva what we have narrated of oil and mucilages.
Then order her to move slowly. When you have done that, then make her sit on a seat, and order her to lean to the right side. Then order her to compress herself, and make her sneeze with condiments.
The exit of the prepared fetus on the side. When it descends to the opening of the womb in this manner with the afterbirth, then cut the afterbirth with your fingernail, until it is evacuated from the moisture.
If therefore it hangs over the face of the fetus, and its neck, then cut it from the navel: so that it is not retained, and dies.
Then push the fetus inward, until you place it as it should be: and order the woman to compress herself several times: for it will emerge through a light exit.
The exit of twins, or many fetuses. Know that twins are often born, and sometimes three and four are born. However, that is unusual, and rarely happens.
However, five is a thing beyond nature, and they do not entirely live: and it contains them, however they multiply, with a single afterbirth; and they are separated by a sifach, which distinguishes between them, tied to the navel of each of them.
And sometimes they emerge like a single fetus emerges by natural, easy exit; and they emerge by that praiseworthy exit, as we have narrated.
If some of them are difficult at birth, and they reach the unnatural forms that we have mentioned, then treat them as we have narrated; and let the midwife be skillful, and let her do what she does with ease and with slowness, and let her be inventive according to all figures with whatever is prepared for her by leading ingenuity to health.
A recollection of that which is formed in the womb from embryos, and miscarriages.
Sometimes one, two, three, four, five, six, seven, and more than ten are formed in the womb; and it has already been verified with me that a woman, while miscarrying, expelled seven, and another fifteen, all formed.
That which was done by the measurement of the glorious sage.
Therefore, all this must be in the knowledge of the Midwife. Perhaps, for it will come to her something like that: therefore let her oppose it with that which is fitting.
Chapter LXXVI.
Concerning the extraction of a dead fetus.
When you treat a dead fetus with what I said in the division of medicine, and it does not emerge, and you are forced to use an operation with the hand, then you should consider.
If therefore the woman is of sound constitution, and is not afflicted with any of the accidents from which her life is feared, then the woman should be laid on a bed, on her back, and her head lowered, and her legs raised; and the attendant should hold her from both sides, or she should be bound to the bed, so that her body is not moved during the extraction of the fetus.
Then moisten the mouth of the womb with moist oils, along with the mucilage of marshmallow, fennel, and flaxseed.
Then let the midwife anoint her hand with these oils and mucilages; then let her insert it into the womb with ease. Then let her seek with it the place where she can fix the hooks from the fetus, and suitable places for it.
Therefore, consider then, if the fetus is prepared to descend head-first; then fix the hooks in its eyes, or in its neck, or in its mouth, or in its palate, or under its chin, or in its fork, or in the nearby ribs, and under the hypochondria.
If, however, the descent is on its feet, then in its pubis and middle ribs.
And it is necessary that she hold the hook with her right hand; and place its curve in that which is between the fingers of her left hand; and insert it with her hand with ease, and fix the hook in any of those places that we have said, until she reaches the empty space.
Then let her fix another hook, or a third if necessary, in opposition to it, so that the contraction is equal and does not lean to one side.
Then let it be extended with equal extension, and let the extension not be according to straightness alone, but let it be moved in every direction, so that its exit may be eased; as is done with a tooth during its extraction.
And it is necessary in that which is between it, that the extension be softened. If a part of it is retained, then the midwife should anoint some fingers with oil, and insert them from the side, so that she may revolve that which is retained with them.
If, therefore, a part of the fetus emerges, then let the hooks be moved to another location which is higher; do this until the entire fetus emerges.
If, therefore, the hand emerges before the other parts, and it is not possible to return it, because of its constriction, then it is necessary that a cloth be wrapped around it. Then pull it out until the whole thing emerges; cut it either at the ankle joint or at the elbow; and thus you should do for the other hand, and for both feet.
If, however, the fetus's head is large, or constriction occurs in its exit, or water has collected in its head, then it is necessary to insert a thorny Spatumile between the fingers, and to split the head with it, and to extract the water; or to break it with the instrument called Almisdac, the form of which is presented with the forms of the other instruments in the chapter that follows this one.
Similarly, if the fetus is of a large-headed nature, then it is necessary to separate it with algengematu; or extract it with misdach, as we said. Then pull the bones with forceps.
If the head emerges and is constricted at the fork, then it should be split until the moisture in the chest is released.
If, however, it is not entangled, nor does it yield to exit, then cut the head into fragments, as much as possible for you.
If the lower belly has an abscess, or is hydrops, then it is necessary to pierce it until moisture runs from it.
If, however, the fetus is over its own feet, then its extraction is easy, and its direction towards the birth canal is gentle.
If, however, it is constricted at the belly or chest, then it is necessary to extract it with a cloth wrapped around your hand, and to split the belly or chest until what is within runs out.
If the other limbs are removed, and the head returns, then let the midwife insert her left hand. If therefore the birth canal is open, then let her insert her hand deep into the uterus, and seek the head with it, and pull it with her fingers until the birth canal: then let her insert one or two hooks into it, and pull it with it.
If, however, the birth canal is constricted due to a hot abscess that occurs in it, then it is not necessary to labor over it, but rather it is necessary to administer the effusion of moist, oily things, and apply plasters; and she should sit in waters that soften and moisten.
If the fetus is on its side, then it is possible to straighten it; then administer what we said regarding a living fetus.
If it is not possible, then it should be cut into pieces and extracted.
And it is necessary that you not leave any of the afterbirth inside at all.
When you have completed your operation, then administer what we said about hot abscesses that occur in the uterus.
If a flow of blood occurs, then it should be placed in astringent water, and administer the rest of the treatment.
And I myself have seen a woman in whose belly a dead fetus was; then she became pregnant again over it, afterwards another fetus died also. Therefore, a long time later, an abscess occurred in her navel, and it swelled until it opened, and it began to discharge pus.
I was therefore summoned for her treatment, and I treated her for a long time, and the wound did not heal.
I therefore placed upon it certain plasters of strong attraction, and bone emerged from the place. Then a few days passed, and another bone emerged. I was therefore amazed by this, since there is no bone in the belly.
I therefore thought that they were from the bones of a dead fetus.
I therefore searched the wound, and extracted many bones, the woman being in her best condition. And she lived thus for a long time, and little pus was discharged from the place.
And I did not firmly establish this as foreign, unless because there is knowledge and assistance in it for the artful physician to administer, with a hand skilled in healing.
Chapter LXXVII.
Concerning the shapes of the necessary instruments in the extraction of the fetus.
The shape of the pivot by which the matrix is opened.
Here below is the shape of the Strictorium, by which books are equalized, to which equally there are two pivots at the two ends of two pieces of wood. However, this pivot ought to be finer than the strictorium, and to be made either from ebony or from boxwood.
And the width of each piece of wood is about two fingers, and the edge of each of the two is about one finger, and the length of each of them is a palm and a half.  And in the middle of the two pieces of wood are two additions of the same type of wood, which are already fastened to each, the length of which is half a palm and a little more, and the width of each is about two fingers, or a little more.
And these two additions are inserted into the mouth of the matrix, so that it may open with them when you turn the two pivots.
Another shape similar to that, finer and lighter.
Let it be made of ebony wood, or boxwood, according to the likeness of forceps. However, at its extremity there be two additions, as you see here above: and the length of each of these additions is about a palm: and the width of each is two fingers.
When therefore you wish to open the matrix with it, then make the woman sit on a bed, with her feet hanging down, opening that which is between her legs. Then introduce these two joined additions into the matrix: and you hold the lower end of the instrument between her thighs. Then open your hand with the instrument, as you do with scissors, equally according to the amount of opening of the mouth of the matrix you wish, until the midwife does what she wants.
Another form of dizziness, which the ancients called the form of the impelling force by which the fetus is propelled.
The Almisdac form, by which the large head is crushed; and it is likened to a pair of tongs, which have teeth at the end, as you see.
And sometimes it becomes elongated, like tongs according to this form, as you see, which has teeth like saw teeth, by which it is cut and crushed.
The form of a hook having one spine.
The form of a hook having two spines.
The form of a spatula having two ends for severing the head.
The form of two spatulas, also broad.
All these instruments are used, and the more their types are multiplied and prepared by the craftsman, the more rapid and effective his operation is, and the more highly regarded he is among people because of their quantity.
Therefore, do not disregard any instrument, without it being prepared by you. For their necessity is inexcusable.
Chapter LXXVIII.
Of the extraction of the Secundinae.
When the Secundina is retained at birth, then you ought to instruct the woman to hold her breath. Then make her sneeze with condiments: and press your hand upon her mouth and nostrils. If therefore it emerges by this, it is good.
If not, then take a pot and pierce a hole in the lid: and put into it herbs that open the mother's mouth, such as calamint, rue, dill, chamomile, hyssop, cinnamon wood, and centaury: take all these herbs, or some of them, and immerse them in water, and place them on the fire.
Then place a reed tube over the hole in the pot's lid, and the other end in the mother's mouth: and let her hold it until the vapor reaches the cavity of the uterus.
Then make her sneeze, as we said. For the afterbirth will come out with ease.
If, however, it remains after this treatment, and does not come out, then instruct the midwife to immerse her hand in sesame oil, or marshmallow mucilage. Then let her introduce it into the vagina, and search for the afterbirth with it. When therefore she has found it, then let her take it and extend it gradually.
If, however, it is attached deep within the womb, then let her introduce her hand, as we have described, until, when she has found the afterbirth, she extracts it gradually and straightly, so that the womb does not fall during the forceful pulling; rather, it must be moved easily to the right and left sides, then the amount of pulling should be increased. For then it obeys, and is preserved, and escapes from adherence.
If the mouth of the womb is constricted, as we have already described: treat it with sneezing, and with a medicine pot containing herbs.
If, however, it does not emerge with all that we have described, then be careful not to tire yourself in repeating the extraction.
But it is necessary that whatever emerges from it to the mouth of the womb be tied to the woman's hip. Then give her an enema with the tetrapharmacon ointment. For it will putrefy it after days, and it will dissolve, and it will emerge.
However, when it putrefies, a bad odor rises from it to the stomach and head, and harms that frail one. Therefore, suitable fumigations should be administered to it.
Some of the ancients have already experienced the fumigation of nasturtium and dried figs.
The shape of the instrument with which a woman is fumigated during the retention of the Secundinae and menses.
Let it be made of glass similar to alembic, or of bronze: and let the wide end be placed over the fire: and let the fumigation be placed over the prunas: let her hold it, until that fumigation is consumed: and again another.
Chapter LXXIX.
Concerning the treatment of the Non-perforated Anus.
Many children are born with their anuses not perforated, which have already been obstructed by a subtle sifac. Therefore, it is necessary for the midwife to perforate that sifac with her finger: and if not, to perforate it with a sharp Spatumili: and let her beware lest her hand touch it.
Then place upon it wool steeped in wine and oil. Then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
If however you fear that it will be obstructed, then place a lead cannula in the opening for many days, and remove it when the child wishes to relieve itself.
And now, obstruction of the anus also occurs from the consolidation of a wound, or an abscess. Therefore, it is necessary to cut that consolidation, then treat it with what we have said concerning treatment and lead.
Chapter Eighty.
Concerning the treatment of Fistulas that occur in the lower part.
Fistulas that occur in the lower part are swelling, and thickening that occur near the anus externally, or in the space of one part, and is one fistula, or several.
When therefore that swelling is old, it is opened, and watery white moisture, or thin pus, runs from it.
And sometimes there are penetrating ones from these fistulas. Therefore, penetrating ones are recognized by what emerges from evacuation and windiness when the sick person uses evacuation, and perhaps worms emerge from them.
And some of them are fistulas, when they are penetrating in the space to the bladder and to the urethra. And some of them are penetrating to the hip joint and to the tailbone.
By which a penetrating fistula to the anus is known, even from a non-penetrating one, is that you introduce your index finger into the anus; and introduce a thin probe into the fistula from air or iron; when there is no winding.
If there is winding in it, then introduce into it a thin lead probe, or a hair from horse hairs, until you feel the probe or hair on your finger.
If you do not feel it completely, nor does anything come out of the opening by excretion, nor wind, nor worm as I said: then know that it is not penetrating; therefore hasten to the treatment.
And when the fistula is penetrating to the bladder or to the urinary passage, then its sign is the excretion of urine from it, and the prohibition of it from this so that the place is consolidated with medicines.
If however it is penetrating to the hip joint, or to the hip bone, then its sign is that the probe reaches there, if there is no winding in it to the bone; and the finding of pain in the surrounding part, and that pus emerges from it constantly; and that medicine is of no avail in it, nor is it consolidated with ointment.
And in all these penetrating fistulas there is no cure at all; and their treatment is labor and vanity to him, from foolish physicians, upon whom it is formed.
In that one, however, which is not penetrating, and not ancient, healing is hoped for with a saw, according to what I tell you, and I have already tested; and it is that the sick person lies between your hands on his back; and raise his legs above his thighs, and his thighs be inclined to his belly. Then introduce a lead or bronze probe, if there is winding in the fistula, until you know where the probe reaches.
If however the sick person feels it around the anus, then it is necessary that you introduce your index finger into the anus.
If therefore you feel the probe already to have penetrated itself detected, beyond what you feel between your finger, and between it and the flesh; then therefore know certainly, that it is penetrating: therefore do not labor on it, because there is no cure for it, as we said.
However, they have said that in some people the flesh heals, and from the medication with which he is hoping for help, it is that you heat a fine cautery according to the width of the fistula, as has been said before, and you introduce it heated into the fistula until it reaches the anus. Then repeat it twice, or thrice, until you know that all those added, filtered flesh, which are like bird feather tubes, are now burned.
Then treat it with lint wrapped in butter, until those burned flesh come out; then treat it with ointments that generate flesh and consolidate it.
If therefore it heals, it is good.
And if not, then it will never heal with any other treatment.
And if a probe emerges, and does not reach your finger in the anus, and there is a thick layer of flesh between it and the probe, or if such a thing occurs: and you see a fistula on the surface following the skin, then cut the skin from the beginning of the fistula, and you proceed by cutting with the probe that is in the fistula, until the incision reaches where the probe's end reaches, and the probe is released and falls out.
Then cleanse those filtered flesh, which resemble the hollow of a bird's feather, so that nothing remains of them.
If, however, blood overcomes you, and separates you from your operation so that you cut those tissues, then it is better and more quickly helpful to cauterize it with fire or with a sharp medicine.
Because cauterization with fire brings together good habits, burning those added tissues, and cutting off the blood, and drying out the moisture.
Then treat it with lint wrapped in butter, or crushed herbs, until the place produces pus, and those burnt tissues emerge in the pus.
Then treat the wound with consolidating ointments, which generate hard flesh: and there are medicines in which there are astringency and drying.
For when it is restored, then it is already healed: and you do not fear recurrence.
If however the fistula has already reached the depth of the anus, and is extending from bodily excess, then insert your finger into the anus and investigate with it.
If therefore you feel a tension, and between you and it is a membrane of sifac or flesh, and it is close to penetration, then there is no operation on it, except according to the way of trust and hope.
This is because one of three methods is administered in it: either to cauterize it, as we said, or to dissect it until you reach its concavity, so that it is possible for you to insert sutures, and healing from nearby. And do not proceed much with the incision, so that you do not sever the muscle containing the anus, and thus cause an unintended, involuntary bowel exit. Then treat it with what we have said.
Perhaps it will be healed; or else, you will lengthen the membrane, and cleanse it after having cut it near the anus, as we said, whether with the probe or with another instrument of a sharp end. Then cleanse those filtered flesh that are in the incision, until you can with scrutiny.
Then attempt the restoration of the entire wound with the incision and the upper opening where it consolidates; and an open fistula will remain in the inner part of the anus, and it will be easier on the patient.
And sometimes the fistula is sutured in this way: and it is, when you insert a probe into the fistula, and it is on the side of the anus near the surface of the body with skin and the margin of the anus; then take a probe with a perforated end, which is like a needle of aleskef, that is, of those preparing hides, according to this form. And insert into it a thread wrapped with five threads, and approximately that. Then insert the probe with the thread into the fistula until it reaches its concavity.
If therefore it penetrates the inner margin of the anus nearby, then pull the thread out of that opening: so that you insert your finger into the anus, and pull out the end of the thread, and bring together the two ends; and tie each, and leave it for one or two days; and the more the thread penetrates the flesh, tie it tightly, until those flesh which are between the two ends of the thread are cut and fall away; then treat the wound until it consolidates and heals.
If however the opening of the fistula is not penetrating, then make it penetrate in whatever way is prepared for you, unless it is very deep. For you do not have the ability to do this for fear of hurting it.
Then do with it as we said, until it is healed.
A pointed spatula, with which the fistula is split, whose one curvature is very sharp, and the other part is not sharp, so that it does not harm what is not necessary, as you see.
Chapter Eighty-one.
Concerning the suturing of hemorrhoids, from which blood flows from their incision: and the treatment of fissures.
Hemorrhoids are of two kinds. For they are either inside the anus, like bags of charcoal or prunes, or like bunches of grapes themselves. And there are small ones and large ones; and blood flows from them always, and there is one, and there are many. And some are outside the anus, and at its extremities.
However, those outside the anus are mostly of little moisture, from which yellow water or a little blood runs with constant flow; and they are according to the color of the body.
And the treatment of that which is within the anus is to order the sick person to strain, and to express it until it comes out the anus, and the piles appear to you. Therefore, also suspend them with hooks, or hold them with your fingernail. Then cut them at their roots.
However, if the hooks do not hold on them, because of their moisture and their softening: then take them with a rough cloth, or with wool from a double-layered fleece, and pull them out with your fingers. Then cut them, and after the incision sprinkle some hot powders over them, so as to stand for them in the place of a cautery. Or cauterize them according to what preceded in the chapter on cauterization. Then treat them with the rest of the treatment, until they are healed.
If the anus does not yield to evacuation, then inject the sick person with an enema containing a small amount of a caustic substance, so that it washes away what is in the anus, and it will yield to evacuation quickly, when the sick person expels it.
If however the piles are external to the anus, then their removal is easy and simple: and it is that you should grasp them with your fingernail, or grasp them with a hook, and cut them off. Then treat them with what we have said, until they are healed.
And whoever abhors cutting with iron, then it is necessary that you administer their suture in this manner. Take a wrapped thread, and insert it into the needle. Then pull the pile upwards, and make it penetrate with the needle at its root on the other side: and wrap the two ends of the thread, at the lower part of the needle, and let them be lengthwise: and tighten the pile with a strong constriction. Then tie the thread, and remove the needle.
Do that with all the piles, and leave one of them: and do not treat it, so that superfluity of blood may run from it.
Then place a cloth soaked in rose oil, or camphor oil, over the anus, and put it over the anus: and tell the patient to rest. Then leave it until it falls.
When therefore the piles fall, treat them with ointments and other treatments until they are healed.
Concerning the treatment of Fissures. For they often occur from the drying up of feces and from constriction of nature.
When therefore they become hardened, and medicine is ineffective on them, then it is necessary to scrape them with a pointed spatula, or with your fingernail, until they become moist, and the outer layer which prevents their consolidation is removed. Then treat them until they consolidate as they should.
If however they do not consolidate, then scrape them again with a more forceful instrument than the first, until they bleed much, and are scraped clean; then treat them until they heal.
Concerning the treatment of inverted and non-inverted almirmar, and of dry athelil and formicae.
Chapter LXXXII.
Know that almirmar is nothing other than a round knot, according to the color of the body, similar to the head of a nail; and it forms throughout the body, especially in the lower parts of the feet and toes; and pain occurs from them during walking.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut that which is around the nail, and to hold it with scissors, or with a hook, and to remove it from its root.
Then treat it; and if you wish, cauterize it according to what has preceded in its chapter.
From dry athelil, one is a small, rough, filtered, round eminence above the surface of the body. And frequently they occur in the hands of children.
And its treatment is, that you extend the athelil and cut it; or bind it with a silk thread; or with a thread, until it falls. And if you wish, cauterize it according to what has been stated with fire, or with a sharp medicine; and thus the inverted athelil are eradicated, and not the non-inverted ones with the instrument which I will name.
An ant is also a small, filtered, coarse eminence on the surface of the body, going deep very much; and when cold finds it, a sensation like an ant's bite is felt in it, and it spreads throughout the body; and most of it occurs in the hands.
And its treatment is to take a cannula from a goose's quill, or a strong vulture's feather; and place that cannula over the Ant, until the cannula's circle encompasses its parts. Then, rotate your hand with the cannula, until you separate what is around the Ant; and place it deep within the flesh, and eradicate the Ant from its root.
And if you wish, do it with a cannula of air or iron, according to this form.
Let the cannula's upper part be at a height pulling the deaf wrapped thing, so that its grasp and rotation over the finger are alleviated.
And if you wish, cauterize the Ant according to what was previously stated in the cauterization of Athelil in the chapter on the cautery.
Chapter LXXXIII.
Concerning the shapes of instruments which are administered for enemas in diseases of the anus, and for loosening the bowels, and for colic.
Sometimes an enema is made of silver, or of bronze, or of cast brass, or of hammered brass.
And sometimes it is made of these instruments small and large, according to those in which they are administered.
Therefore, that which is administered in the treatment of young children should be small; and whose anuses are weak or painful, so too should their enemas be.
Large enema.
Its tube, that is, the upper head to which the bladder is tied, should be wide in this manner; and it should have a division so that the bladder is tied as you see, and its lower end, which is inserted into the anus, should be blunt, that is, not open, smooth, very much, drawing to thinness; and on one side of it should be two holes, and on the other side one hole.
And the size of the hole should be according to the thickness of the rays, and a little thicker; and let the bladder in which the medicines are placed be from an animal's bladder, or from parchment, which should be in the form of a small asofrat; whose root is one palm.
And let it be pierced with many holes, as it is rolled, and let the space between each hole be the thickness of a finger. Then insert strong thread, made from ten threads or thereabouts, into those holes, and bind the bladder with it like an asofrat. When therefore the bladder is placed in it, let it be tied at the head of the clyster between two distinguishing constrictions with a strong binding. Then administer the clyster with it.
And this is the shape of the bladder here depicted, and of the holes.
The method of administering the clyster with it is: fill the bladder or vessel with medicine, which should be heated according to the patient's tolerance; and be careful that it is not excessively hot or excessively cold.
Then the sick person should lie on their back, and raise their legs upwards; and place beneath them *inthaan*, that is, a skin upon which people lie, or a linen cloth.
Then anoint the end of the clyster with oil, or with egg white, or with the mucilage of fenugreek, or similar substances. Then introduce it into the anus with ease. Then let the attendant grasp the bladder with both hands simultaneously and express it forcefully until all that is in the clyster is evacuated into the intestine.
Then withdraw the clyster, and let the sick person raise their legs towards the wall.
If the medicine compels him towards death, then let him hold it as long as possible. And if he sleeps with it the whole night, it is more beneficial in the end.
If however you clyster him in illnesses of looseness of the belly, and his outcome is hastened: then clyster him again, until he is cured.
If however there are hemorrhoids in the anus, or sores that cause harm, then let the clyster be very thin, gentle, from the clysters with which children are clystered.
And this should be the form of a thin clyster over which there should be tight skin.
Chapter LXXXIV.
I have already spoken in the division on the treatment of wounds, what was fitting for the book, and I will say here the treatment of them with the hand and with medicines, according to completion.
Therefore, I say that wounds are diversified according to that by which the wounds are made, and according to the place upon which the wound falls.
Therefore, the things by which wounds are made are often like the throwing of a stone; or the incision of a sword, or knife; or the striking with a lance, or wood, or arrow; and similar things from many objects.
And wounds which are made according to the places of the body are, such as a wound which falls upon the head, or neck, or chest, or belly, or liver; and similar ones from those members.
And I am narrating the treatments of certain wounds, so that I may set forth this reasoning and rule concerning the remaining wounds.
And I will begin with simple wounds of the head, because the recollection of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
I say therefore. When a simple wound occurs on the head, and there is no fracture of the bone, consider then, if it is from the throwing of a stone, or similar things, and has only broken the skin; and is a large wound, and you fear a bad outcome of a hot abscess: then hurry, and perform a cephalic phlebotomy immediately; and do not delay it; and draw from its blood according to the amount of its strength; if nothing prevents phlebotomy, especially if the blood flowing from the wound is little. And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded part.
And let the phlebotomy be on the opposite side of the wounded part. And let the sick person avoid repletion, wine, and heavy foods.
If, however, something prohibits lessening the intake of food, then lessen it from food, and let him not approach meats, nor wines.
And if a hot abscess occurs on the wound, apply a poultice soaked in rose oil alone, or with wine in which there is astringency.
If, however, you are certain of the absence of a hot abscess, then if the wound is recent with its own blood, and the air has not altered it, apply the powder I shall describe shortly; and if the air has already altered it, then apply some ointments until it is healed.
If, however, the wound is large, or from a sword cut, and similar things; and its edges do not come together by means of pads, then bring them together with stitches, according to what I will describe in wounds of the belly concerning their stitching.
If, however, the wound is now exposed to the bone, and hangs, and its retention is only in a small suspender: then cut it. Then treat the wound with medicines, and restore the tough flesh in place of the skin, and prescribe for the sick person food in which there is bulk, such as heads and cooked wheat, until it is like porridge, and similar things.
If you find putrefaction in the skin, and it does not adhere to the bone: then cut off the entire putrefied part, and throw it away; then treat it.
If, however, in these wounds there is an artery, or vein from which blood flows: and it is not stopped by medicines, then investigate the artery. If therefore you find it not severed, then sever it with a spatula, or tie it. If necessity compels when what we have said does not help, then cauterize it, until the blood is stopped.
If however these wounds are small, simple, then their treatment is easy, slight: so that you powder them with this powder, because it consolidates them before the air alters them.
Method of the flesh-growing powder. Take one part of olibanum, and two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of quicklime: let all be thoroughly ground and sifted: and fill the wound with it, and bind it with a good bandage, until it adheres well to it, and clots with the blood.
Then bind it tightly from above with bandages, and leave it, and do not loosen it while it remains, so that it does not fester. For the medicine is not removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes lime alone does this, when you do not have olibanum and dragon's blood present, and the latter also does the same work in many large wounds, when it is fitting to place it upon them and bind them, and are fresh wounds with their own blood, which the air has not altered.
If however the air has already altered them by some alteration, especially when it is summer time, then the intention of applying this powder is not appropriate: therefore treat it with what we have said, by applying some digestive ointments to them, or applying poultices of barley flour made with water and honey, until they discharge pus. Then treat them with the remaining treatment until they are healed.
However, if a fracture occurs in the bone with a wound, and it is small, then remove it with forceps; and I will now describe the treatment of fractures of the skull in what will follow.
Concerning wounds of the neck.
However, if a wound occurs in the neck, then there is no difference between it in treatment and a wound of the head when it is simple.
If, however, the nerve of the neck, or artery, has already been cut: then, if it is a nerve, there is no cure for it, except to avoid treatment that causes strong astringency, such as ziniar, and zegi, and similar things. Because they harm the nerves and contract them.
And do not put anything cold on the wound at all, because the substance of the nerves is cold, and its continuation is with the brain, which is the highest of the members.  And let that which is used for treatment be mild, such as lime washed with fresh water infused with oil, or with rose oil, and tuttia, when washed also with fresh water; and the rest of the mineral stones, in this manner, and moist ointments.
If, however, the wound is large, then administer sutures; or constrict its edges with strips. Then treat it until it is healed.
If however the wound has depth, or a sinus occurs within it in the lower part, in which pus has already collected, then pierce it in the more depressed place within it.
If, however, an artery in the wound has been incised, and its blood has begun to flow, then separate, or cut it, and tie it; or cauterize it, if necessity calls for it.
If, however, the larynx has been incised, either in part or entirely, and the trachea has been saved, then stitch together the skin edges over the larynx, and do not touch the larynx itself, rather, straighten it and restore it to its natural form. Then bind it with a suitable constriction, and leave it for some days. Then treat it with ointments containing astringents and drying agents, until it is healed.
If, however, the wound has already made an impression on the bone, then probe it: then if there are fragments of bone within it, extract them all, or as many as you can: and leave what cannot be removed until the wound has festered, and its discharge is separated and lessened.
If the wound is recent, then powder it with the powder I have described. If, however, bone is visible in the wound and it becomes a fistula, then treat it with that which is mentioned in its chapter.
Concerning a wound in the shoulder blades.
If there is a blow from a lance or knife, and you see its depth, then consider. If therefore wind escapes from the wound, when the sick person gasps from it, then know that the wound is mortal.
If its depth is not great, and it is fresh with its own blood, then do not put powder on it from the first hour, nor bind it, so that blood is not retained in its depth, and returns to the heart, and kills the sick person; but place attracting ointment on it, and loosen it twice a day.
If, however, the ointment is not present to you, then place old wool in the wound's opening, so that it sucks what emerges from it of moistures; and cause the sick person to sleep upon the wound, so that what is gathered there may run.
If, however, three days or more have passed since the wound, and no spasm occurs to the sick person, nor bad tremor of the heart, nor constriction in breathing, and you see the rest of his conditions good, then know that the wound is safe. Therefore, treat him with bandages and other treatment until he is healed.
If, however, his healing is excused, and it always opens again, then know that it has now become a fistula. Therefore, treat it according to its chapter.
And if the wound is from the incision of a sword or knife, and is simple on the surface of the chest or back, then treat it with what preceded concerning sutures, if it is large, or with powder, if it is small.
If, however, it has already made an impression on the bone and chipped pieces from it, then investigate the wound and hasten to remove those fragments, if they are separated. If, however, they are not separated, then leave them until the wound has become putrid, for their extraction will be easier.
However, for the treatment of other accidental wounds in the remaining limbs, the judgment is that which we have said.
Concerning wounds of the belly, and the exit of the intestines, and their expulsion.
A rupture occurring in the belly is great, medium, and small.
From a great one, therefore, sometimes an intestine, or a number of intestines, emerges.
Therefore, their protrusion occurs, and their suture is more violent and difficult.
And a small rupture also becomes difficult in another way. Therefore it follows that their reduction is more difficult if you do not hasten their return to their place immediately, because it becomes inflated, opened, and enlarged, hence its entrance becomes difficult.
Therefore, a medium rupture is better, because with it there is no difficult reduction of the intestine, as there is with these two types.
Know that the suture of the belly is according to four methods.
Two common sutures are appropriate for the suture of the belly and for the suture of the remaining wounds of the body; and two other sutures are appropriate specifically for the remaining wounds. However, they are more appropriate for the suture of the belly; and I will tell you each one with an explanation and a statement.
Therefore, I say that when the wound is small, and something from the intestine comes out of it, and it is difficult to return it, then it is because of one of two methods. Either because of the smallness of the disruption, as we have said, or because there is inflation in the intestine due to the coldness of air.
Therefore, when this is so, it is necessary to warm it, just as one would immerse a sponge or wet cloth in simply lukewarm water. Or boil it in water with squinancy, and cyperus, and spica, and moisten the intestine with it, until the inflation resolves.
Sometimes, wine with astringency does it, and is better than water alone in resolving the inflation.
When therefore you see the inflation already resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which mallow or marshmallow has been boiled. For by that its entry is relieved with a small thing.
If however its return is excused after this treatment, then cut a little in the rupture with the instrument by which hemorrhoids are cut.
And the first treatment is better when prepared, than the incision; and no compulsion to cut is made after necessity and failure of the instrument.
Let one part of it be twisted, sharp, and the other part not sharp, and the slender end not be with the slenderness of a Spatumil, but be somewhat blunter: and it is that which is likened to a caiada, as you see.
Therefore, when the wound is enlarged and the intestine enters, then it is necessary that its reduction be according to its natural form in its proper place, if possible over it; because it is better.
When however the rupture is large in the lower part of the belly, then it is necessary that you make the sick person lie on his back, and raise his legs higher than his head. And if it is in the upper part of the belly, then place his head and chest higher than his lower parts.
And similarly, if the rupture is in one of the two parts of the belly, then place your intention and your consideration always in this, that the part in which the wound is should be higher than the other part.
And so it is necessary that you administer this in large wounds and in medium-sized wounds.
In small wounds, however, place it according to what is prepared for you.
Then have a pious attendant present, who holds the entire rupture with his hand, and bring together its edges. Then uncover from it to the one who follows, a bit after a bit: and this is the manner of a single common suture.
And it is that you take a needle, or a number of needles, according to the amount of the wound's width. Then leave from the extremity of the rupture the amount of a ring finger, that is, of the physician: and fix one needle beyond that into which you insert a thread at the two edges of the skin, with the two edges thus made underneath the skin from the interior, until you make it penetrate from the other side, and you have now brought together two edges of the skin, and the two edges thus made: and four threads are made.
Then tighten with the double thread in the circumference of the needle several times from both sides together, until the two edges of the wound are brought together by a proper joining.
Then leave the thickness of a finger also, and fix another needle in them; afterwards return it with the thread, as you did with the first needle. Therefore do not cease to do that with the needles which you need, until you complete the tightening of the entire wound.
And the needles should be of medium thickness, neither too fine nor too thick; because very fine needles pierce the flesh too quickly, and thick ones are difficult to insert into the skin; therefore they should be of medium fineness and thickness.
And it is not appropriate for the needle to be fixed too close to the two edges of the skin, lest the flesh be pierced too quickly and the wound be opened before it is healed, nor should they be drawn out too far with the suture, lest the wound be prevented from healing.
Then trim the ends of the needles so that they do not harm the patient during their sleep; and place cushions of cloth around them, which hold the needle ends; and leave them until you know that the wound has healed.
And this method of suture with needles is indeed more suitable for small wounds. Because one or two, or approximately that number of needles, are sufficient for their suture.
However, the second common suture is to gather with the suture four edges: two edges of skin, and two edges of the wound in one turn, with a needle in which the thread is wrapped equally in thinness and thickness.
Then, when you have pierced these four edges with the needle, withdraw the needle from the side from which you began, so that the thread is drawn back from the upper part of the wound; so that the suture is like the suture of cloth by which cloths are tightened, and place between each suture a quantity of thickness less than that of a little finger.
However, men know this suture.
And with this suture I healed a wound that had occurred to a man in his abdomen, who had been wounded with a knife, and the wound's rupture was greater than one palm: and approximately two palms of the middle intestine had protruded, and there was a rupture in the middle of the abdomen.
Therefore, I returned it after his intestine had remained outside the wound for twenty-four hours.
Therefore, the wound was consolidated in a space of approximately fifteen days: and I treated him, and he was healed; and he lived after that for many years, engaged in his activities.
And the physicians had judged that it would not be healed.
And it was miraculous that I did not treat it with ointment. Because I was in a place where no ointment, nor any of the medicines, could be found. Therefore, I placed old linen over the wound twice a day, and I diligently washed it with honey water, until it was healed.
I will now describe two proper types of suture, according to the narration of the text.
And it is, that you begin with a pointed suture: and you insert the needle from the outside to the inside.
When therefore the needle pierces the skin and the muscle running along the length and breadth of the entire body, leave the end of the thread on the side on which you inserted the needle, and make it penetrate to the other end from the inside to the outside at the other extremity.
When therefore you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate in this same extremity in this way from the outside to the inside; and leave the end of the thread which is on this side, and make the needle penetrate to the other end from the inside to the outside; and make it penetrate with your penetration, by which you make it penetrate in the thread end on its side, until it penetrates the whole thing.
Then begin also from this same side, and sew it with the end which is from the thread on the other side, and withdraw the needle from the skin which it pierced.
Then return it to that skin, and sew the thread end, which is on the other side, with this extremity; and pull it out from the skin that is on its side, and do it once, and another; and do it turn by turn, until you have sewn the whole exterior according to that example.
And the sum of this suture is to sew the skin of the leather workers, as they sew hides, so that you sew the thread at one time from one skin's end, and that you leave it at another time from another side, until you complete it.
The second kind of proper suture, which Galen said.
This account of his words is that you sew it according to the likeness in which some physicians sew them, so that they bring together each part to its fitting counterpart naturally, and apply the thread end to its other end, and the needle end to its other end. And that is done according to what I tell you.
You should place the needle on the end of the thread nearest you, from the outside, and have it penetrate to the inside in that area alone, and leave the two ends of the thread. Then turn the needle and have it penetrate from the outside to the inside at both ends of the thread.
Then turn both, also have both penetrate from the inside to the outside, into the two other ends of the needle, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of stitching is better than common, easy stitching, and is a stitch whose recall precedes.
This is therefore also Galen's statement.
And some said. The belly stitch is rectified with two needles. That is, insert one thread into each of them: and begin with the insertion of the needle, from where it is with you, and make it penetrate to the other side, according to the stitching of those who stitch skins equally.
And know that when there is a rupture in the middle of the belly, then its stitching is more difficult than that of the other places of the belly.
However, the judgment of the treatment of a wound is the judgment of the other wounds.
That is, when you seize a fresh wound with its blood, before air alters it, and you reduce the intestine and suture it and adapt it, then place over it the powder of the incarnating agent.
If, however, the air has already altered the wound, then apply to it certain ointments that are present to you, until they cause pus to form: and the threads fall, and the wound is consolidated, as is done with sifac and mirach. Then treat it as is the treatment of the other wounds, until it is healed.
If, however, the medicines are not present to you, then immediately, from the time it begins to emit pus, apply old cotum, and change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it is healed.
For you do not need another medication in many dispositions, when wounds are simple.
If, however, you fear that the wound will communicate to the principal members in the injury, then it is necessary that you soak soft wool in oil of moderate heat, or in rose oil, and place it around the places that are between the groin and under the armpits.
If, however, he feels pain, or pricks, or putrefaction in his intestines (for this often happens), then administer an enema to him with astringent, black, lukewarm wine; especially if putrefaction has already reached the intestine, and a penetrating exit has been made to its cavity.
And know that that which is from the large intestine is of easier healing; and that which is from the small one, of more difficult healing.
However, the intestine called the Jejunum does not receive healing from a wound that occurs in it at all; and this is because of its multitude of veins, and their size, and because of the subtlety of its body, and the proximity of its nature to the nerves.
If, however, that which proceeds from the wound is zirbum, and you obtain it fresh, reduce it according to your reduction, by which you equally reduce the intestines.
If, however, it has passed a while, and has already become green or black, then it is necessary to tie it with thread above the place that has become darkened by it, so that a flow of blood does not occur. For in zirbum are veins and arteries.
Then cut that which is beyond that ligature, and place the two ends of the thread hanging from the lower part of the wound outside it, so that the pulling of it may be alleviated upon you, and the extraction of it, in case of zirbum, and the emission of the pus of the wound.
A recollection of the wound that occurred in the Intestine.
When a rupture occurs in the Intestine, and it is small, then it is possible that it may be restored in some people; because I have seen one who was wounded in his belly with a lance blow, and the wound was from the right side of the stomach, and the wound was old; and it had become a fistula, and excretion and flatulence came out of it.
Therefore I applied treatment, until I was not confident in its healing; nor did I cease to refine it, until he was healed, and the place was consolidated.
After I saw the place already consolidated, I feared for the sick person, that a bad accident might befall the sick person in their belly; and no bad accident befell him from that. And he remained in better dispositions, eating and drinking, and copulating, and entered the bath, and was exercised in his daily routines.
And now some of the experimenters have said that when a wound occurs in the Intestine, and it is small, then it is necessary that it be tied in this manner. And it is, that you should take ants of large heads. Then bring together the two lips of the wound, and place one ant of those which have an open mouth upon the two lips of the wound. When therefore it has seized upon them, and contracts its mouth, sever its head, for it will adhere, and will not be loosened.
Then place another ant near the first, and do not cease to do this with ant after ant according to the quantity of the entire wound.
Then reduce it, and suture the wound. For those heads remain attached to the intestine, until the intestine is fed, and healed, and no harm occurs to the sick person at all.
And now it is possible that the intestine may be tied also with a fine thread: which has been scraped from the intestine of an animal, attached to it after it is inserted into the needle.
