And it is that you take the extremity of this thread from the intestine, and it is scraped a lot. Then tie to the extremity of it a fine linen thread wrapped around. Then insert that thread into the needle, and sew with it the intestine. Then reduce it into the rupture.
And this mode of suturing with ants and intestine is not except according to the way of trust and hope.
Know that a wound that is large, broad, especially if it is in one of the small intestines: then there is no remedy but subtlety, nor is its healing complete.
Concerning the treatment of Zuchemen and Fistulae.
Know that every wound, or abscess, when it becomes old and aged, and becomes an ulcer, and does not heal, and continually discharges pus, and does not close: then it is universally called a Fistula, wherever in the limb it may be, and we call it Zuchemen.
And a fistula, truly, is a filtered, hard, white knotting, with no pain, having the hollowness of a bird's feather; and for that reason some call it Risati, that is, a feather.
And in some hours it is moist, which emits pus. And perhaps the moisture is cut off in some hours. And sometimes this moisture is abundant, and sometimes scarce; and sometimes thick, and sometimes thin.
And I already stated in the division, when I spoke of wounds, that any ulcer that does not heal, and flesh does not grow in it, is not due to anything other than one of nine causes.
Of which one is, either because of a lack of blood in the body; or because of its bad quality; or because there is hard flesh between it and the edges of the ulcer, preventing the growth of flesh; or because of a large amount of dirt and filth; or because the ulcer itself is putrid, and the material applied to it is of bad quality; or because the treatment is unsuitable; or because the decay occurring in the region is a form of plague, or due to a specific property of the region, as happens in Caesaraugusta, where the digestion of illnesses is difficult, and the natural healing of abscesses is delayed; or because there is a single bone, or a number of bones, in it.
And I have already shown all these causes and their signs, and their cures with their medicines, in the division; and it is necessary here to make you know the treatment of Fistula and Zuken, which is done by surgical means with the hand.
Therefore, know that fistula occurs in all parts of the body. For among them are fistulas that reach major veins and arteries, or nerves, or sinews, or the intestine, or the bladder, or one of the ribs, or one of the vertebrae of the back, or one of the joints of the limbs, such as the hand and foot; and some reach near to the main limb and similar parts.
Therefore, whichever of these fistulas is of this kind, it is from among the illnesses difficult to heal, and it does not receive a cure, and dealing with it is labor and ignorance.
If, however, there is any fistula among them whose depth does not reach these places I have mentioned, and you hope and trust in its cure and wish to attain knowledge of it, then take a probe of air, or iron, if the fistula tends in a straight line, and explore it with that.
If, however, there is twisting in the fistula, then investigate it with a probe of fine lead. Because lead, with its gentle nature, is easily bent and follows the twist.
If the fistula has many openings, it is not possible for you to mark it with a probe. Therefore, irrigate one of its openings from among its openings.
For the moisture you irrigate it with, goes towards the other openings, and flows out of them.
Then carefully investigate by what means it is possible for you to ascertain whether there is a bone, or a nerve, and whether the fistula's cavity is distant or near; or whether it is a single fistula with many openings, and stand over all of it with your utmost strength, with your questioning, by which you question the sick person, and the discovery of pain upon compression with your hand over the area, and with similar signs and causes of accidents to the abscess.
When you have thus stood over all that location correctly, then proceed to the treatment with confidence; and it is to consider then whether the fistula is an apparent near one, or in a safe location far from a joint, or from a nerve, or artery, or vein, or one of the places I have told you; then cut the fistula as previously instructed by my account, and remove what is in it by filtration, and the corrupted flesh, that grows in its opening from the flesh, or ulcers, and the like; and treat it until it is healed.
If however the fistula is of distant depth, and is in a straight line, then it is necessary for you to cut it in depth as much as possible. Then cleanse it from all its corrupted flesh.
Then apply bandages wrapped in sharp medicaments, and insert them into the cavity of the fistula you grasped with the iron; do this with it several times, until the sharp medicine corrodes everything remaining in the cavity of the fistula from corruption.
Then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh, until it is healed.
If however it does not heal by that, then cauterize it according to what we have described.
If therefore the cause of the fistula is a bone, and this is ascertained by you, then seek it out, and cut it out according to what I have told you: if nothing prevents you from doing so, such as a vein, or nerve, or principal limb, as we said.
If therefore a bone is discovered by you, and there is something of corruption and blackness in it, then scrape it, until all its corruption is removed. Then treat it with that which causes it to flesh over, until it is healed.
If it does not heal, and emits pus as it was doing: then know, that you have not reached the complete eradication of the corruption.
Therefore, expose it again, and diligently make its scraping and cleansing with your utmost strength.
Then restore it. If therefore it heals, it is good; and if not, then know that that corruption is in a remote depth of the body, which you have not achieved. Therefore, you have no skill in it, unless you leave it to nature.
If the small bone is corrupted, and it is possible for you, draw it out with fine forceps suitable to it.
If however there are many bones, then skillfully perform the extraction of all of them, and do not leave any of them behind with your strength.
If however any of the humours oppose you, and there is no fear or error in their incision: then incise those humours and flesh, if there are any there, and free the bones.
If however you lack the preparation to remove the bones from your hour, then place something over the wound which will cause it to putrefy, and leave it until the surrounding flesh and humours putrefy around those bones.
And guard the wound so that it does not heal. And constrict the section which you have cut in your operation. Indeed, place over it, if you fear, a poultice soaked in crushed sulfur mixed with oil; or a poultice soaked in Egyptian unguent, or some other green unguents.
For when those humours putrefy, and the bones separate from them, the removal and extraction of them will be eased.
If, however, there is one large bone, such as the bone of the leg or the hip bone, and similar to them, and that which is corrupted from it is a large part: then scrape it with the utmost scraping, until that blackness and corruption is removed. Then restore the wound.
If, however, the corruption within it is a large part, and corruption has already reached the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to saw it and cut it all the way to the place where the corruption has reached: then treat it until it is consolidated.
And I narrate to you the event which happened to a man in his leg: so that you may place it as an example and aid for your operation.
There was a young man approximately thirty years of age, in whom pain arose in his leg, from a cause which originated within his body, that is, from the interior parts, until the materials reached the leg, and it became a large abscess: and there was no external cause for it.
Therefore, time passed with him and with the error of the physicians, until the abscess was opened, and many materials came together with it. And his treatment was delayed, until his leg became fistulous, and many openings were made in it: all of which emitted pus and bodily humors.
Therefore, a multitude of physicians treated him for approximately two years, and there was no one skilled in the art of the hand among them, until I was called, and I saw his leg, and the materials flowing from those openings with great force: also the entire body of the man was already decaying, and its color was yellow.
Therefore, I inserted a probe into one of the openings, and the probe reached the bone.
Then I examined all the openings: and found that they reached each other from all parts of the leg.
Therefore I hastened and cut over one of those openings, until I uncovered a portion of the bone: and I found it corrupted: which was already corroded and blackened, and decayed, and perforated, until it had penetrated to the marrow.
Therefore, I sawed what was exposed to me, and prepared it from the corrupted bone.
And I judged that there was nothing in the bone except that corruption, which I cut away and sawed. And I had already eradicated it.
Then I applied a restoration of the wound with consolidating medicines for a long time. And it was not consolidated.
Then I tried, and discovered a second layer of bone above the first discovery: and I found continuous corruption with the bone, and I sawed what appeared to me also from that corruption.
Then I attempted its restoration. And it was not restored.
Therefore I again discovered upon it, and I did not cease to cut the bone piece by piece, and to attempt its restoration: and it was not restored, until I cut about one palm's breadth from the bone, and extracted it with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicines, and it was quickly consolidated and healed: and this repetition in its operation, and its section, was not necessary except for the disposition of the patient's weakness, and his scarcity of tolerance, and my fear for him concerning death; because it was happening to him at all hours from the excess of evacuations, evil syncopes.
Therefore, he was healed with complete healing: and hard flesh grew in the place of the bone, and a good disposition was made in his body, and his strength returned: and he was restored to his usual disposition: and no harm was caused him in walking at all.
If however a projecting bone in a part of the body is already fractured, then it is necessary to saw it in this manner: and it is as follows: that you take a ligament and tie it to the end of the protruding bone: and instruct someone to pull it upward: and put another thicker wool ligament on the first ligament. Then tie it above the flesh that is under the bone: and pull its end to draw the flesh downwards: and you uncover the flesh above the place you wish to saw, so that the saw does not injure the flesh.
Place wood or a board beneath the bone properly below: for when you do this, it does not prevent the sawing of corrupt flesh.
And it is necessary that the sawing be over the corrupted place for a short time, so that there is no corruption in the concavity of the bone: and it does not appear in its appearance to the senses, and compels a sawing of it in another way.
If the bone is corrupted, and is not protruding, but rather its part is continuous with another part, and the corruption is in its middle or on a part of it, then uncover the flesh from all sides completely. Then place wood below.
Afterwards saw it from the first part, where the corruption is, until the sawing continues from the other part: and let the sawing be along the length from the corruption for a short time, as we have said.
If the corruption is in the joint, cut the corrupted joint itself, and saw the bone where it continues from the other part.
If however the corruption is in the continuation of two joints, then there is no ingenuity in it except abrasion.
If the corruption is in the finger of the hand, or in the toe of the foot: then the matter is very difficult in it. But it is necessary that you inquire how the corruption appears to you. And you should abrade it and cleanse it according to whatever arrangement is possible for you, and with whatever ingenuity it is rectified for you, when it does not cause you opposition from the vein or nerve.
And know that there are many knives and saws for cutting these bones, according to the position of the bones, and their preparation, and their thickness and thinness, and their size and smallness, and their hardness and their rarity. Therefore, it is necessary to prepare an instrument suitable for that operation for every type of operation.
And know that these operations show you the type of instrument you need when you have long study with you. And the knowledge of the methods of this art is a testament to the methods of illnesses: for then you will already find within yourself what is suitable for it from the instruments for every illness.
And I am setting forth for you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments, which you will set as examples on which you will cut, and the test by which you will test others.

Of the incision of the extremities, and the sawing of the bones.
The extremities putrefy, either from an external cause, or an internal one.
Therefore, when you have treated that corruption with medicinal cures, and the treatment does not help, and you see the corruption affecting a limb, which nothing repels, then it is necessary that you cut that limb until you reach the corruption, so that it escapes from death or from the greater harm that is from the limb's deficiency.
And a sign that this happens to it is that the limb darkens until it stinks; that fire has burned it, or that it putrefies after the blackness; until that rot is cut away down to what follows that limb, and spreads to the whole body.
Therefore, hurry with its incision, and likewise if the cause of the corruption is from the sting of certain venomous creatures, such as the sea scorpion, and the viper, and the adder, and similar ones.
If, therefore, the corruption, or the wound, is in the extremity of a finger, then cut off the finger, and do not give space to the corruption to spread to the rest of the hand.
And similarly, if it happens in the hand, then cut it off from the root of the forearm, and do not give space to the corruption until it reaches the upper arm.
And if it invades it, then cut off the arm at the elbow at its joint.
If, however, the corruption passes, and you see it around the elbow, then in that is the death of the infirm person. And to administer beyond that, in terms of treatment, is the measure of skill.
And similarly do with the foot, when corruption invades the toe, then cut it off at one of the joints; and if it reaches the sole of the foot, then cut off the whole foot. If, however, it ascends to the knee, then cut off the leg at the knee joint.
If, however, the corruption has already reached above the knee, then there is no skill in it, except for releasing it, and leaving the sick person to death.
And the way of cutting or sawing the limb is that you should tighten the ligament below the one you wish to cut, and tighten another ligament above the place. And let the assistant extend one ligament downwards, and let the assistant extend another ligament upwards. And you should separate the flesh that is between the two ligaments, with a broad spatula, until all the flesh is exposed. Then it is cut or sawn.
And it is necessary that you place linen cloths on all sides, and that the saw does not touch a healthy area; and let an additional injury and a hot abscess befall the sick person.
If, however, an internal bleeding occurs during your work, then cauterize the spot quickly, or place some powders that stop the bleeding upon it. Then return to your treatment, until it is completed.
Then bind the wounded limb with a suitable bandage, and treat it until it heals.
And I tell you that it happened to a man in his foot, the same happening that I tell you.
That is because it happened in his foot with a blackness, like a burning that is similar to the burning of fire: and that corruption first happened in his toe, until it began the whole foot.
Therefore, the man hastened from himself, after he saw the corruption fall upon the limb with its violence, which he found from its pain and burning: and it fell upon it at the joint, and he was healed.
And after a long time passed, the same corruption happened to him in the index finger of his hand.
So he came to me, and I tried to recover that superfluity with that which I placed on his hand from medicines after the cleansing of his body: and the superfluity was not repelled, and it began to proceed to another finger: until the corruption began in his hand.
Therefore, he called me to cut his hand: and I did not want that, hoping from me on the repulsion of that superfluity: and I also feared for him at the incision of his hand death. Because the man's strength was over the fall.
Therefore, after he despaired of me, he returned to his own land: and it came to me about him, that he hurried, and cut his whole hand: and he was healed.
And I have not brought forth this account, unless that it be a help concerning that which falls from the kind of this illness: and that it be a signification by which it may be signified, and according to which it may be done.
Concerning the treatment of Sinuses, and the quality of enemas with medicines.
When an abscess occurs in some fleshy members, and the space of the abscess is prolonged until it forms a collection of pus: then it bursts, or is perforated, and that which is in it of pus goes out, and a hollow place remains as if it were a vessel: and the skin which is above it is like a cloth which has already become thin: and it does not reach its final stage of corruption, nor does it impinge on the bone, nor on the nerve, nor on the ligament: it deserves to be called a Sinus: and it is not called a Fistula: however, if its space is prolonged until the corruption impinges upon any of these members, then it is called a Fistula, or zuken.
And its treatment is to cut that entire skin as it is rolled back; and especially when it has become thin, and is like a cloth; and you are confident that it does not adhere to the place, due to its corruption.
If however you hope that the skin adheres, and that corruption does not result from that final adherence; and if there is thickness of flesh within the skin, then treat it with an enema; and it is necessary to consider the Sinus.
Then, if it is large, and the pus that is emitted from it has a foul odor: then irrigate it with an enema containing Egyptian medicine, which is vinegar, oil, and ziniar, taking equal parts, mixed in a vessel; and boil them over fire until the medicine turns reddish; and it comes to the consistency of honey.
Then take from that what you need; and dissolve it with water and honey, and irrigate the Sinus with it; and tighten its opening, and leave the medicine in it for a period of two hours.
Then extract it with pressure; do this for so many days, until the sinus is cleansed and the foul odor is removed.
And sometimes it is irrigated with ash water, when this medicine is not available to you; and it is necessary to take the ash of vine wood, or the ash from holly wood; and pour water over it. Then strain it, and irrigate with it, until you are confident that the sinus is now cleansed.
If the patient cannot tolerate the Egyptian medicine, nor ash water, then irrigate it with water and honey, to which a bit of pounded ziniar has been mixed. Or irrigate it with honey and wine mixed together, because the nature of honey is to cleanse and wash away; and wine makes the sinus adhere, especially when there is an excess of astringency and dryness in the wine.
When you have done this several times, and are confident that the corruption of the sinus is now removed, then irrigate it with that which causes flesh to grow there.
And you should take a piece of the dyafinicon plaster, and dissolve it with rose oil and astringent wine; or irrigate it with certain medicines and other ointments, whose properties I have described in my treatise on ointments.
If the opening of the sinus is narrow, so that the clyster tube cannot enter: then widen it a little with an iron instrument; or place in it a linen cloth wrapped in Egyptian ointment, or in Basil ointment, until it widens.
And similarly, if the opening is too wide, then bring together its lips with sutures, and leave it the size into which the clyster can enter without additions.
And similarly, if the opening from which pus flows is elevated upward, then cut it in the lower part of it, so that the pus flows downward; because when pus is constricted in the depth of the sinus, it prevents flesh from growing there.
If you cannot cut the sinus approximately below the desired level, then try to prepare the limb in a manner such that pus flows from it easily, as preparation dictates. Try to completely prevent any pus from remaining there, and take consolidating medicines that are placed on the mark, suitable for what you desire. And spread them over a linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such a size that it covers the entire sinus. Then, with scissors, cut a hole in the direction of the sinus opening, slightly larger than the sinus opening. Then, spread some mild ointments onto another cloth, according to the size of the hole; and place it over the area, positioned some distance from the sinus opening, so that the pus exiting from it may be eased; and do not remove the large cloth, unless after some days, once.
However, the one that is over the sinus opening, this is the one you remove every hour, so that what gathers in it of pus may exit; and so that you may know whether that which runs from it of matter, is much, or little; or digested, or undigested.
And with this, again consider the location of the sinus itself, whether its owner feels pain there, or there is no pain, and whether there is an abscess there, or there is no abscess there.
Therefore, when your treatment extends thus, and you see the matter exiting from above the sinus opening, then know that the sinus is now adhering, or approaching adhesion; then, therefore, loosen it in the morning, or on the third day; and when you loosen the wound, then exchange the cloth and renew the ointment.
However, if thin matter exits from the sinus after a long time, then do not despair of its adhesion, for its healing is approaching, because there are men who delay the generation of flesh in their wounds, and there are those who act contrary to that.
If, however, undigested pus exits from the sinus after many days, then know that the sinus does not adhere.
If, therefore, the adhesion of the sinus is delayed and its condition prolonged, then add to the drying of the medicines, but let the physicians ensure that the medicines are moist in their substance and dry in their virtue, such as the dyafinicon ointment, when superfluity of addition from zegi is added to it.
And from the most recent way of treating it is to take the dyafinicon ointment and dissolve it with rose oil, and sprinkle upon it old wine equal in its substance.
Then mix it with that, and use it.
And it performs a similar action to this, when dyafinicon ointment is not present for you, honey, when you boil it until it thickens, and use it. Or take myrrh, and aloe, and frankincense: grind the whole, and powder it over honey while it is over the fire. Then line a cloth over it, and bind it over the sinus; and line with the honey that you boiled until it thickened; and powder spices over it, and place it over the cloth, and bind it.
And sometimes long aristolochia, and roots of the heavenly lily, and the lily itself, and the herb's flour, and centaury are administered in it. Use them, simple or aggregated, grind them, and sieve them: and powder them over the honey that I mentioned, and use it.
If the sinus's condition is prolonged and it is not cured by this treatment, then know that there is corruption deep within it; or it is already making an impression on the bones, or on other hard members, or ligaments; then treat it with the treatment of fistulas, according to what has been said before.
On the treatment of whitlow and a worn nail, and the incision of a swollen finger, and the section.
Whitlow is an abscess that arises under the nail of the thumb of the hand, or foot, and perhaps arises in the other fingers.
When, therefore, its condition is prolonged and its treatment is neglected, and it becomes an abscess with a hot abscess, or it becomes corrupted and emits pus until it eats away the root of the nail; and perhaps it corrupts the whole of it, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone, until it has a foul odor; and the end of the finger becomes wide, and its color becomes dark.
Therefore, when you treat it, according to what we have said in the division, and your treatment does not help, then it is necessary to cut away with an iron all the excess that remains of the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. For the cautery is very helpful.
If however the bone is healthy, and the nail is also healthy, and the projections of the nail are now pressing on the flesh internally, and beginning to prick it and injure it, then it is necessary to place a fine probe under the angle of the nail that pricks the flesh, and push it upward; and cut the flesh easily, and place on what remains of the flesh burning, corrosive medicines until the whole of it is removed. Then treat it with ointments until it is healed.
If however decay has already taken hold in the bone, then it is necessary to cut away that bone and extract it; for it will never heal while the corrupted bone remains in it.
If you see corrosion and decay spreading into the finger, then cut it at one of the joints, according to the memory of its description that has been given. Then treat it until it is healed.
If, however, the nail suffers abrasion or impact, and intense pain occurs in it, then it is necessary to first bleed the afflicted person. Then cut the nail with a sharp spatule in a transverse section from above, in view.
And take care that the cut does not reach the flesh beneath the nail, for you will cause intense pain to the afflicted person through that, and it will be a cause for the birth of added flesh in the place.
Then treat the place with that which calms the pains.
Indeed, the extra finger that is born in some people's hands is perhaps entirely fleshy; and perhaps some of them have bone, and perhaps a nail is in it; and some of them are born at the root of the joint, or its birth is in some of the nodes of the finger. Therefore, the one that is born in some nodes of the finger does not move; and the one that is born near the joint of the finger, perhaps moves.
Therefore, the excision of the one that is fleshy among them is easy. That is, cut it at its root with a broad spatula.
However, the treatment of the one whose origin is in the root of the joint is difficult. Therefore, abstain from its incision.
In that one, however, which is born on the finger at the last joint, then it is necessary to cut first its flesh with a round incision, down to the bone. Then saw the bone with one of those saws suited to it. Then treat it until it is healed.
And the consolidation which happens to fingers together, happens many times either from the birth of man, that is, is born with him, or from the consolidation of a wound, or burning by fire, and similar things.
Therefore, it is necessary to separate that consolidation until the fingers return to their natural form. Then place between them bandages, or cloths soaked in rose oil, so that they do not consolidate quickly, and separate between them, or place between them a thin sheet of lead until the place is consolidated as it should be.
And similarly if consolidation happens between some fingers and the palm, then cut that consolidation, according to what is prepared and suitable for the shape of the limb.
About the incision of a defect.
Ulcers are convoluted, large veins, full of melancholic superfluities which occur in many parts of the body, and their occurrence is most frequent in the legs, especially in the legs of those who travel and work, and those who carry burdens.
Therefore, it is necessary that you administer a shaking of the body from the black bile occasionally with a strong shaking.
Then, let the patient be bled from the basilic vein.
However, their treatment with iron is according to two methods. One of them is that they be cut, and the black blood be extracted. And the other method is that the vein be lifted, and the whole [blood] be extracted.
However, the incision of it is according to this method. First, soak the leg in much hot water until the thick, foul blood is dissolved. Then bind the sick person's leg from the upper part of the thigh, up to the lower part of the knee, with a bandage. Then cut the vein in one, or two, or three places with a wide incision.
Then raise, that is, draw the blood in your hand from the lower part of the leg, up to the top, and from the top, the phlebotomist should first treat the sick person. Then cut the nail downwards, until an amount of blood sufficient and tolerated by the sick person's strength comes out.
Then tie it off; and tell him to abstain from foods that generate black bile; and repeat the evacuation and phlebotomy when the veins are filled, and that harms the sick person.
However, its elevation is according to this method. Shave the leg of the sick person, if there are many hairs on it. Then immerse him in a bath; or apply a warm water compress to his leg until it reddens, and the veins bulge; or, if a bath is not available, have him exercise vigorously until the limb warms up.
Then cut the skin in a straight line along the vein: either at its end near the knee, or at its lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with hooks, and strip the vein from all sides until it becomes visible; and at the point of its appearance, you will see it turning from red to a blackish hue. When it therefore emerges from the skin, you see it white, like a string.
Then insert a probe under it until, when raised, it emerges from the skin: suspend it with a blunt, gentle hook.
Then make another incision close to that incision, by three fingers' breadth. Then strip the skin from above the vein until it appears. Then raise it with the probes as you did, and suspend it with another hook, as you did at first.
Then make another incision, or several incisions, if needed. Then lift it, and cut it itself at the last incision near the heel.
Then pull it out to the incision that is above it.
And do that until you pull it out from the incision that is higher, or above all the incisions, until when the whole of it emerges: then cut it.
However, if it does not obey you in pulling and lifting it, then insert a needle with strong thread doubled, and tie it, and pull it out; or insert a rod under it; and revolve your hand to every part until it emerges. And beware, lest it be cut. For if it is cut, it becomes difficult for you to lift it very much, and it enters upon the infirm from it a great harm.
When therefore you have pulled it out entirely, then put on the place of the wound wool soaked in wine and rose oil, or oil; and treat it until it is healed.
If the vine is contracted, having a twisting to which there is an involution to parts, and it is not as we said according to straightness, then it is necessary that you cut over it to every part of its twisting, and the places of its involution.
Then suspend it with hooks, until you raise it entirely; and beware at your cutting over it lest you strike a vein or wound it, for it will become difficult for you to raise or pull it out. Therefore preserve your prudence.
Form of the Incident by which the Vine is healed.
Form of the Blind Hook.
It does not have a curvature like the other hooks, nor a sharp tip so as not to wound a vein; and it is of a thick, flexible form, smooth. For if it were slender, it would cut a vein by its thinness; indeed, its thickness should be excessive, as we have said.
Of the extraction of the vein of the leg.
This vein is generated in the legs in hot countries, like Al-Andalus and the land of the Arabs, and in arid, hot lands, few in tenderness, that is, in which neither grass nor trees grow.
And perhaps it is generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
And indeed its generation is from the putrefaction that occurs under the skin, as happens within the bodies of snakes and worms, and worms generated between the skin and flesh.
And a sign of the beginning of the outcome of this Vein is that a violent inflammation occurs in the leg. Then the place becomes blistered. Then the Vein begins to emerge from the place of that blistering, as if it were the root of a plant or animal.
Therefore, when its extremity appears from it, then it is necessary that you wrap a piece of lead around it, whose weight is from one drachma keil, up to two drachmas, and tie it; and leave the lead hanging from the leg.
Whenever therefore something emerges from it to the exterior, wrap that in lead, and tie it. If it extends too far, then cut off a part of it, and wrap the remainder. And do not cut it off at the root before the whole of it emerges. Because if you cut it, it contracts and enters the flesh, and causes an abscess and putrefaction in the area, and a bad ulcer.
Therefore it is necessary that it be gently treated and pulled out gradually, until it emerges from its entirety, and nothing of it remains in the body; and from this vein, in some men, there emerges something that is in length five palms and ten. And it has already come to my attention that it emerged from a man twenty palms in length.
If however it is cut during your time of treatment, then introduce a probe into its opening, and pierce it with a long probe, with the body, until all that is within it of material is evacuated; and treat the putrefaction of the area with medicines for several days. Then treat it with the treatment of abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the joint of the foot, or in the foot itself; and many openings occur in it, and a branch emerges from each opening.
Therefore, treat it as we have stated in the division, and with what has been previously mentioned.
Concerning the incision on worms generated under the skin, and it is called the Disease of cattle.
This disease is called in a certain land among us "the disease of cattle," because it frequently occurs in cattle, and it is a small single worm which is born between the skin and the flesh.
And it moves throughout the body, ascending and descending, and its passage is apparent to the senses from limb to limb, until it breaks through wherever it breaks through the skin, and exits.
And its generation is from the putrefaction of certain humors. Just as worms and snakes, that is, intestinal worms, and ascarides occur in the belly.
And it is feared because of its malice. Because when it travels within the body, and rises to the head, and reaches a place, then perhaps it bursts open there and exits, and destroys the eye, and this happens many times.
Therefore, when one wishes to cure it and extract it, this is not done except by its movement and its appearance to the senses.
Therefore, it is necessary to tie that which is above it and that which is below it with a strong constricting bandage. Then cut above it, and extract it.
If it has penetrated into the flesh, and you do not find it, then place a cautery with fire upon the spot until it dies.
And the greater fear is its corruption, by which it corrupts the eye, as we said.
If however you see it having already reached the head near the eye, then bind under it the eyebrow with a good ligature. Then cut above it, and extract it.
And it is necessary that the sick person use cleansing of their body, with medicines that dissolve bad putrid humors.
And let him beware of foods that generate putrefaction.
Concerning the section upon the illness Nakir.
This illness is called Nakir in our land: and it is a pain that happens in some limbs, then changes from limb to limb.
And I myself have already seen it, as I tell you. I was called to a sick woman in some villages. And she exposed her arm, and I saw a small swelling in the vein of the arm's tendon.
And after remaining for one hour, I saw one swelling move with a quiver, as a worm moves, ascending towards her shoulder more swiftly than it could be, like quicksilver running from place to place.
Therefore the pain receded from that place, and settled in the shoulder.
Then I sat for one hour, and it ran to the rest of the body, until it reached the other arm.
Then she told me that it moved around her entire body, as I had seen.
Therefore I was amazed at the speed of its shifting from limb to limb. And I myself had not previously seen this illness with my own eyes in such a manner. However, I have seen many who find the pain shifting from limb to limb, but I did not see it with my own eyes, as I saw it in the woman.
And I do not think it is anything other than that because the woman was of those whose bodies are dry and whose veins are detected; hence, this windy movement appears to the senses.
And it is necessary that they not appear in this manner in those who are delicate and have moist bodies, with hidden veins.
When, therefore, you wish to treat it, and the patient feels that pain: then if it appears to you with the eye, as we said, then tighten above it and below it quickly; and cut above it until that compressed windiness comes out; and cauterize the place.
If, however, you do not see it with your eyes, then treat it with shaking the body and with what expels winds, and dispel them with foul pills and pills of seraphin, and similar ones.
On the extraction of Arrows.
Arrows are diversified according to their types and according to the places in which they strike the body.
Their diversity is such that some have large points, and small ones, and concave ones, and blunt ones. And some of them have three angles. And some of them have tongues. And some of them have stars.
Those that are according to the members on which they fall are according to two ways. For either the members are from concave principal members, such as the brain, and heart, and liver, and lung, and kidneys, and intestines, and bladder, and similar things. Therefore, when it falls into any of these members, and the signs of death appear to you, which I will tell you later, then you should avoid extracting that arrow from them, for death follows it in the one in whom it is, in many dispositions. And when these bad signs do not appear to you, and the arrow is not hidden deep within the member, then extract it and treat the wound.
For the signs of the brain, when an arrow falls into it, and pierces the bone, and emerges through the covering over the brain, are: since a fierce headache, and scotoma, and dizziness, and redness in the eyes, and swelling, and redness of the tongue, and spasm, and confusion of intellect, and vomiting of cholera occur from it. And perhaps blood emerges from the nostrils or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off, and the voice is destroyed. And white moisture like gruel emerges from the site of the wound. And from it emerges something like the watery substance of flesh.
If therefore these signs appear to you, then abstain from treatment, and remove the arrow if it does not emerge.
The signs of an arrow striking the heart are these: that it is near the left nipple; and that one feels as if it were fixed in a hard substance, not a hollow one. And perhaps the arrow's movement is similar to the pulse's movement: and black blood flows from the wound. And that is followed by coldness of the extremities, and cold sweat, and fainting. Know therefore, that death descends undoubtedly.
And the sign of an arrow wounding the lung is the exit of frothy blood from the wound: and the vessels following the neck fester, and the patient's complexion changes. And he exhales a large, deep breath: and seeks the attraction of cold air.
If the arrow strikes the covering, which is in the chest: then it is near the small ribs: and there is a great gasp with intense pain, with sighing: and all the limbs of the shoulders move.
And if the arrow strikes the stomach, then perhaps some undigested food will come forth from the wound.
And if the arrow strikes the abdomen and is fixed in it, something of excrement, or of zirbum, or of an already ruptured intestine, will issue forth from the wound. Therefore, there is no hope in its treatment, nor in extracting the arrow.
If the arrow strikes the bladder, and urine comes out, and something proceeds from it to the exterior, and the pain is stronger than the weakness: then know that it is mortal.
However, the remaining limbs, such as the face, neck, throat, shoulder blade, the arm, the vertebrae of the back, the collarbone, the hip, the leg, and similar limbs are preserved according to various conditions, when the arrow does not strike an artery or a nerve and the arrow is not poisonous.
And I tell you what I have seen of these arrows, so that you may indicate by that your treatment.
Because an arrow struck a man in the tear duct of his eye, at the root of his nose. Therefore, I extracted it from the other side, under the fat of his ear, and he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from a Jew, which had struck him in the fat of his eye beneath the lower eyelid; and the arrow was already hidden, and I only recovered a small tip of it, with which it adhered to its shaft; and it was a large arrow from the bows of the Turks, with soft iron, in which there are no ears.
Therefore, the Jew was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And it was an Arabic arrow, and it is of the kind which has ears. Therefore, I cut above it between the two guides, and it was already submerged in his throat. Therefore, I worked with it delicately until I extracted it, and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from the man which had fallen into his belly, and we thought that he would die from it.
After thirty days had passed, and nothing had changed in his condition, I cut above the arrow and worked on it, and extracted it; and he was healed, and no evil happened to him.
And I saw a man on whom an arrow had fallen on his back, and the wound was consolidated over it. After seven years, therefore, an arrow emerged in the root of his thigh.
And I saw a woman on whom an arrow fell into her belly, and the wound was consolidated over it, and the arrow remained within her; and no change was made in any of her conditions, and the woman found no harm in any of her natural functions.
And I saw another man, on whose face an arrow had fallen; and the wound was consolidated, and it remained, causing him not much pain; and many similar things to these.
And I extracted an arrow from the king's chief men, which had fallen into the middle of his nose, and had already slightly deviated to the right side; and the arrow was entirely hidden.
Therefore, I was called to treat him after the arrow's impact for up to three days. Therefore, I found the arrow wound to be very tight.
Therefore, I sought it with a fine probe, and did not feel it; and indeed he found a prick and pain beneath his right ear. Therefore, I hoped that this prick was from the arrow's tip.
Therefore, I plastered the area with a plaster having attracting and digestive virtue, confident that the area would form an abscess, and that a sign of the arrow would appear for me to cut upon it; and it did not happen that an incident signifying the arrow having reached the area occurred in the place.
Therefore, I applied the plaster to it for many days; and no incident occurred, and the wound meanwhile healed; and he remained weak from its extraction for a space of days, until he felt the arrow one day within his nose. And he told me that.
And he told me that. Therefore, I applied a sharp, corrosive medicine to that wound for many days, until it opened; and when I probed it, I felt a slender arrowhead adhering to the wood.
Then I added to the opening of the wound through that sharp medicine, until the arrowhead appeared to us visibly; and about four months of time passed with him.
Then, after the wound had enlarged, and I was able to insert forceps into it and move it, and it did not yield to removal, I did not cease to refine and devise ways upon it until, one day, I began to use carefully crafted forceps, in the manner that their form will appear at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And indeed, the physicians judge that the cartilage of the nose is not restored. Therefore, I restored it, and the wound consolidated, and the weak man was healed with complete health; and nothing at all harmed him.
And I relate to you the quality of extracting certain arrows, so that you may place it as a rule and meaning upon that which I did not say.
Since the parts of this art and their distinctions are not encompassed by the narrative, nor does that book encompass it, and a skillful craftsman compares the little with the much, and the present with the absent; and finds a new operation and a new instrument from the descent of foreign matters of this art when they occur.
Therefore, I say that arrows do not emerge from the members to which they are affixed, except in two ways. Or by traction from the place from which it entered. Or by the contrary of the other part.
And that which emerges from whence it entered is either an arrow proceeding in a fleshy place: and it is pulled, and emerges.
If it does not yield to emergence at the hour in which it strikes, then it is necessary that you leave it for several days, until the flesh that surrounds it rots: and its extraction will be eased, and its emergence.
And similarly, if it is fixed in the bone, and does not yield to you for emergence, then leave it also for several days: and repeatedly apply extraction and movement every day, until it emerges.
If it does not yield to you for emergence after days, then it is necessary to pierce around the arrow in the bone itself from every side with a fine drill, until the arrow's [passage] is widened. Then pull it, and extract it.
If, however, the arrow is already hidden in a place of the body, and is concealed and hidden from sight, then seek it with probes. If, however, you feel it, then extract it with certain instruments suitable for its removal.
If, however, you cannot remove it, because of the constriction of the wound, and because of the arrow's length deep within; and there is no bone, nerve, or vein there, then enlarge the opening around it until the wound is widened and it is possible to remove the arrow.
If it has ears by which it is held, then free the fixed flesh in them from every side, by every means you can, and devise ways, if it is unable to escape, if the flesh is broken in the ears' rupture and twisting, until it escapes.
And when you are performing the extraction of the arrow, wherever it is, then use the twisting of your hand, with forceps on all sides, until you make it escape; and use the utmost delicacy, so that the arrow is not broken, and its removal becomes difficult for you.
If you cannot remove it within an hour, then leave it for a few days, until what is around it has begun to decay. Then try again: for it will be easier then. But if a flow of blood opposes you, then administer what we have said in the cure in its chapter; and protect your skill from incision of a vein, or nerve, or tendon.
And employ every means possible for the arrow's escape; and let it be with ease and calmness, or slowness and firmness, as I have told you.
And it is necessary, when you pull out the arrow, that you do so according to the way it was, according to the position of the arrow's penetration. Because it is more helpful. If however it is not possible for you, then use what you can from the figures.
However, if an arrow emerges from the opposite side, either something is already protruding outward, or the arrowhead's tip forms a bump perceptible on the skin's surface above, and you see it protruding; therefore, cut above it, and let the incision be according to the amount that the forceps can grasp.
Then pull it out: for it will be easy to remove.
If however it remains in the bone, then turn your hand in a circular motion until the arrow is embedded in the bone and enlarges itself, then pull it out.
And if not, leave it for a few days, then try again until it comes out.
If the arrow's shaft is in it, then push it out with it.
If however the shaft has fallen away, and you wish to administer a push, then insert a concave instrument so that its concavity enters the arrow's tail; then push it out with it.
If however the arrow is concave, then push it out with an instrument entering it. For the arrow is alleviated by it.
If the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary to cauterize all the flesh in which the poison is, if it is possible for you. Then treat it with that which is appropriate for it.
If the arrow is lodged in the breast, or in the belly, or in the bladder, or in the side; and it is near to that which you touch with the probe, and it is possible for you to cut above it; then cut, and beware of cutting a vein or nerve, and extract it.
Then suture the wound, if you see fit, and treat it until it is healed.
And this is the form of the forceps with which arrows are extracted.
Let the extremities be similar to the beak of a bird, made of iron; when they grasp the arrow, they do not release it.
And many kinds of them are made, large, and small, and medium, according to the size and smallness of the arrow, and the size and constriction of the wound.
The form of the concave driver.
And the form of the blunt driver.
This is a blunt extremity like a ray, so that its entry into the concave arrow may be eased, and it may be propelled with it.
Concerning the phlebotomy of veins.
The veins to be opened, whose use runs through the body, are thirty.
Of these, sixteen are in the head: two pulsating veins, which are behind the ears, called the two Herbs; and two arteries, which appear in the two temples; and two veins which are in the two tear ducts of the two eyes, called the Looking-Ones; and a prepared vein in the middle of the forehead; and a vein that is at the extremity of the nose. And two Guidegi, which are in the neck. And two veins that are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins that are in the lower lip. And these are the veins called Algecherich. And two veins that are under the tongue.
The veins, however, that are opened by phlebotomy in the arms, are five veins in each arm.
One of them is the Cephalica, and it is from the wild side; and the common people call it the vein of the head. And Fusca. And it is the Middle vein, and it is composed from a branch of the Basilica, and a branch of the Cephalica; and the common people call it the vein of the body. And Basilica, and it is located on the domestic side; and it is also called Titillicaris. And the common people call it the vein of the belly. And the Cord of the arm, and it is located above the fold; and it is that in which phlebotomy is performed, and it is that which appears above the thumb with clear appearance.
And Saluatella: and it is the vein that is between the ring finger and the little finger; and it has two branches.
And in the leg and foot are three veins. One of them is that which is under the curve of the thumb on the wild side. And the second is Saphena; and its location is at the heel on the domestic side. And the vein Nesse; and its location is at the heel on the wild side.
And in the other leg are three veins similar to those.
Concerning the phlebotomy of two veins behind the ears.
Phlebotomies, however, of the two veins which are behind the ears, are a help for old catarrhs, and hemiplegia, and sahafati, and bad old ulcers of the head.
And the quality of phlebotomizing it is according to what I relate. And it is, that the head of the sick person be shaved, and his occiput be rubbed in the place of the two veins with a rough cloth by a good rubbing. Then let the sick person tighten his neck with a strip, until the two veins appear. And their locations are behind the ears in two depressed places of the head. Therefore, seek them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, mark that place with ink.
Then take a phlebotomous knife, and it is the one called Incidens. Then insert it beneath the vein in the skin, until the phlebotomous reaches the mouth. Then lift your hand with the vein and skin upward, and cut the vein with the skin with an incident incision. And let the length of the incision be the amount of two joined fingers, or approximately that: and cause as much blood to issue forth as you wish.
Then bind it with bandages, and leave it, until it heals.
And already in the beginning of the book the incision of those [things] and their cauterization preceded.
The phlebotomy of the two arteries located in the two temples is helpful for ancient hemiplegia, and crookedness of the face, and persistent ophthalmia, and the flow of excessive acute secretions to the eyes.
And the quality of phlebotomizing them is according to what I tell you. Let the infirm person draw their neck very much, until two veins appear clearly to the senses, and their pulse is demonstrated under your finger. Then mark the signs with ink.
Then raise the skin above the vein upwards with your index finger, and insert the incisioning lancet underneath, and raise the vein upwards. And cut it, as you did in the two other veins: and cause the blood to run according to your necessary quantity.
Then loosen the infirm person's constriction, and place your finger upon the vein for one hour. Afterwards, place upon it a cloth and cushions, and bind it tightly above the constriction: and leave it, until it heals.
And already the recollection of them, and their incision, and their extraction have preceded in the beginning of the book.
Indeed, the benefit of phlebotomy of the forehead vein is after phlebotomy of the Cephalic vein, for the old ailments of the face: and for migraine, and redness: and foul ulcers.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is, according to what I tell you. The infirm patient ties his neck with a strip, until the vein appears. Then take the instrument called Fossorium.
Place the prominent point that is on the head of the Fossorium above the vein itself, and strike upon it with a comb, or with something else in its manner: and let the blood run according to the quantity you wish. Then loosen the perforation of the infirm person, and bind it until it is healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a wide phlebotome. However, it should not be a phlebotome with a sharp end, like the other phlebotomes, but rather one with a broad end. And phlebotomize it across, since the bone is near; and perhaps the phlebotome will break in it when it is thin.
The relief of the two veins located in the two tear ducts of the two eyes is for eye scabies and redness, and a scab in the eye, and for ailments of the face.
And the quality of their phlebotomy is such that the weak one tightens his neck with a bandage. Then phlebotomize them, and you stand over his head.
And let the phlebotomy be made across, a little lengthwise, with a small, slightly broad phlebotom, for there is no flesh in the place. For if the phlebotom is of a slender extremity, perhaps it breaks.
Then make the blood run out as much as you need. And place a compress on them, and bind them for one night, and loosen them.
Phlebotomy of the veins of the nose is beneficial for acute fevers, and violent headaches, and ailments of the face, such as hemicrania, and redness that occurs on the face, especially when it is chronic.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that the weak man tightens his neck. Then hold his nose with your left hand, and take a slender, long lancet, and insert it into the middle of his nostril, between that which divides the nose according to its straightness. Because the vein is not apparent to the senses there, and because blood begins to flow immediately.
And you should hold your hand with the lancet for a short time, and make blood flow according to your need. Then tie it with a bandage. For it quickly recovers.
Indeed, the benefit of Guidegi's phlebotomy is for the constriction of breathing, and the inception of leprosy, and melancholic illnesses, and impetigo and evil ulcers, and corroding ones, that is, burning herpes.
And the quality of their phlebotomy is such that the weak man holds it tight under both sides of his neck with a bandage: and the craftsman stands over the patient's head, and the patient sits on a seat.
Then the phlebotomist opens the vein to the length of the wide phlebotomy for a short time. Then draw out a moderate quantity of blood, or according to what you see as necessary for it. Then do likewise with the other vein.
Afterwards, loosen the bandage, and tie the two veins with a middle binding, so that the patient is not choked. And leave it until morning. For the wound is healed.
Concerning the phlebotomy of the veins of Algeberich. The veins of Algeberich are helpful in phlebotomy, because they contribute to the prevention of sores in the mouth, and the corruption of the gums: and bad ulcers: and fissures of the lips, and bad ulcers, even those that are in the nose, and around it.
And the quality of phlebotomy of them is, that you should make the sick person sit before you: and bind their neck with a strip. Then roll back their lip, and look at the two veins: of which one is on the right side of the lip, and the second on its left: and the indication of each is through the blackness of both. And that is true, because around them are small, black veins. Therefore, cut each with a cut made incidently.
However, if there is doubt upon you, and you do not know which of them it is: then aim for the incision of the larger of them, and the more manifest of them.
And similarly do with the two veins which are in the upper lip: and most certainly custom runs with it: and cut the two veins which are in the lower lip.
Of phlebotomy of the vein under the tongue after the phlebotomy of the cephalic vein. However, the benefit of the phlebotomy of the two veins that are under the tongue is that the phlebotomy of the cephalic vein is for the suffocations which are in the throat, and the sickness of the uvula, and the sicknesses of the mouth.
And the quality of phlebotomizing them is such that the sick person sits between your hands in the presence of the sun; and raise his tongue, and look under his tongue on one side of it for a vein, and on the other side of it for a vein; and the colour of each should slope towards blackness.
Therefore, phlebotomize each one; and beware that you do not injure your hand when phlebotomizing them. For beneath it are arteries, and perhaps a flow of blood will occur from those arteries.
The three veins, however, that are phlebotomized in the elbow, are those in whose phlebotomy custom has run in all men.
And their phlebotomy is done according to two methods. Either by pricking with a broad myrtle phlebotome, or an olive to the fineness of a slope, or by cutting with a knife phlebotome, and it is Alnesil. And this is its form.
And from them are broad and subtle types, according to the breadth of the veins and their constriction. The form of the myrtle phlebotome should be broad, as you see. And it suits for opening concave, full, proceeding, apparent, thick veins, and those that contain thick, turbid blood.
And this phlebotome is of lesser width, and of finer extremities.
And it suits for the phlebotomy of thin veins, which contain thin, yellowish blood.
The Basilica, which is one of these three veins, is an aid to phlebotomy. Because it draws blood from afflictions that are beneath the throat and neck, from that which follows the chest, and the belly; and it befits the phlebotomist, when phlebotomizing it, to be cautious, and to be above fear of it, for beneath it is an artery. If therefore he errs, and adds to the puncture of the phlebotomy, he will cut that artery, and an outflow of blood will occur. Therefore it is necessary, that he not phlebotomize it with a phlebotomy-piercing instrument; rather let him be cutting with an Alnesil.
If, therefore, the Basilica vein does not appear with clear manifestation, then it is necessary to abandon it, and to change to another, or to seek one of its branches; or to phlebotomize the brachial vein in its place. For it is indeed manifest, and cut it with a phlebotomy Anesil instrument, as we have said.
If, however, you wish to phlebotomize its same place, then it is necessary, before the constriction of the arm, to feel the area, until you know the pulse location. Then mark the area with ink, afterward the arm, and cut the vein with a concave incision using a phlebotomy Alnesil instrument, as we have said. And be careful that the incision does not fall within the space of the artery.
And when you see, as you tighten the ligature, a swelling in the spot that you have marked with ink, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery: therefore, avoid it.
If you see blood at the phlebotomy leaping forth, like a child's urine, and it is thin, red blood: then know that it is from the artery's blood. Then quickly place your finger upon it for a long space, then remove your finger. If, therefore, blood is cut off (for it is often cut off), then tighten the arm, and release it: and let the sick person be wary of its movement; and let it be above his fear.
And do not move it for several days, until it is healed.
If, however, the blood does not cut off, and it conquers you, and there is no medicine present at your moment, then cut the artery, if it appears to you. For its extremities contract, and the blood is cut off.
Or take a piece of fig-bark, and split it: take one half, and bind it over the place of the vein's constriction in a suitable manner, with a bandage and dressings until another day.
Then if the blood is cut off, it is good. And if not, then treat it with what has been previously mentioned from the place of powders that cut off the flow of blood: and the cutting off of its blood is not difficult in many situations, on account of the small size of the wound, and the possibility of bandaging from the arm.
Phlebotomy of the Fusca vein is beneficial, as it draws blood from the upper part of the head and the lower part of the body, because it is composed of a branch of the Basilic vein and a branch of the Cephalic vein, as we have said.
And it befits the phlebotomist to be wary when performing phlebotomy on it, for there is a nerve beneath it. Therefore, if the phlebotomist, in making the puncture, encounters a nerve, a numbness occurs, and the healing becomes difficult, and perhaps it is not completely healed.
And this nerve frequently appears to the senses. However, if it is hidden in some people, and is subtle and not shown, then it is necessary that you place your phlebotomy, so that you phlebotomize it, a section with Alnesil, and avoid the nerve with your strength.
If the vein is between two nerves, then split the vein lengthwise.
Concerning the Cephalic vein. Phlebotomy of the Cephalic vein is beneficial, because it draws blood from the head and is helpful in diseases of the eyes.
And it is proper in this vein, if you wish, that you phlebotomize it by pricking with an olive phlebotom, or with a myrtle phlebotom, because it is safer than all the veins, because there is no artery nor nerve beneath it.
However, it befits you in phlebotomy to avoid striking the head of the muscle, and to seek a gentler location.  And it does not harm it if it is not found on the first strike, so that phlebotomy may be repeated upon it several times.
However, perhaps it becomes inflamed in some people when it is not phlebotomized on the first strike, but that inflammation does not harm it in any way.
On the usefulness of Phlebotomy, and the precautions to be observed in it. The quality of phlebotomy, and its resultant accidents, which you must consider correcting.
First, you must know that phlebotomy is not performed except in the preservation of health, and its persistence, and caution against the onset of illnesses.
And so that phlebotomy is performed in one of the three veins in the elbow, namely the Cephalic, and the Fusca, and the Basilic. And that phlebotomy be performed at the beginning of spring on an empty stomach, in spring and summer; and in autumn and winter after eating, when signs of fullness appear.
And let phlebotomy be performed on the first or third day, after three hours have passed from the day.
However, phlebotomy performed in illnesses does not have a set time: but when convenience and necessity call for it, at night, or by day, and in any hour, and at any time.
And children should not be bled until they reach fourteen years of age. Nor should a pregnant woman be bled, except from the fourth to the seventh month. Nor should elderly people be bled who have already passed sixty years.
Therefore, whenever someone wishes to be bled, for whatever reason, it is necessary that they cleanse their bowels before the phlebotomy with a gentle clyster, if there is much retained stool in them, so that the veins do not draw putrid, harmful superfluities from the bowels during the phlebotomy, damaging the principal limbs.
Nor should a patient be bled who has nausea from satiety, nor one who is bloated, nor one recovering from drunkenness, until that is removed from them. Nor a sick person with dropsy.
And phlebotomy should be avoided again after a colic attack, and vomiting, and diarrhea, and excessive intercourse, and labor, and exercise, and vigils, and fasting, and anything that dissolves strength from the body or the animal.
Then consider, in the subtlety of the humours beforehand, if the blood is thick with foods and drinks, and medicines; if it is possible for him to do so.
Then let him enter a bath, if nothing prevents him; or let him exercise with some exercise, so that the blood may be refined.
And place his phlebotomy on his chest, about the third day, as we have said; and endeavor that his chest be emptied that day of all harmful animal accidents: such as anxiety, and anger, and fear; and of all bodily accidents, such as labor, and pain, and excessive intercourse, and similar things.
And let him have present in his seat things with whose use custom has run among men from aromatics, and scents, and dances, and things similar to them to the extent possible.
Then let the phlebotomist sit on a pillow which is higher than the phlebotomist's pillow; afterwards, let him pull out his arm, and let the phlebotomist rub it with his hand twice, or thrice. Then let him tie the ligature with a strap, and wrap it twice.
And let the constriction be moderate, because constriction, when it is unequal, or by excess in the constriction, then it hinders the flow of blood; or in slackening, then it also hinders the flow of blood.
Then after the constriction, it is necessary that the phlebotomist rub his hands together until the veins swell and become apparent to the senses.
Then let the phlebotomist wipe the phlebotomized area with a little old oil, properly.
Then let him place his left index finger just above the vein, slightly below the place where he wants to perform the phlebotomy, so that the vein does not move and the puncture is not missed. Because there are veins that, like a fleeing chord during phlebotomy, and some that are full of air; and when you place the phlebotom on them, they contract under the phlebotom, deceiving the phlebotomist and preventing the opening of the vein; and if he does open it, it is narrow. Therefore, it is necessary for the phlebotomist to be firm and careful in all these matters.
Then let him remove the phlebotom. If therefore the vein is opened at once, then it is good. And if not, then do it again a second time just below that place, or slightly above it, with haste, if the place is not already inflamed.
If, however, it becomes inflamed or the patient grows weak, then leave him for a day or two, and do not tighten the bandage. For perhaps it will draw the inflammation. Nor let him enter a bath.
Then let him have phlebotomy, if he desires.
If the opening is small or the blood flows thinly, and you fear that not the desired amount of blood will issue, then again perform phlebotomy in the same opening along the vein, and add a little more to the operation.
And do it quickly, before the place becomes inflamed, for an inflamed place occurs in many people with a minor operation.
If, therefore, you see it already inflamed, then do not repeat the procedure on it at all, for nothing excuses you in this. And place on it something from the dregs of oil, since it alleviates the flow of blood. And it is better in this place than the oil itself, and than other oils.
And similarly administer the dregs of oil in all your phlebotomies, where phlebotomies of the veins are at the prohibition of the flow of blood: and the Tyriaca alfarach and the sagzenea do this, when one of them is placed upon the site.
For blood is refined and resolved, when it is thick.
If a large abscess happens at the site of phlebotomy, for it often happens, especially to one who is not a phlebotomist except on that occasion, or the opening of the vein is small, then quickly place on the site a sponge soaked in water and salt, warmed a little: and bind it for one hour, because it is resolved.
And it is necessary to do this after the blood has flowed out from the vein itself, with its completion, or from another vein.
If some blackness or greenness remains at the site after a few days, it does not harm: and if you wish, place some aloe or dissolved myrrh on it, or something from the juice of mint, that is, of calamint or something similar to it.
And very often indeed abscesses and swellings occur at the phlebotomy of the Basilica.
Therefore place your hand upon it; then if you find softness upon your compression upon it, then that swelling is bad. Therefore beware, that you place something on it from that which we have said. For perhaps blood will flow from it of an artery.
But make a plaster for it with that in which there is astringency, so that the place may be hardened. Then treat it with the remaining treatment, until it is healed.
And it is necessary that you extract from every man's blood according to the quantity of its virtue. And that which appears from the color prevailing over the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it exit until it reddens: and likewise if you see it thick, then let it go until it becomes thin: and likewise if it is sharp, until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for one who is full, strong, and needs bloodletting at once, that the vein incision be enlarged and the phlebotomist be wide: and in the one who is weak, be the opposite of that.
And it is necessary that he extract it in stages, and that the opening be narrow: and better that which is administered in vein incision is that it be a transverse section, not a puncture.
And this method of bloodletting is safe from blood flow and from nerve incision: and it is more praiseworthy and safer than bloodletting by width and length.
And to whom it is accustomed to come upon bloodletting syncope, it is necessary that he eat before bloodletting with moderate bread steeped in pomegranate water: or Secaniabin, if it is heated: and extract from his blood in three or four stages.
And if he is of a cold complexion, then let him take before bloodletting bread soaked in date syrup, or in syrup of aromatized honey with spices, or in aromatic fragrant syrup.
If syncope occurs during bloodletting, and its cause is the excessive outflow of blood, then it is necessary that he drink water of flesh and a subtle fragrant syrup; and that he use aromatic substances with alalia and that his chest be lined with it; and that he use the rest that we have mentioned in the division in the chapter on syncope from evacuation.
He who wants to empty his arm, and to release his blood a second time: it is necessary for him whose bloodletting is for a great evacuation, and whose strength is weak, to release his blood gradually in proportion to his strength over consecutive days.
However, he who wishes to empty his arm and release his blood a second time, and his body is strong, then let him do so after seven or nine hours from his first bloodletting.
He who wishes to draw blood from his body to the opposite part to which it inclines, it is necessary that he be emptied on the second or third day.
In whose body, however, there is much blood already heated and sharpened, and it causes fever to occur, it is necessary that blood depart from it in one turn, and a great quantity be extracted from it; and the opening be enlarged until syncope occurs; after you have observed all the conditions of bloodletting; and that you place your hand upon his pulse at the flow of blood, so that death does not occur in place of syncope. For often that happens, when the bloodletter is ignorant, and negligence falls.
And it is not necessary, when you wish to empty the arm and release blood a second time, when the opening of the vein is already closed, and the exit of blood is difficult, to press upon it with violence, and to rapidly bend or extend it. For that is very bad, indeed, leave it, until it is bled a second time.
Or remove with sharpness the blood clot from the vein opening, and place some dissolved salt in water upon it. And place something of tyriaca alfaroch, or sagzenea, upon it, and apply gentle pressure until blood emerges.
If however the vein is already suppurated, then leave it and do not touch it until the abscess subsides.
If necessity compels you to draw blood a second time, and there is no excuse, then either bleed him above that spot, or in another arm in another vein.
The arm's vein should be opened at the place of the Fusca and Basilica, if they are not found, and are hidden, since it is composed of them.
And the quality of the phlebotomy is such that the weak person should immerse their hand in warm water until the skin reddens and the vein appears clearly.
Then it should be tied above it with a ligature of medium constriction. Then the incision should be made transversely, gradually, not laterally, nor lengthwise: and let the opening be wide, and let your phlebotomy be just above the joint of the hand for a short while.
If the outflow of blood is prevented, then again immerse the hand in warm water and let the blood run into the water until you achieve your need.
However, if you are in the days of summer, then you should refrain from repeating the immersion of the hand in warm water. And indeed, the course of blood is placed most in warm water in the time of winter.
And the phlebotomy of this vein is safer than all other veins, because there is no pulsating vein or nerve beneath it.
Phlebotomy of the Saluatella vein of the right hand is beneficial for diseases of the liver.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is such that you should tie the mahason of the hand with a bandage, or with your hand, after you introduce it into warm water, until the vein swells and is clearly visible to the sense. Then, phlebotomize it transversely. And if you cut it completely, it will not harm him in any way.
And take care not to injure your hand with the phlebotome. For beneath it are the nerves of the fingers bare of flesh.
Then, return your hand to warm water, and let the blood run into it. For if you do not return it to warm water, the blood coagulates in the opening of the vein, and is prevented from flowing.
When therefore you have extracted the necessary quantity of blood, then place oil and salt over the vein: so that it does not clot quickly.
And similarly, it is necessary that you do with every constricted branch.
The benefit of phlebotomy from the left hand, however, is: because it is beneficial for illnesses of the spleen: and similarly do in its phlebotomy, as you did in the second equally.
The benefit of saphenous vein phlebotomy, however, is for illnesses that are in the lower part of the body, such as illnesses of the womb, and retention of menses, and illnesses of the kidneys, and old ulcers of the hips and legs, and similar illnesses.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is that the sick person should immerse their foot in warm water, and make friction upon it, until the veins swell, and tie above the ankle joint with a strap. And indeed, the vein's location is at the heel, appearing towards the big toe: and from it branch out many branches on the foot's surface.
Therefore, phlebotomize it in its larger branch, or at the heel in its aggregation, because it is better and safer.
If you phlebotomize it on the foot's surface, then beware of the nerves that are beneath it on the foot's surface.
And place your phlebotomy in such a way that you phlebotomize it across, as if you wanted to cut it, and let the phlebotomist be Nesil.
If, however, the flow of blood is prohibited, then again place his foot in warm water, and let the blood run into it, until it is evacuated.
If the phlebotomist errs in the first attempt, then he should return with his phlebotomy a little higher, for the place is safe, and you do not fear harm from it, when you beware of the nerves, as we have said.
And similarly with the saphena of the other foot equally.
The Sciatic vein's location, as we have said, is near the heel on the wild side.
And the benefit of phlebotomy is for the pain of the hip, when it is due to hot blood.
And the quality of its phlebotomy is that the infirm person should enter a warm bath, and hasten; and he should bind their leg from the side of the hip up to the heel by four fingers, with a thin long bandage: for it does not appear except because of that.
When therefore it appears, then the phlebotomist should lance it according to whatever method is possible for you, or by transverse incision; and it is better either to cut it with an incision or to sever it with a section. For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in many people.
For its location is safer, and it is very hidden in many people. If you do not find it, and it does not appear at all to the senses, then lance one of its branches, and that one which appears on the back of the foot, and towards the ear: and beware of the nerves, and cause as much blood to flow as you wish.
Then loosen the bandage, and place leather over the phlebotomy site, and bind the location. For it heals quickly.
Concerning Cupping, and the quality of its use.
Cupping instruments are made from horns, and from bronze, and from glass.
And cupping is indeed done in two ways. With scarification, and the extraction of blood: and the other is cupping without scarification.
And this cupping, which is without scarification, is according to two methods. Or it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And cuppings that are administered with scarification and the extraction of blood are fourteen places on the body.
