Of which one is the cupping of the Alnocrati, and is at the back of the head; and the Alkehel, and is the middle of the neck; and the cuppings of the Alhagdahim, and are two blades of the neck on both sides together; and the cuppings of the mentum, and are beneath the jaw in the lower mouth; and the cuppings of the two shoulder blades; and the cuppings of the Alachosos above the end of the tail; and the cuppings of the two hollows, and are in the middle of the two arms; and the cuppings of the two thighs; and the cuppings of the two heels.
And cupping indeed does not extract blood from the small veins scattered in the flesh, and for that reason no virtue is lost from them, as is the case with phlebotomy. Nor should cupping, whether with fire or without fire, be administered in any illness arising from fullness, until the whole body is emptied.
If necessity prompts cupping due to illness or custom, we administer it at any time during the beginning, end, and middle of the month, and at whatever time it may be.
This is because there are people who, when blood multiplies within them to the point where cupping is needed for its extraction, find heaviness and fullness in their head.
And among them are those who find fullness and redness in their face, head, and neck. And among them are those who find itching in their face and forehead, and darkness and dullness in their eyes.
And among them are those who scratch the site of their cupping. And among them is one whose laughter is multiplied. And among them is one who finds the taste of blood in their mouth; or the tongue becomes suppurated, and they spit out blood. And among them is one whose sleep is multiplied. And among them is one who sees in their sleep blood and redness, and slain men, and wounds, and things similar to these.
Therefore, when we see any of these things, and especially if it is in the third middle of the month, we then order that a venesection be performed, after two or three hours have passed of the day.
The benefit of venesection of the nape of the neck is that it contributes to the heaviness in the head and that which flows to the eyes. However, it is necessary that it be after the evacuation of the whole body.
And this ventosa is in the place of a cephalic phlebotomy.
And it is not permitted to use it for one of cold complexion or one with a cold. For it brings him great harm.
And because of that, it is not appropriate for the elderly to use it, nor those with cold ailments in their heads.
But whoever becomes diligent over it, oblivion is generated over him.
And because of that, it is necessary to order the Ventosator to lay his hand with the wind instrument a little lower, fearing the generation of oblivion.
Ventosation, or Alkehel, is in the place of phlebotomy of the Fusca and phlebotomy of the Basilica, and because of that it helps asthma, and constriction of breathing, and the fissure of the breathing instrument, and cough, and fullness.
And it is necessary that the Alkehel cupping be applied a little, because if placed lower, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
Ventosation with alhagdahim helps accidental headaches, and ophthalmia, and migraine, and perforation, and toothache: and it is in place of phlebotomy of the Basilica.
And it is necessary that you instruct the Ventosator not to press his hand with the scarification, so that he does not cut an artery, and a flow of blood occurs.
Ventosation under the chin helps sores in the mouth, and gum inflammation, and similar diseases in the mouth: and it is in place of phlebotomy of alieherich, which are on the two lips.
Ventosation with spatulas helps alchafacham, which comes from repletion and heat.
Ventosation of the inner parts of the two cheeks helps the benefit of phlebotomy of the three veins, Basilica, and Fusca, and Cephalica: because it draws blood from all those small veins which are in the flesh: and those small veins draw from other larger veins than themselves, until the drawing reaches the three veins.
And it is necessary that you instruct the Ventosator not to proceed too much in the scarification, because the place is denuded of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
A ventosa that is used on alchausos helps hemorrhoids of the anus and lower ulcers.
And it is necessary that you instruct the Ventosator to use a large, air-filled ventosa, because the place needs strong suction, and perhaps the small ventosas will break, and a large scarification should be made.
Ventosas of the legs cause noticeable reduction: because they draw blood from the entire body, and they help old pains in the kidneys, and the uterus, and the bladder: they provoke menstruation, and they help with boils, and ademenul; and they take the place of phlebotomy of the two saphenous veins, and alhorcobeni.
However, they weaken the body greatly, and cause fainting in many people.
And the benefit of alhorcobeni ventosas is close to the benefit of ventosas of the two legs.
The quality of applying ventosas is to place the ventosa initially empty, and to apply moderate suction; and do not prolong the application of ventosas, but place them quickly and remove them quickly, so that the humors come to the place with equal arrival; and do not cease to repeat this and continue until you see the place already red and swollen, and the redness of blood appears; then scarify, and then repeat the suction gradually. Then consider the disposition of the bodies.
For those who are of tender flesh, with rare pores, it is necessary that you scarify them once, no more, so that the place is not ulcerated.
And instruct the ventosator to enlarge the scarification a little and deepen it slightly; and to temper the suction with ease, speed, and smooth movement.
If there is thickness in the blood, then it is necessary to scarify it twice. Indeed, in the first instance, a path is opened to the thinning of the blood and its becoming watery; and in the second, to the careful investigation of extracting thick blood.
If, however, the blood is very foul, then a scarification should be performed a third time, so that the ultimate goal may be achieved.
And finally, when we wish to extract blood gradually, or a little bit, one scarification is sufficient for us. If we wish for a large extraction of blood, we scarify with many scarifications.
And if we measure that the blood is thick, then it is necessary that it be scarified with a deep scarification; and the equal limit in the scarification is the depth of the skin.
What is that which must be administered from oils at the application of Cupping-glasses, and what does the cupper beware of?
In him indeed whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and whose pores are tight, it is necessary that the places for Cupping be anointed with oils that are opening, softening, and resolving: if it is in the summer, such as is the oil of alkeiri, or bruised oil, or oil from sweet almonds, or oil from pumpkin seed.
And if it is in winter, then narcissus oil, or lily oil, or camomile oil, or sarbantine oil: and similar to them.
If however the superfluity is thick, cold: then the oil should be amaracin oil, or balsam oil, or alben oil, or dill oil: and similar to them.
If however the cupper is of wide pores, tender flesh: then it is necessary that he be forbidden from oil, and it is necessary that they wash the places of the Cupping-glasses after cupping with rosewater, or with cold water, or with soothing water, or with pumpkin water, or with purslane water: and with things similar to them.
He however whose blood is of much moisture, should wash the cupping glasses with vinegar, or with myrtle water, and sumac: and with things similar to them.
He whose superfluities are thick, should wash his cupping glasses with old wine, or with sorrel water, or with dill decoction, or chamomile: and with things similar to them.
And it is necessary that cupping be avoided in a bath, or in the vicinity of a bath, indeed it is necessary that it be administered after leaving the bath for an hour, or for two hours.
And it is not necessary that anyone sleep after cupping.
What is it by which the Ventosus, or the Phlebotomus, should be governed before cupping?
And after it, one must consider this first: if the Ventosus or Phlebotomus is choleric, and sharpness and inflammation conquer over his blood, then it is necessary that he take cooling things, such as pomegranate juice, and also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and secaniabin, and iuleb, and things similar to them.
And place his food as chickens, and the flesh of sheep sekebiget, and foods seasoned with wild berries, and things similar to them.
And whoever's complexion is cold, it is necessary that he take in drink syrup of honey, or of almiua, or alsecaniabin made from seeds, and take medium fragrant wine, which is between old and new, and let it be prescribed that he use little food. And place his food as chickens, and canebir, and sparrows, that is, small birds, and young pigeons issiadebet.
And it is necessary that the drink on the day of ventosation and phlebotomy be more than the food, and it is necessary that in some people, in the hours, tyriaca alfaroch, or medicine of musk, that is, musk, or alesselithe be given in the drink before ventosation, and before phlebotomy, or after, so that the principal limbs are strengthened, and the blood is refined. And it is not necessary that they take it in heated drink.
Of Ventosations without scarification. However, ventosations without scarification are ventosations that are placed upon the liver, and spleen, and gout, and upon the belly, navel, and places of the kidneys, and the chest cavity, because these members do not endure scarification upon themselves.
And they are not sought out unless either an attachment of blood from a member to a member: such as we place a ventosation upon the nipples in the illness of a nasal blood flow. And we use them to release cold airiness stuck in a member, such as we place a ventosation on the stomach and navel. For it indeed rarefies the member, and warms it, and removes the pain, because it has dissolved that airiness.
Also, it is placed upon the kidneys, when obstruction or a stone occurs in them. For by its power of attraction it perhaps opens the obstruction or uproots the stone from its place.
And similarly it does when placed upon the liver and spleen, for airiness to be broken up in both of them.
And these cupping glasses are administered empty, with only incision. And sometimes they are administered with fire.
And sometimes they are administered full of lukewarm water in cases of pleurisy; and that is, that the cupping glass be filled, and be large, with just hot water, or water in which herbs suitable to it have been boiled.
Form of cupping glass administered with fire.
Let its opening be the size of two open fingers, according to what we have shaped; and let its depth be half a palm; and let there be a small hole on its side, approximately in the middle of it, to fit a needle; made of brass or tin, with thick, smooth, even, polished edges so it does not hurt the limb where it is positioned above it; and let there be a transverse rod of brass or iron in its middle, where the candle with fire is placed.
And now this cupping glass is made large, larger than we have described, and a little smaller than that, according to the illnesses and age of the one who uses it.
For the cupping glasses of children and thin people are different from the cupping glasses of men and those with large bodies.
The method of placing this cupping glass with fire on the limb is to light a wick with fire, made of linen, properly, or a small candle made of wax, and place it over the middle of the transverse rod that is in the middle of the cupping glass, so that the fire is lit approximately at the lower part of the cupping glass, so that the sick person's body is not burned.
Then it is placed on the limb, and a finger is placed over the hole I mentioned until we hold the cupping glass; when we need to, we remove the finger, and vapor will come out through that hole, and the cupping glass will immediately be released.
Then light the wick according to its method, and reapply it if you need it.
However, in a cupping glass used in the illness of pleurisy with water, there is no transverse rod, nor a hole; and it is not administered unless it is filled with water and placed on the limb only.
And this is its form.
And the larger this cupping glass is, so that it can hold much water, the better it is.
Concerning the suspension of leeches.
Leeches are not applied in many cases, except where cupping is not possible due to the small size of the affected body parts, such as the lip, and the gums, and similar parts. Or in a limb that is bare of flesh, such as a finger, and the nose, and similar parts.
And the quality of using them is: to choose leeches that are found in fresh, clean waters, free from corruption.
Then let them be kept in fresh water, day and night, until they are hungry, and nothing remains in their bellies. Then the body should be emptied first with bloodletting and cupping. Then wipe the limb of the sick person until it turns red. Afterwards, place them upon it.
When, therefore, they are filled, and fall, and suction of the place with a cupping vessel is possible, then it is more ultimate in aid; and if not, then wash the place with vinegar. Then with much water, and it should be rubbed and squeezed out.
If the course of blood, after the fall of the Sanguisugarum, prolongs itself, and it is oozing: then immerse a linen cloth in cold water, and place it on top, until the oozing is stopped.
If the blood multiplies, then pulverize on it ground zegi, or gallnuts, and similar astringents, until the blood is stopped.
Or place the halves of shelled beans on the area: and let them remain, until the beans adhere to the place, for the blood is stopped.
And if a repetition of the Sanguisugarum is necessary, when possible, change them.
If, however, the leeches are prevented from hanging on, then the area is to be wiped with fresh blood. Or stick a needle into the areas, until some blood comes out. Then place them, for when they feel some blood, they immediately adhere.
When therefore you want them to fall off, powder some crushed aloe, or salt, or ash, over them: and they will fall off immediately.
This chapter is also very necessary in the art of medicine, and it is the restoration of fractures and dislocations of accidents in the bones.
Know ye, O children, that the foolish physicians and the common people boast about this chapter, and those who have never examined the book of the ancients, nor read a letter from it.
And for this reason, this method is private among the sciences in our land. And I never encountered any good in it at all, and I did not acquire that from it which I have acquired except because of the length of reading the books of the ancients, and study on the understanding of them, until I extracted the knowledge of it from them.
Then I adhered to experiment and study for the length of my life.
And now I have described to you from it in this chapter all that my knowledge contains: and upon which my experiment passed, after I abbreviated it for you, and delivered it from the occupation of elongation, and abbreviated it by the end of abbreviation, and showed it by the end of declination: and I formed in it many forms from the forms of instruments which are administered in it, since it is from the addition of declination, as I did in the two preceding chapters.
Chapter II.
It is necessary, as we say in the prologue of this chapter, that the summaries of discourse and chapters, for the understanding of which the craftsman is first required to stand on their truth: namely, that you, and those who are ready, may know, or learn this noble art, and others from you.
I say, therefore, that when a fracture, or dislocation, or twisting, or fall occurs to someone, then it is necessary, firstly, to apply to him phlebotomy, and to loose him, or both at once, if nothing prohibits it, such as weakness of strength; or if the person to whom something like that happened is a child or a decrepit old man; or if the time is of great heat, or great cold.
Then let him be content in his food with cold vegetables, and birds' flesh, and kids' flesh; and forbid wine, and coarse meats, and repletion from foods and every food that fills the veins with blood. Until when you are secure from a hot abscess, and do not fear the effusion of matter to the limb; then let the sick man return to his first regimen with which his habit is accustomed.
When, therefore, the broken bone begins to be restored, then it is necessary that the sick person be fed with nourishing foods, with much, coarse, heavy nourishment, in which there is viscosity; such as foods made from cooked wheat with water, and rice, and heads, and feet, and bellies of cows, and eggs, and fresh fish: and thick wine, and similar things to them. For with this regimen the coagulation of the fracture is made quicker and better.
And know that broken bones, when they are in men who are in the prime of old age, and decrepit, cannot be joined and consolidated according to their own nature always, because of the drying of their limbs; and those that are in them at the end of softness are joined and consolidated, like the bones of small children.
However, nature attaches something similar to glue, in which there is thickness, to the broken bone from every side, by which it adheres to and binds it, until they are glued together and bound together, until it comes to the end of strength and firmness, as it was at first, until nothing prevents it from its functions; and for this reason it is necessary that you place the sick person's nourishment on foods in which there are weight, and viscosity, and thickness, as we said.
And know that the species of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of the limbs: for the fracture of the leg is different from the fracture of the head bone; and the fracture of the breastbone is different from the fracture of the spine; and likewise all the other limbs are different from each other: and I came with a recollection of every species of fracture, set out in its own chapter, distinct from another. And the species of bone fracture are diversified even within themselves.
Because a fracture of it occurs in an arched manner, besides which splinters happen in it. And sometimes a fracture of it occurs along the length of the bone; and there are its fractures that are splinters, that is, splinters and additional protrusions, just as they are in a reed, when it is sharpened by a knife for writing. And some are not protrusions; and there is a fracture with a wound and disruption at the head; and there is a fracture with a small fissure.
And for every species there is a particular nature, or instrument, in its restoration, according to which its restoration will come, distinct in their places.
And from what is known about the fracture of the bone, there is its twisting, and its prominence; and its appearance to the senses, and its sound when you compress it with your hand.
Therefore, when there is no apparent twisting in the location, nor sound upon touching the bone with agitation, nor does the patient experience significant pain, then there is no fracture there; rather, it is possible that there is a twist, or a small fissure.  Therefore, it is not necessary to move it with the hand and pressure entirely, but rather place upon it medicaments of which the application is known, according to the time appropriate to the location; then a gentle binding is applied.
And know that when a location is bent and split into two, besides the struggles that occur in it, yet every part is removed from its counterpart; then it is necessary for you to begin your work to rectify it, equalizing it before a hot abscess occurs in it.
If however a hot abscess occurs in it, then leave it for several days until the abscess subsides. Then equalize it according to any method possible with gentleness and skill.
And know that the restoration and equalization of it is easier than of a bone, in which struggles occur.
Then bind it according to how its recollection comes to you.
However, if there are struggles in the bone, then an extension of this fractured limb is necessary, in two parts if it is a hand or foot; or with your hand if the limb is small; or with two ropes, or with a rope and a hand.
And let your position be such that you place the limb upon an equal surface, according to its natural shape, until, when the parts of the fractured limb are extended, then endeavor to return those additions to their places with all your skill and by any means possible; and be careful, according to your strength, lest pain or injury befall the weak through your operation; and endeavor, according to your strength, to join one of the two bones with its fellow, in the best forms.
And it is necessary at that hour to try and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see anything different there, correct it and equalize it by the measure of your ability.
And beware of violent extension and strong compression, as many fools do. For many times they cause a hot abscess or contraction in the limb through their operation, as we have already seen it from their operations many times.
Then bind after equalization, and proper operation, and constriction of that member, and rest, and quiet; and advise the sick person about it. And make him beware that he does not move it in the hour of his waking, and his sleep at its turning, and his agitation, and at his excretion, and all his movements with his utmost strength. And that he beware that the position of the limb be not one that will be followed by pain. For when he feels pain or injury in the disposition of the position of the limb, then it is necessary to change it to a different position in which he does not feel pain.
And beware with him that his position be equal, straight, so that twisting in the limb does not occur when it is restored.
And the quality of the fracture of the limb is as I relate.
Know that fractured limbs are diversified in their smallness and their size, and their shape.
Therefore, those of them that are small, such as the arm, and the finger, and the foot, and similar to them, then it is necessary that there be a wrapping of soft, thin cloth. However, those of them that are large, such as the thigh and the leg, it is necessary that there be a wrapping that is broad, hard.
Because a broad ligament connects a large limb and binds it from every side with equal constriction, so that no spot enters it.
Then, after your evacuation, begin to place suitable liniment on that, in soft linen upon the very place of the fracture itself. Then begin wrapping the bandage over the place of the fracture itself with three or four wraps, according to what the limb deserves: and tighten your hand somewhat with the bandage.
Then proceed with it to the upper part from the place of the fracture, and tighten less than your previous tightening, with which you tightened the fractured place. Then move away from the place of the fracture with the bandage somewhat, and gradually loosen the tightening, until you take a great deal from the healthy place.
Then take another strip, and wrap it again over the fractured place with wraps. Afterwards, proceed with the bandage to the lower part from the fracture. And let your operation be in the tightening of the bandage, and in its loosening, according to what we said in the first upper bandage.
Then place between the bandages soft wool, or cloth, with which the twisting of the fracture is equalized, if there is twisting in it: and if not, do not place anything in it.
Then wrap another strip over it. Afterwards, place evenly over these bandages properly made splints, if there is no swelling in the place, nor a hot abscess.
But if there is swelling in it, or an abscess: then place over it what settles that abscess, and removes the swelling: and leave it for several days. Then tie the splints over it.
And let the splints be made from the halves of wide canes, shaved, skillfully shaped; or let them be of the wood from which sieves are made, which are made from pine, or gerard, that is, palm branches, or calingi; and let the width of each splint, according to this form, and this same figure, be uniform.
However, the splint placed over the wound itself should be somewhat thicker and wider than the other splints; and the length of the splint should be according to the size and smallness of the limb.
Then tie the splints with another bandage, according to the same tightness as the first. Then bind above with threads neatly, according to what we said about the tightness; and it is as if your tightness over the fracture site should be greater; and the further it is from the fracture, the less the tightness should be.
And the threads should be medium in thickness and fineness, and be of soft linen.
For if the threads are thick, as I have seen from the operation of foolish men who place their threads from twisted linen cords, this is a great error, for the tightness fails with them beyond quality.
And again, thin threads are not suitable, for you will not achieve through the tightness with them that which you desire.
And there should not be less than a finger's breadth between splint and splint.
If the patient is injured by the ends of the splints after the tightness in healthy areas, then place under them soft tow, or dyed wool, until nothing is injured by it.
However, when there is a fracture with a wound, and a rupture occurs in the skin, I will come with a recollection of it in the second chapter according to its singularity.
And know that not every fractured limb should be bound with splints from the first day. Because, when the limb is large, you should not place splints upon it unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your safety concerning the outcome of a hot abscess.
And this is the method of plasters, by which the ancients restored those things that are placed over a fracture and dislocation.
A method of an abbreviated common plaster, restoring a fracture, suitable for many complexions, especially women and children, because it is from that which is not overcome by heat nor cold.
And it is, that you take from the mill dust, and it is the flour bran which hangs on the walls of the mill at the movement of the millstone: and you make it as it is, except that you sift it with the white of an egg: and place its mixture neither thick nor thin. Then use it.
Another method of plaster, restoring fracture, dislocation, and twisting. Take of almess, laudanum, acacia, enula, and mugath, and juice, of all, each a drachm. Of myrrh and aloes, each a drachm. And of alethil, twenty drachms. Grind all these, and sift them, and mix them with alethil water; or with the white of an egg, if the complexion of the sick person is inflamed. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, quickly restores broken bones, and benefits many people.
Another method of plaster, which also benefits fracture and twisting. Take of mugath, mess, and white marshmallow, of all, each a drachm. Of myrrh and aloes, each a drachm. Acacia, six drachms. Armenian bole, twenty drachms. Grind all these with much grinding, and sift them, and make them into a paste with water, or with the white of an egg, and administer them.
Method of plaster for joints and bones separated from their places; and it soothes the pain that comes to them, and the fissure of the bone, and the fracture. Take soft wool and soak it in vinegar and boiled oil, and place it over the affected area.
And in this plaster there is no power of restoration, but it is good in calming a hot abscess and expels pain properly.
Method of plaster, which restores a fractured bone. Take leaves of the figwort and leaves of the wild poppy; and grind them all, and make a plaster from both.
Method of a shortened plaster, administered in the restoration of a fractured bone; and when you want resolution of the remaining abscess. Take of althea root, chamomile, and violet flowers, and herb flour, of each one part: let all be ground, and mixed with wine, if the limb is not inflamed.
If however it is inflamed, then mix it with the water of moist coriander, or with water, and use it.
Method of another stronger plaster for resolution, than this first one, when a hard abscess occurs in the restoration of a limb. Take of althea root, flaxseed, pomegranate bark, melilot, sorrel, violet flowers, and chamomile, of each one part: let all be ground, and mixed with willow water, or with sweet water, or with wine. All of that according to the heat of the limb, and the quiet of its heat.
Hippocrates, however, did not say in his book that anything should be placed on a fractured limb during its restoration except a cerate made from wax and oil: nothing else; and he recounted that it should be intermediate in thickness and thinness.
Galen, however, considered that things should be placed on a fractured limb during its restoration, which have a drying effect on heat, such as myrrh, aloes, frankincense, and similar to them from those we have mentioned.
Method of a plaster that is helpful for injury and pain. Take equal parts of mugwort, chickpeas, cut human hair or bird feathers, marshmallow root, and salt: grind them, sift them, and mix them: and make a plaster from it.
The amount of time the bandage should remain in place, and then be removed, is as follows: if no pain, itching, or movement of the fractured bone occurs, then do not remove it for many days. However, if intense itching, or troubling pain, or swelling occurs in the area, then quickly remove it at once and take the plaster off. Take a soft cloth or soft sea sponge, wet it, and immerse it in lukewarm water and wipe the area with it until the itching subsides and the pain is relieved.
However, if intense itching, or troubling pain, or swelling occurs in the area, then quickly remove it at once and take the plaster off. Take a soft cloth or soft sea sponge, wet it, and immerse it in lukewarm water and wipe the area with it until the itching subsides and the pain is relieved. Then leave the limb at rest for one hour. Then place upon it cut wool soaked in vinegar and oil, or rose oil, and bind it over the area for one night, until you are sure of the absence of a hot abscess, and the swelling of the limb subsides, and its pain is removed.
Then again apply a thin bandage and a small plaster, and do not tighten it with your initial constriction, and proceed with a thin one, until it is healed.
If you see that the abscess, redness, pain, swelling, and all the other symptoms have completely disappeared, and you no longer need a plaster and bandage, then apply it again as you did in the beginning, evenly.
If nothing of what we have said happens to the limb, then do not unbind it unless after three, or four, or five, or seven days. And when it is left twenty days, according to what appears to you from the unwinding of the limb, as we said, until the fracture adheres, and the flesh around it has coagulated, then add more to the bandage than your first complete bandage; and also add to the strengthening of the weak parts, according to the previous recollection of it.
If however you see the fracture site already drying out and wasting away more than necessary, then know that nourishment is prevented from reaching it. Therefore, anoint it with warm water every time you loosen it, and let it be for all three days, and slightly ease the bandage.
By this operation therefore, nourishment flows to the limb, and it heals quickly.
However, what foolish restorers do with a broken limb another time, when it is not restored in the first place as it should be, and is restored according to twists and turns, is an error of their operation, and great fear.  And if it were correct, the ancients would have said so in their books, and would have operated with it.
And I have seen no trace of it anywhere in that at all, and it is better that it not be done.
Concerning the accidental injury to the head.
The types of fractures are diverse, and its shapes are many: and its causes are distinct.
For there is a fracture which occurs from a blow with a sword: and the blow removes the bone entirely as far as it reaches the siphac, which is beneath the bone, just as an axe does in wood: and because of that, this type of fracture is called Axialis.
Or the sword cuts a part of the bone, and cuts its surface, and the incision does not penetrate to its end: and this type of fracture is called Kalaan, that is, a Lifting absolutely.
And there is a wound of two fractures, either large or small.
And there is a fracture which is a crushing or abrasion: and its cause is a blow with a stone, or a fall upon a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also either penetrating, which now approaches the tissue beneath the bone, or is on the surface of the bone: and the wound of these two fractures is also either small or large.
And there is a fracture which is hidden in the bone, the subtlety of a hair: and it is a small fissure, and because of that this type of fracture is called Capillaris.
And there is a fracture, which occurs from a fall, or a blow of a stone, and similar things, which cause the lamina of the bone to enter its interior, and a concavity is formed: just as happens with copper kettles, when a blow strikes them, and a part of them enters their interior.
And this most often happens in the heads of moist bones, such as the heads of children.
And in all these types of fractures, separate splints are made, and I will come with a reminder of all of that in its place.
And we recognize all these types of fractures through examination over them, and investigation of them with probes, and removal of the corrupted flesh which is over them.
The capillary type is recognized by this, that it is detected on the bone, and is cleaned, and anointed over it with ink: for the fracture appears black.
In treating a fracture, consider first the patient's symptoms: then, if you see from the symptoms that it signifies manifest signs of danger, such as vomiting, cholera, extension, loss of understanding, loss of voice, syncope, acute fever, protrusion of the eyes, redness of them, and similar symptoms in various forms, undoubtedly it will not be healed; and if you see symptoms that do not alarm you, and hope for healing: then begin his treatment.
If a wounded person comes to you immediately after being wounded, and it is in the days of winter, then it is necessary that you endeavor in the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all procedures.
And if it is in the days of summer, then it is necessary that you hasten to remove the bone before the seventh day, before the thing under the bone is corrupted by the bandage, and those symptoms which we have recounted occur.
If the fracture of the bone has already reached the bandage touching the brain, and is with fragments and pulling, then it is necessary to remove the broken and ground pieces, in the way I tell you.
And you should shave the head of the wounded person, and uncover the bone in whatever way is possible for you, and according to the shape of the wound and what is light upon the sick person.
If during the uncovering of the bone, a flow of blood or a hot abscess should occur, then apply to it what is appropriate. And you should fill the place with cloths soaked in wine and rose oil, and keep the wound at rest until the abscess subsides, and you are safe from the flow of blood. Then begin the treatment of the bone and its removal.
And that is done according to one of two methods.
One method indeed is to cut the bone with a lancet of narrow blade; and this is its form.
Then administer another lancet, a little wider than the first lancet; and this is its form.
Then administer yet another lancet, even wider than the second.
And the second method is that you should have a number of different lancets; some of which should be wider than others, and some of them shorter than others; and let the points of their blades be sharp, and let them be made of tempered or hardened iron; and use ease in striking on the lancet, so as not to strike the head and injure it.
If however the bone is strong, hard: then it is necessary that you pierce around it, before you administer the lancets, with drills called non-penetrating drills; and they are not called so, unless because they do not pass through the boundary of the fleshy bone to that which is behind it; because a drill has a round end on that which is under its sharp head, like a margin, and a small circle prevents it from sinking and passing through the thickness of the bone.
And it is fitting for you to take from these drills a multitude, each suited to the thickness of the bone, until you have a drill for every skull; whose pointed end's size in length and shortness be in accordance with the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three kinds of drills, or trepans.
The method of drilling around a fractured bone is: to place the drill on the bone, and to rotate it with your fingers, until you know that the bone has been pierced. Then change the drill to another place, and place that which is between each hole the thickness of a radius, or approximately that.
Then cut with the chisels that which is between each hole from the bone: and make it thinner than you can above it from the end of lightness, as we said, until you remove the bone, either with your hand, or with some other of those instruments, which you have prepared for it: such as pliers, and fine forceps.
And it is necessary, that you beware with all caution, lest the drill, or chisel touch anything from the flesh.
When therefore you have encircled the bone by drilling, and it has been separated from the flesh, if it has been continuous with it, and you have extracted it, it is necessary that you smooth and equalize the end of it that remains in the bone with another similar instrument like a chisel. However, it is necessary that it be thin and more subtle than other chisels.
If any small pieces of bone remain, and splinters, then take them with ease, with that which is with you of instruments. Then treat the wound after that with the liniments and ointments that we have mentioned.
And another method of operation is easy, entirely remote from fear: which Galen said, and praised it with great praise: and this is his statement. It is necessary for you first to begin uncovering the part of the bone from the place where the fracture is more severe and more evident until, when you have uncovered that part, and placed under it the extremity of this lenticular chisel, which has this shape.
And the lenticular part cuts nothing smooth; and its pointed part, going lengthwise on its two sides, as you see; so that the lenticular part rests upon the bone, its pointed face striking the bone.
Then strike the chisel from one side with a small hammer until you cut the entire bone with ease, as it turns; and you be secure from the cloth, that no accident happens in it that you fear entirely.
And if the surgeon were not more foolish than men, and more ignorant of it, and were not asleep, then if anything remained attached to the bone from the cloth in certain places, he would cut it away from it with the tip of his lenticular chisel, and free it from it with ease. It is freed from it without harm and without fear.
And if the fracture of the bone does not penetrate to the cloth, and that which is cut away from the bone is its surface, and roughnesses and fine irregularities remain on it: then it is necessary to scrape away that roughness and eradicate those irregularities with fine scrapers from which you have prepared a number of different sizes, so that it may be possible for you to administer from them the most suitable and straighter ones according to that to which the operation leads you, and the fractured bone and its shape. And it is necessary that you administer in your judgement, when you first scrape the bone, more broadly with those scrapers. Then use a finer one after it.
And it is necessary that you administer in your judgement, when you first scrape the bone, more broadly with those scrapers. Then use a finer one after it. And do not cease to do it according to continuity, until you arrive at administering the finest and most delicate of them all.
In each of the remaining small fissures and fine fractures, it is necessary to administer care, according to that which leads to its straightening.
And it is a matter not hidden from all who have any zeal in this art, and stands upon what we have written and shown in large fractures.
If, however, a flap is retained when you remove the bone, then it is necessary to take a linen cloth, according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it over the wound opening. Then take another cloth, doubled or tripled, and immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it over the first cloth; and do it as easily as possible over it so that it is not weighed down by this.
Then apply a broad bandage over it; and do not tighten it, except to the extent that it holds the wound only; and leave it with this treatment for one day or two, until you are certain of being free from a hot abscess. Then remove it, and apply after that remedies with which superfluous dryness is overcome: such as lily roots, and herb flour, and frankincense powder, and birthwort, and similar to them; and it is so that you make a powder from these medicines, which is powdered over the wound as they are dry.
And universally apply to it any medicine whose property is to cleanse, and not to bite. And carefully, according to your skill, ensure that the wound in your treatment is clear; and neither is there any filth of ointment, nor any dirt in it.
And carefully, according to your skill, ensure that the wound in your treatment is clear; and neither is there any filth of ointment, nor any dirt in it. And do not allow any virus to collect in it at all; for when virus collects on the brain membrane, it corrupts it, and putrefies it, and great harm occurs to the sick person after that; and this happens in the skull cavity, when the mouth is cleansed from it, especially when its treatment is neglected, a blackness appears on its surface.
Therefore, if the sick person experiences the misfortunes that we have mentioned, understand that it is undoubtedly harmful.
And if the blackness does not occur, except from the medicine that is placed on it, and it is in the power of that medicine to cause that blackness; then it is necessary that you take one part of honey, three parts of rose oil, and linen with them. Then place it over the skull cavity; afterward treat it with the types of treatment that are appropriate, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the nose when it is broken.
Know that the nose is not broken unless two of its upper parts are broken simultaneously, or one of them; because it is composed of two bones. Since the lower part of it is cartilaginous, it is not broken, and it suffers only attrition, twisting, and crookedness.
If one of its two parts is broken, then it is necessary that you insert the little finger into the nostril, and straighten that fracture with your index finger and thumb from the outside, until the nose returns to its natural shape; and do this skillfully, with ease. And beware lest your operation cause pain to the sick person.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose, and the finger cannot reach it, then it is necessary to straighten it with the extremity of a stick, having a small thickness.
If the fracture is in two parts, then do likewise: and hasten its restoration on the first day of the fracture, if possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day, at the rest of a hot abscess.
Then insert into the extremity of the nose a bandage of linen cloth, if the fracture is in one part; or insert two bandages if the fracture is in two parts; and let the bandage have a thickness of such a quantity as to fill the nostril.
And some of the ancient restorers said that bandages should be soaked in butter and changed every day. However, that does not seem to me, rather it is necessary that the bandage be soaked in the white of an egg, mixed with mill dust. Then leave the bandage until the bone is strengthened and the cartilage hardened.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the nose instead of a bandage, after a soft cloth is wrapped around it; and it is done so that it holds the nose fracture more firmly and so that the weak person is not prevented from breathing.
And this is not necessary. If you wish, do it; and if you wish, do the bandages.
If, while treating a hot abscess, the nose should again become inflamed, plaster the nose with caeroto, or with cot infused in rose oil, or a little of dyaquilon.
However, if a hot abscess does not occur, then it is necessary that you plaster it with sesame flour, and powdered frankincense mixed with egg white. Then place soft lint over it, and do not bind the nose at all.
If, however, the bones of the nose are fractured, or broken into small pieces, then it is necessary that you dissect over them and extract them with instruments that are suitable for them. Then suture the wound, and treat it with whatever causes healing and consolidation with suitable plasters or ointments.
If a wound occurs between the nostrils, then it is necessary to treat it with bandages and administer a lead cannula until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of the lower jaw, when it is broken.
When the lower jaw is broken, and its fracture is not with a wound: then consider if its fracture is only on the outside, and it does not break into two, and curves inward: then its knowledge is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is on its right side, you should insert the index finger of your left hand into the patient's mouth; similarly, if the fracture is on the left side of the jaw, then insert the index finger of your right hand; and with it, gently push the curvature of the fracture from inside toward the outside with your other hand on the inside of the bone, and thus make it even.
If the fracture of the jaw is already separated into two pieces, then it is necessary to apply traction from both sides, according to the straightness, until its alignment is possible.
If, however, movement or separation has already occurred in the teeth, then bind those you hope will remain with gold thread, or silver thread, or silk. Then place a poultice on the fractured jaw. Then place a folded cloth over it, and put a large, properly made compress on the cloth; or a piece of leather sole, equal in length to the jaw.
Then tie it on top, according to how its tying is prepared and its application suits, until it does not come undone; and instruct the sick person to use rest and quiet; and give him/her soft drinks as food.
If, however, you estimate that something of its shape has already been altered in some way, then hurry and untie it on the third day. Then correct what has been altered in it, and plaster it with mill dust, with egg white, or with sesame flour, after you have removed the poultice; and place soft wool over the plaster.
While, therefore, that plaster remains attached to it, and the position of the bone is not altered, leave it, do not untie it, until it is healed; or bind the fracture. For this fracture is often bound for three weeks.
If, meanwhile, a hot abscess occurs, then administer what we have said many times in its calming, until that abscess is removed.
And if the fracture is with a wound, consider. For if a piece is already separated from the bone, or from the surrounding tissue: then be careful in this, so that you remove those pieces of tissue with what is appropriate for you in removing them with instruments.
If, however, the wound opening is tight, then enlarge it with a phlebotome according to the amount of your need.
Then, when you have removed that surrounding tissue, and nothing remains of it, then close the wound opening, if it is wide; and if not, place over it plasters that are suitable for it, and cause it to heal, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of the Furcula, when it is broken.
Indeed, the Furcula is most often broken from the anterior part, on the side of the humerus, and its fracture occurs in one of three ways.
Either it is broken and separated into two, apart from the surrounding tissue that happens to be involved: and it is easier to restore it.
Or such that there are injuries to the bone itself: and it is more difficult to restore it.
Or such that it is a fracture with a wound.
And the operation on it when the fracture is without a wound is that you must have two assistants present, so that one of them holds the support that follows the fractured Furcula, and the other extends the neck towards the opposite side.
Then adjust the fracture with your fingers until its shape is as it should be: and there should be neither a prominence nor a concavity in it.
If you need more extension, then you must place a sphere of cloth or wool under the patient's neck: and its size should be according to your need: and extend and elevate the Furcula, and compress the sphere with your hand, until you have adjusted the fracture as it should be.
If you cannot pull the extremity of the Furcula outwards, because it has gone deep, then the patient must be laid on their neck; and you must place a medium-sized cushion under their shoulder, and the assistant must press down on their shoulder until the bone of the Furcula that is deep is brought upwards; and then straighten the fracture and adjust it with your fingers.
If, however, you feel a splinter from the Furcula, and it moves, then you must cut over it and extract the splinter easily.
If, however, it is retained in the bone, then make an incision for it with one of the knives you have prepared for it, after you have placed an instrument that keeps the sifach under the Furcula; and it is an instrument of wood or iron. And this is its form.
It resembles a spoon without a concavity.
And let its width be according to that which you need for it, from the size of the bone and its smallness. Its length, however, also according to what is possible for you for the operation.
And let it have two ends, as you see: and one end be wide, and the other narrow.
If, however, the rupture of the wound which you made when you extracted the bone's splinter is wide, and you are safe from a hot abscess: then bring together the two edges of the wound with sutures.
And if the rupture is small, or you fear a hot abscess: then fill the wound with bandages, and dressings according to the quantity of the wound's tear.
If a hot abscess should occur, then pour a bandage into rose oil, and vinegar, and wine, and place it upon it.
A constriction of the bone, however, when it is without a wound, and without incision, is that you place upon the bone a plaster made from mill flour with egg white. And place soft lint upon it. Then place a sphere beneath its tickling, if you need it. Then take a himatem, that is, a wheel which the Saracens hold, which is very long and has a width of one palm, or about that. Then place a doubled pad over the lint and plaster. Then take a thin wooden strip, whose width is three fingers, and in length likewise. Then wrap it in cloth. Then wrap that cloth with the strip in the place of the alhimemati that falls over the fracture and the place. Then place the alhimemati over the fracture as it revolves, and wrap it over its neck, and under its healthy and under its diseased tittillico: and bring it over every part, and how you see that the constriction holds the fracture with a fitting constriction: and that is it, which is not concealed from you, and everything, so that the strip is not removed from above the fractured bone. And for this, it is necessary that you examine the sick person every day. Therefore, whenever the ligament softens, and you see the strip is already being removed, then straighten it, and tighten the ligament.
Then place the sick person's sleep on his back, and place a small pillow under his arm at his sleep at night, so that with it his help is lifted from his side. Therefore, the fracture of the collarbone is lifted by lifting the shoulder.
Or bind his arm to his neck, and do not loosen the ligament if it does not happen in the place, a mishap from itching, or from an abscess, until twelve days.
Then renew the plaster if you see his face towards it. And add in stricture, and leave him until he is restored and coagulated.
And indeed the fracture of the collarbone is bound and strengthened in most cases in twenty-eight days, and sometimes it occurs in some men in a shorter time.
Concerning the restoration of the fracture of the shoulder blade.
The shoulder blade is rarely broken, and in its broad area; and only its margins are broken from it.
Therefore, when a part of it is broken, and it is broken in its middle, and it is not recognized except by touch, then according to the nature of the fracture, attempt its preparation. And restore it to its natural form by every means of ingenuity that is possible to you.
Then place mill dust with egg white, and soft linen, and place a pad of doubled cloth on top. Then place a wide splint of thin wood over it, according to the size of the entire shoulder blade, or a little larger.
If, however, there is a concavity under the splint in any part of the shoulder blade, then equalize that concavity with soft linen until the splint settles according to equality.
Then bind over it with a long bandage, with a suitable constriction, and firmness of the splint, until it is not moved from its place.
And consider the bandage every day. Whenever therefore the bandage softens, bind it, and equalize the splint if it is moved from its place: and let the sick person lie on their healthy side.
And the shoulder blade is indeed restored in twenty days, or in twenty-five days.
When therefore this number is completed, then loosen the bandage: and you will be in safety. For it is from bones whose junction is not feared, nor their separation.
If however a fragment from a bone protrudes, and pierces beneath the skin: then cut over it, and remove it. And do what we said in the fracture of the furcula from the sedition of a hot abscess, if anything happens from it.
On the restoration of the fractured breast.
The breast sometimes breaks in the middle of itself, and that rarely happens. Its extremities indeed are broken and shattered very often from its accidents.
When its middle breaks, which inclines downward, and great pain, and difficulty in breathing, and coughing befall it; and perhaps it vomits blood, and a cavity is revealed in the broken bone, and is not hidden from the senses; its restoration is that the weak person be laid on his back; and a pillow be placed between his shoulder blades.
Then press his shoulders, and gather the ribs from both sides with the hands; and equalize in its position, according to every way in which that is possible and suitable, until the form of the bone returns as it ought.
Then place a plaster and linen bandage on it, and put a splint of thin, light wood, or rush, or similar materials, after you wrap it in cloth. Then, adjust it by binding it over the fractured bone so it does not move; and proceed with the bandage around the back several times, and tie it with a suitable tightening.
Then check the bandage every hour, and whenever it softens, tighten it; and if its need prompts its loosening due to itching, or corrosion that occurs in the area, or pain, or an abscess: then quickly loosen it, and remove the plaster, and rectify what occurs with the manner of its treatment.
Then replace the plaster, if you see fit; and apply a bandage to it until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of Ribs, when they are broken.
Know that the fracture of ribs does not occur except in thick places that follow the back, and from their extremities on the anterior side. And attrition does not occur to them, unless because they are cartilaginous. And its knowledge is not hidden from the sense in an investigation with fingers.
And its restoration is done so that one equalizes the fracture with fingers according to the possible way, until the figure is made equal, according to what is necessary. Then apply plasters and bind the broken bone with splints, if it needs that.
And if the fracture of the ribs is sloping inwards, then a severe pain and a stabbing pain occur to the sick person, like the stabbing pain that occurs to one who has pleurisy; because the bone stabs the membrane, and also difficulty in breathing, and coughing, and vomiting of much blood occur to him; and this is a difficult condition to cure.
And the ancients have been ingenious in it with many methods; and among them are those who say to place foods for the sick person which generate inflammation and windiness, so that the belly is inflamed, and stretched, and the fracture is propelled outwards.
We, however, abhor that a swift cause for making a hot abscess be made, if it does not happen. If however, it has already happened, then he adds to it.
And some of them said: let a windy substance be placed over the place, then let it be sucked with strength, and it is similar reasoning. However, it is feared that the windy substance will draw superfluities to the place, because of the weakened condition.
And some of them said: the place ought to be covered with wool that has already been dipped in hot oil, and cushions should be placed between the ribs until it is filled, so that there is an equal bandage when you wrap it around the curve.
Then let the sick person be treated with the treatment for pleurisy, with food and medicine.
If a violent thing constricts the sick person, which he cannot tolerate, and his mouth stings or the covering wounds him with a piercing injury, and we fear for the sick person, then it is necessary that we cut open the place and expose the fractured ribs. Then let us place upon it the instrument which preserves the sifac, whose description preceded: and let us cut the bone easily, and cut it.
Then let us bring together the two lips of the wound, if it is large, with sutures, and let us treat it with plasters until it is healed.
If in the meantime a hot abscess occurs, then hurry and pour lint into rose oil, and place it over the place, and let the sick person be treated with what is suitable for the abscess.
Meanwhile, also, let him lie on his side on which he sleeps, until he is healed.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured spine of the back and neck.
A fracture of the bones of the neck sometimes occurs, and attrition rarely happens to them; and similarly for the vertebrae of the back as well. Therefore, when this happens to someone, and you wish to know whether it will heal or not, then consider. For if you see his hands already softened, and stupefied, and dead; and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor contract them; and when you contract them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, he does not feel it, and does not feel pain in them: then know that it does not heal, and in most cases is mortal.
If, however, he moves them, and feels the contraction with his fingers and the prick, then know that the bone of the neck is sound, and that the sick person will be cured with treatment.
If the same thing happens to the vertebrae of the back, and you wish to know whether it will also heal or not, then consider his feet.  Then, if you see that they are softened, and that what happened to the hands occurs in them; then, when he lies on his back, wind and evacuation come out without his will; and when he lies on his stomach, evacuation comes out without his will; and when he lies on his back, and wishes to urinate, and cannot: then know that it is mortal. Therefore, do not labor in his treatment.
If, however, nothing of that happens to him, the condition is less severe.
And the treatment for what arises from that is to try to reduce the heat of the abscess; thus, place on the bruised vertebra only rose oil, or with the yolks of roasted eggs.
Place that on it several times a day, until the hot abscess subsides; then put one of the strengthening, drying plasters over the spot, and bind it with a bandage; and command the sick person to use rest and quiet. Nor let him sleep on the part where he finds pain until he is healed.
If, however, there is a separation of the bone tissue, or something that has already separated from it, then it is necessary to cut over the skin, and remove that. Then join the two edges of the wound, if it is large, with stitches. Then treat it with some kind of healing ointment until it heals.
If, however, the last bone of the alhausos (tail) breaks, and is at the end of the tail, then it is necessary to introduce the thumb of the left hand into the anus, and straighten the broken bone with the other hand, as much as possible, and with a second straightening.
Then place it over it, and remove it; and treat the wound with the previous treatment, until it heals.
Concerning the restoration of the Ancha.
Chapter XI.
The bones of the Anchar are rarely broken. If they are broken, then their fracture is not complete unless it is a shattering at their extremities; and they are split lengthwise, and deflected inward; and pain and a puncture occur in the location, and the leg that is broken becomes numb.
And its restoration is to extend your hand over it, until you are positioned above the fracture in the manner of its shape.
If the fracture is only at its extremities, then equalize that fracture according to what is prepared for you by equalization, until it resembles its natural shape.
If however the fracture is lengthwise, or already inclines inward, then place the sick person on their stomach, until its fracture's restoration is prepared for you. When therefore you have equalized it, place a plaster over it; then place a splint of wood or leather over it; and bind tightly over it so that you fear not a change of the fracture, nor the removal of the splint.
And equal the concavity of those things with that which fills it, until the bandage takes it according to equality; and command the sick person to sleep on their back, or on their healthy side.
If however a hot abscess happens to him, then cease from its extension and its restoration until the hot abscess subsides; and place upon it that which settles it, according to what has gone before, then return to its restoration; and bind it as is proper.
If however it occurs in the bone's shaft, or it is shattered, extract it.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured limb.
The limb is that which is between the elbow and the head of the shoulder blade.
If therefore it is broken, then its restoration is according to one of two methods.
One of which is: that you take a piece of wood of medium thickness, according to this form that follows.
And two ligaments are tied at the two extremities.
Then it is suspended from a raised place, and the sick person sits upon a seat. Then he shall place his broken arm upon the wood, until it reaches the notch, in the middle of which the curvature of the wood is attached.
Then suspend something heavy above it, so that the attendant extends it downwards. Then the physician shall straighten the fracture with his hands together, until the fracture returns to its proper alignment.
And another method is that the sick person lie on their back, and suspend their arm from their neck with a bandage. Then instruct two attendants, that one of them should hold that which is above the fracture with his hands, and that the other should hold below it, and that each of them should pull to their side.
And if you wish the extension to be stronger, then bind under the fracture with a bandage, and above it with another bandage; and let each of the two attendants pull to their two sides.
If therefore the fracture is near the extremity of the arm, then it is necessary that one bandage be placed below the armpit, and another below the fracture toward the elbow.
And similarly if the fracture is near the elbow, then it is necessary that a bandage be placed over that spot, and over the elbow itself.
Then adjust the fracture with ease without exertion, until when the fracture is adjusted as it ought to be, and a good application is applied; then bind it, if a hot abscess does not occur.
If however a hot abscess occurs, then leave the binding until the seventh day. And place upon it a shredded wool soaked in vinegar and rose oil, until when the abscess subsides; then bind it; and the way to bind it is that you place a plaster over the fracture.
Then place a wrapping of new cloth over the plaster. Then support the arm on the aid itself, and place his open hand on his shoulder.
And place a cloth, and bind the arm on the support, so that the arm stands in place like a star; if nothing prevents you from doing so, nor is anything disturbed on you from the broken bone.
If you fear that something may shift on you from the broken bone, then use splints; and it is as if you place a splint over the fracture itself that is wider and stronger than the other splints. And you should place the width of a finger between each splint; and the length of the splints should be according to the fracture with the addition of three fingers on every side.
Then bind the splints with the bandage I described at the beginning of the chapter.
And it should be that your bandage is tighter over the place of the fracture. And the more the fracture is extended, the smaller the bandage should be.
Therefore, if you see the position of the splints and the bandage, as we have said, at the hour when you restore the limb immediately, then do so; and if you fear a hot abscess, then leave the bandage and splints until the seventh day, as we have said.
Then consider the bandage in all three days, so that no itching or swelling occurs in the place, or food is prevented from reaching the limb, due to the disposition of excess moisture from the bandage. Therefore, rectify it all, according to what we have said.
However, if you are confident that nothing will happen from it, then do not loosen the bandage until after three days, or more.
And let the patient's sleep be on his back, and his hand on his stomach; and place a mattress full of wool evenly under him as an aid.
And consider every hour of night or day, so that the shape of the fractured limb is not disturbed, or the bandage softened. Therefore, rectify it all with your skill.
And provide the patient with food according to the order we have previously described, so that the food is initially bland, until his mouth begins to heal; then it is necessary to increase the thickness of his food.
For it is the custom of the injured arm or leg to heal in forty days; then, therefore, it is necessary to loosen it, and administer a bath, and treat it with ointments that rectify it.
If the fracture is severe, crushed: then do not loosen the bandage and splints from it until fifty days, and up to two months.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured arm.
The arm is composed of two bones, which are called two forearm bones.
One of which is small, and it is that which follows the thumb. And the other is large, and it is that which is situated below the small one on the lower part.
Perhaps therefore the larger forearm bone is broken alone, or the smaller one, or perhaps they are broken together.
When therefore the smaller forearm bone is broken above, then its restoration is easy, and its healing is easier.
And when the forearm bone is broken below, its fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
And worse than that is when the two bones are broken together.
If therefore the bone that is broken is the smaller forearm bone above, then it is appropriate for the physician, in restoring it, to apply a small extension with ease, until he makes it even.
If however the larger forearm bone is the one that is broken: then it is necessary that he apply a very strong bandage.
And it is necessary that he place the shape of the hand in its restoration, and its extension outstretched upon a cushion. And let the thumb of the hand be higher than all the fingers; and let the joint of the hand be lower than all the fingers, or the rest; and let the sick person sit square on himself, and let the cushion be before him, or in a straight line with him, in height, so that the sick person does not incur exertion.
Then the attendant should extend the arm from the lower part, either with his hand, or with a bandage. And another attendant should extend it from above similarly.
Then the physician should equalize the bone, until he restores it according to the best shape possible.
If there are fragments in the broken bone, then try to restore each fragment to its place with your skill.
If however a separate fragment appears in it, and it pierces the skin, and you have no hope of restoring it: then cut over it, and remove it according to the method we have stated in what precedes.
If however the fracture is with a wound, I have already made a separate chapter for it, therefore take its treatment from there.
If, however, a hot abscess arises at the beginning of your restoration, then bind a cloth with a cerate made from rose oil, and white wax, and let it be medium in thickness and thinness: and bind the cloth over it with a subtle binding, until the abscess is quelled: then remove the cerate, and apply a plaster prepared from mill dust with egg white. Then place splints, and let the splint placed over the fracture itself be somewhat wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in most arrangements, whether the fracture be in one or two foci simultaneously.
Then place your binding over the fracture site more strongly and vigorously, and somewhat more with the binding towards the upper or lower part of the fracture; or place a binding that is slightly softer than the previous binding, according to what has been mentioned at the beginning of the chapter.
And let the cloths wrapped around the fracture be soft, damp cloths; and let them not be hard, rough. And let the thread with which it is tied be of linen, medium between thin and thick, as we have said.
And consider the limb and the ligament after days. If anything occurs requiring rectification, such as itching which arises in the limb, then it is necessary to embrocate the limb with hot water until that itching subsides, and to leave the limb unbound until it subsides, then to apply the binding again.
If the binding is now softened, and the bone is now removed, and similar things to these: then rectify all of it by your skill, and consider it again.
For if nourishment is prevented from reaching the limb, due to the excessive tightness of the binding, then it is necessary to soften it for a short time, and to leave it for a few days, until nourishment flows to it, then bind it again.
If, however, nothing of what we have said occurs to the patient, then it is not necessary for it to be loosened, unless after twenty days, or about this many.
Then the patient's hand should be suspended from his neck. And his neck should be level, and should be protected by his skill from violent movements. And make him sleep on his back.
And know that this fracture is restored in thirty days, or in thirty-two days: and perhaps it is restored in twenty-eight days. All of that is according to the dispositions of complexions, and the dispositions of strength.
Concerning the restoration of fractures of the Hand.
Know that fracture of the hand's finger ridges, and the joints of the fingers, rarely occurs. And it does not occur to them unless by very frequent attrition.
Therefore, when a fracture or attrition of the sole occurs, then it is necessary that the weak person sits square, and in front of him be a seat of equal height. Then place his hand upon it extended.
Then the attendant should extend the broken bones, and the physician should adjust them until they are joined with a good union.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and bandage, if a hot abscess does not occur. Then place a pad over it, according to the size of the area, and you will have wrapped it in linen cloth.
If however the fracture is towards the lower part, approximately the sole of the foot, then make a sphere out of cloth, and instruct the sick person to compress it with their fractured foot; then bind it with a long bandage.
And let the pad be of leather, in which there is softness, so that the leather is applied to the body of the foot; and bind it as is appropriate.
If however the fracture is outward, then it is necessary that you place a pad over it, and another pad underneath the foot; and let the hand be open and straight. Then place a bandage, as the hand is turned; and extend it between the fingers with a ligature.
If a fracture should occur in any of the finger joints, then if it is the thumb, it should be aligned as is appropriate. Then it is bound to the foot; and if you wish, place a small straight pad on it, so that the fracture is raised and does not move.
If however the fracture is in the remaining fingers, such as the middle, and index, or ring, and little finger, then it should be aligned, and bound to the healthy finger following it; or all should be bound according to their continuity; and this is better. Or place a small pad over it, as we said in the thumb.
And consider in your restoration, and after the disposition itself of the hot abscess: and apply with what is appropriate, when something from it occurs, according to the reiteration of its account.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured hip.
The hip bone is frequently fractured, and is apparent to the senses; because it is turned to the inner and posterior part.
And its restoration is done thus, that a ligament is tightened above the fracture, and another ligament below the fracture. And the sick person should lie on their face.
Then let the attendant stretch all ligaments towards their respective sides equally; and this, when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
If however the fracture is near the root of the hip, then let one ligament be towards the pubic region so that the stretching is upward; and another ligament below the fracture.
And similarly, if the fracture is near the knee, then let the ligament be near the knee, so that the stretching is downwards.
Then the physician should align the bone with both their hands until they have restored it to its natural shape; and the bone is joined with a good union.
Then it is necessary to apply a plaster and a bandage, if no abscess occurs on the limb. If an abscess occurs in it: then leave it for a few days, until the hot abscess subsides, then return to your treatment.
However, its bandage should be like this. It is necessary that you wrap a hard bandage over the fracture; that is, a bandage: and double it twice, or thrice: and let there remain excess of it. Then bandage the leg until the heel reaches the root of the buttocks.
And insert a long thread between the thigh and the leg near the knee downwards, and let the ends of the thread come from the upper part of the two parts. Then wrap over the leg and thigh what remains of the bandage.
Then place splints on the thigh, in the place of the fracture itself, and place one splint over the shin bone.
Then fill the spaces between the leg and thigh with soft cloths, so that the tightness is equalized.
Then begin with a strong bandage from the middle over the fracture site, with three or four turns. And the further you move away from the fracture site with the bandage, the less tight and softer and gentler your bandage should be.
Then pull on the two ends of the thread, which you have inserted between the thigh and leg. Therefore tie together the three splints that are above. Then proceed with the two ends of the thread to the lower part, until you reach the ankle of the foot. And with both ends tighten the ends of the splints on the other side, so that the bandage does not move from its place.
Then release the tightness on that, while it remains, so that there is no corrosion of the limb, nor abscess, nor swelling, and similar things to them. If anything of that happens. Therefore, hasten with its solution, and rectify whatever happened from it entirely, according to what we have said many times.
If however there is a piece of bone in the bone that pierces: then it is necessary that you make it even, if it is possible for you. And if not, split over it, and remove it: and treat it with that whose remembrance preceded, until it is healed.
And sometimes this fracture of the hip is bound, besides which are added splints, as we said in the assistance and the arm.
However, in the restoration of it thus broken, lameness does not happen to the sick person. And if it is restored alone, besides that another leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that the one to whom it belongs should limp forever.
And know that the hip is bound in fifty days, or a little is added, or a little is lessened: all that is according to the diversity of complexions and other dispositions.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured kneecap.
Know that a fractured kneecap rarely occurs, but attrition happens to it frequently.
If however a fracture does occur, then it is only either a fissure or a hole in its parts. And it is [a fracture] with a wound, and without a wound. And the whole thing stands with sensation.
And its restoration is such that what is disrupted from its parts is equated with the fingers until it is aggregated and joined as much as possible by means of equating, facilitating, and fabrication.
Then apply a plaster of sesame flour, or mill flour, with egg white; and place a round disc on it, if you need one; and bind it above with an appropriate bandage.
Then consider all the dispositions we have narrated in other fractures, such as a hot abscess and similar things; in order that you oppose every accident with that which is suitable to it, until it is healed.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured leg.
Know that the leg has two bones, one of which is thick, and is called the Cruris; and the other is thin, and is called the Focile.
And the same types of fractures occur in both, as occur in the two bones of the arm; and for this reason its restoration is done in the same way as the restoration of the arm, and the operation is one.
If both bones are broken at the same time, the leg turns in all directions.
And if the thinner bone is broken, the leg turns towards the front.
And if the thicker bone is broken, you will find it lower; and it is one of those things that are not hidden from you.
Then administer extension, and equalisation, and ligation of the splints according to that equality.
However, if the fracture of the leg is a foul fracture, having much bleeding, so that the extension should be less, and your relief more gentle.
And indeed, in the leg there is something greater in the operation than in the arm. And when you have equalised the splints, and completed all your operation, then take two strips of pine wood, such as is used in making the rafters placed under the boards: and they are made from palm splinters. And choose those with little thickness, and let them not be too thin. And let their length be according to the length of the leg, from the knee downwards.
Then wrap each one with two wraps lengthwise. And place one part of the leg on one side, and the other on the other side.  And let them be from the knee down to the foot.
