Then tie the two strips in three places, from the two ends and the middle. For when the constriction is prohibited, the leg bends to the right and left, and stands well.
And now indeed a cradle of wood should be administered, lengthwise according to the leg, and the leg placed upon it, so as to preserve it from movement.
And indeed it is very necessary to do this in a fracture, when there is a wound properly with it.
Then consider the leg every day, and strive with that utmost care from an abscess, or swelling, or any other such things. If therefore anything of those things happens, then apply to it with what is necessary, until it is healed, if God wills.
Of the fracture of the bones of the Foot and Toes.
A fracture of the heel bone does not happen completely.
However, fractures occur sometimes in the bones of the Foot, and a fracture also happens rarely in the Toes; and they do not happen to them except for attrition in many dispositions.
If a fracture of the bones of the Foot occurs, and you see those bones raised up above one another, then let the infirm person place their Foot on prepared ground, as if walking.
Then stand you, and place your Foot upon that which is raised from those bones; afterward press, or tread upon it; and straighten it, until it returns to its proper place.
Then place a plaster, and tow upon it; and put a small board beneath the sole of the Foot, which shall have two flat ends.
Then bind it with a suitable bandage after you wrap it with cloths and other necessary things.
When therefore three or four days have passed, then loosen the bandage. For you will find the bones straightened, whether they were fractured or dislocated. Therefore, the operation on them is as you see.
If, however, some fingers are broken, then restore them and straighten them according to what I have told you concerning the restoration of the fingers of the hand. Then place a splint under the finger, along the length of the finger, and somewhat wider than it: then place under this foot the splint that I have told you about, and bind it with a suitable bandage.
If, however, that which is broken of the bones is two, or three, or more, then place a splint of its size over each finger, wrapped in linen cloth; and bind the splint to the lower foot; and let it stand upon one head of the splint outside the sole of the foot, so that it holds firmly.
And it befits you, lest you forget, to consider all that I have said to you concerning other fractures from accidents that we have discussed: and apply each accident with what is appropriate.
Concerning the fracture of the woman's vulva, and the rib bone, and the man's penis.
When the woman's vulva is fractured, then make her sit square. Then incline her slightly towards her own back, and let her be held back.
Then let the midwife fill her vulva with wool, until it fills it: and let there be formed in the vulva from it as if a sphere.
Then let the woman be shaken, and raise her back gradually. For that wool will come out, until it is made like a sphere before the door of the vulva, and the fractured opening returns.
Then place a cushion on her back. Therefore, when she wants to urinate, remove the wool with ease, then let her urinate: and return it according to the manner in which you first introduced it: then let her return to her initial cushion.
Let her do that for seven days, or thereabouts, for the opening itself is restored.
And if you wish, take a sheep's bladder, and tie a reed tube over its opening, and introduce the entire bladder into her vulva. Then blow into the tube with force, until the bladder inflates within the vulva, for the fracture returns.
Then let it be filled with wool, and let it stand for some days, according to what we have described, until it is healed.
And when the bone of the penis of a man, or a woman, is broken, then administer in its restoration, and its alignment, that which we have described concerning the bone of the thigh.
And the rectum is not hidden in these nearby fractures, which occur rarely, especially for one who has some skill or experience.
And understand well this book of mine, for the fracture itself signifies to you in many situations the correct way to restore it and to narrow it: understand therefore.
In the penis of a man, when it is broken, take the throat of a goose and insert the penis into it. Then place upon it a bandage of cloth, and let it be wrapped and left for three days, or thereabouts, and it will heal, if God wills.
Concerning the restoration of a fractured bone, when it is with a wound.
It is fitting for one who has suffered a fracture with a wound, especially if it is large, such as the hip bone or the femur, or similar to it, that you hurry and immediately bleed him: if the conditions for bloodletting are present, as we have previously stated.
If the wound is flowing blood, then it is necessary that you hurry to cauterize it, applying crushed zegi powder to it, if you have no other means available.
Then begin restoring the fracture on that same day: and do not postpone it, if no abscess has formed.
If however a hot abscess occurs, then postpone its restoration until the ninth day, until the abscess has subsided. And do not approach him on the third and fourth days at all, for you would cause him to suffer ill effects.
If however the fractured bone is prominent above the skin, then it is necessary that you attempt to reduce it, and to align it with your hands with ease and a small extension.
If however its extension and alignment with your hands are not prepared, then reduce it with this instrument. And the instrument is made of iron, whose length is the amount of seven fingers, or eight, and its width is according to the amount of the wound. And for that it is necessary for the physician to take from them three, or four, according to the amount needed for the treatment in every kind of fracture.
And it should be round, and have a small thickness so that it does not double up when compressed upon it during the operation, and it should be pointed at the ends, with a margin on its extremities. And its upper part should be to the thickness of its middle part down to its lower part.
And this is its form.
In this operation, it is necessary to place its pointed curved end on the projecting end of the bone, and to drive it with it at one time until the bone returns and is equalized by a certain equality.
Therefore, try to make the broken ends equal to each other.
If its broken ends are slender, and the instrument does not grasp them well, then it is necessary to cut the end of that bone until the instrument has hold of it.
If, however, you cannot reduce them with what we have described entirely, then cut it with one of the appropriate cutting instruments we have mentioned, or saw it with one of the saws in any way possible for you.
Then scrape away whatever remains on the bone of roughness and small pieces.
When, therefore, the bone is reduced, and the infirm person finds intense pain harmful after its reduction, then know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Do therefore that. For you confer great benefit on the infirm person, when your restoration of the bone is complete.
Then immerse a cloth in astringent, black wine. And properly if it is in summer. And do not place on the wound a poultice, nor anything in which there is oil: lest it cause putrefaction and corruption in it.
Then administer splints at the hour of your restoration of the bone's completion. And leave the wound uncovered: so that you cut with scissors a hole in the dressings according to the size of the wound.
And beware with every precaution, lest you bind the wound with the fracture. For often foolish physicians have done this: and they have caused upon their sick either death, or corruption, or züch, that is, pus. And let your binding be soft, gentle, different from the binding of other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or is a large wound, and you fear some bad accidents, which we have narrated, and you find pain in the suspended place: then it is not appropriate that you place splints over it. And make them coverings from hard cloths in the place of splints, and bind it with them.
When, however, a day or two has passed, and you see the wound beginning to generate pus: then remove from above it the cloth that you placed upon it with wine. Then administer bandages and ointments with which it is our custom to treat wounds, such as tetrapharmacon ointment, and similar ones.
And it is fitting for you to loosen the bandage. And consider the wound every day morning and evening, until it is consolidated and healed.
And it is necessary that the limb be prepared in such a way that pus runs from it downwards with ease.
If many days pass over the wound, and it is not consolidated, nor is pus discharged from it, then know that there are small bone splinters there. Therefore it is necessary that you probe the wound with probes. And however many of those splinters are separated, remove them and extract them. And those of them that are not separated, and that pierce the limb and cause pain to occur, try to cut them and remove them by any method possible to you.
If a wound experiences suppuration, or eclipsis, that is, festering, or any other form of corruption and putrefaction, then it is necessary that you apply to each of them the appropriate treatment from the previous chapter.
And in what concerns the matter at hand, and to which you should direct your attention when a bone breaks and protrudes from a limb, such as the thigh bone, the shinbone, and similar large bones, do not attempt to pull it back, for often death results from this; rather, leave it until it decays.
Perhaps it will fall out by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then, if you see fit, treat the wound; otherwise, leave it be.
Concerning the treatment of a knot in the place of a fracture.
This knot often happens in the place of healing a fracture. Especially, those near the joints; and the shape of the limb is disfigured by it. And perhaps it hinders the limb from its natural function.
Therefore, if the knot is recent, apply medicines which have astringent properties, such as aloe, and frankincense, and myrrh, and sarcocolla, and acacia, and similar ones.
So, take some of these, or all of them; and mix them with astringent wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar; and place them over the knot in linen, and bind them securely over it.
And leave the bandage, and do not remove it for many days. Then remove it, and apply another, until the knot is removed.
Or bind over it a lead sheet properly. For lead has the property of removing whatever is feared from the limbs.
However, if the knot has already turned into stone, and is tight, and necessity quickly compels its removal: then cut over it from above, and cut the projecting excess; or shave it with some razors, until it is removed; and treat it until it is healed.
Concerning the treatment when it is restored, and the limb remains after that delicate, not according to its nature.
When a fractured bone is restored, and the limb remains after that delicate, weak: then that is not done except for many reasons. One of which is, the multitude of loosening the ligament and its ligature not according to what is necessary; or because of the excess of the ligaments' tightness, until the nourishment is prevented from running to the limb; or because of the multitude of superfluous embrocation at the wrong time; or because of the paucity of blood in the body of the weak person, and its weakness.
And the treatment of that is, to feed the sick person, and make his body tender, until blood is multiplied in him. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and gladness upon him.
Then place pitch upon the limb, so that the pitch attracts much nourishment to it. And diligently apply warm water as a fomentation, until the nourishment flows, and it returns to its natural form.
Concerning the treatment of fractured bones when they are restored crookedly.
When, in a limb already restored after its healing, crookedness and a prominence of the fractured bone, or a knot, occur; and the form of the limb is disfigured thereby, yet the limb is not prevented from its natural operation: then you should not accept the opinion of one who believes that the limb should be broken again.
And already there have been many foolish physicians and bone-setters who have done that in our land.
And indeed this practice is greatly reprehensible, leading to death, or great fear, so as to be fatal.
But if the lameness and knot are recent: then it is necessary that it be soaked in lukewarm water, in which softening herbs have been boiled, such as mallow leaves, and its root, and melilot, and similar ones. And a plaster should be made with a softening plaster, such as a well-made diachylon.
Or take the mucilage of mallow root, and mix it with hen's fat, or sesame oil: and plaster it with that.
Or take fat figs, and grind them with pigeon dung, and similar things to them that are named as medicines diminishing consolidation.
And sometimes the knot is loosened with constant, ancient, and gentle rubbing, which is done with moist hands. And movement of the limb should be administered to every part at all hours.
If, however, twisting has already occurred, and it has become strong, and has turned to stone, and necessity has arisen to treat it with iron: then it is necessary to cut above it, and to loosen the continuity of the bone, and to cut that which is superfluous from the knot or bone with thin knives: and to administer ease to it with care and diligence.
Concerning the discourse on dislocation.
Dislocation is the departure of any joints from their place. Therefore it is prohibited from its motion, and the figure of the limb is disfigured. And it causes severe pains and grievous injuries to the infirm.
Therefore, when any dislocation occurs, it is necessary to hasten its restoration; nor should one delay it at all.
For if it is delayed, the place becomes festered, and perhaps with it the reduction of the dislocation.
Therefore, it should not be moved or stretched during the time of its suppuration. Because it often causes spasms and painful injuries.
But when that occurs, then it is necessary that you hasten to the phlebotomy of the sick person. Then let it be left alone until the abscess subsides.
Then the limb should be bathed with warm water and oil. Then it should be reduced with ease.
And every limb should be treated with that whose remembrance will come to its place.
I have indeed already arranged the chapters on dislocation, even as preceded in fractures, from the upper part of the body down to its lower part.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the lower jaw.
The two jaws are rarely dislocated except rarely.
And the dislocation of both occurs according to one of two ways. For either they are moved from their places by a small movement, and softened for a little while. Or they are dislocated by a complete, thorough dislocation, until they are softened to the chest, until the sick person's saliva runs, and he cannot hold it, nor can he close his mouth, and his tongue is wrapped with his speech.
However, when its dislocation is small, then it returns in many arrangements by itself with little effort.
However, if the dislocation is complete, thorough: then it is necessary that its reduction be administered quickly, and not delayed at all: and it is necessary that the attendant hold the sick person's head, and that the physician insert the thumb of one of his hands into the root of the dislocated jaw between his mouth, if the dislocation is on one side. Or let him insert his two thumbs together, if the dislocation is on two sides. And the remaining fingers of his hands be outside, with which he will balance.
And command the sick person to clean their jaws and set them free to eat on every side. And the physician shall balance the jaw, and push the jaw until it returns to its place.
If its reduction is difficult, especially if both jaws are together, then administer a vaporization with hot water and oil, until the reduction of both becomes easy: neither delay the reduction of both, as we said.
When therefore they return and are balanced, and the sick person's mouth is closed, and they are not softened, then place on them pads of cloth, with cerate made from wax and rose oil.
Then it shall be bound with ease with soft bandages.
And let the sick person's sleep be on their back, and let their head be supported between two pillows, so that they do not move it to the right and left, nor attempt or be forced to chew anything: rather, let their food be a gentle drink, until when the pains are removed, and the jaw is bound, then let them eat whatever they want, and let it be administered with ease.
Let them not exert themselves over the operation of their mouth in eating and drinking, and yawning, until the jaw is bound and healed.
If the reduction of the two jaws becomes difficult, when they dislocate at any time and do not return to their places, then fever and constant sweating frequently occur, and perhaps the sick person's bowels are loosened, and perhaps they vomit pure bile.
Therefore, when you see that, then know that it is loss, and often he who experiences it dies within ten days.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the collarbone and the extremity of the arm.
The collarbone does not dislocate from the inner side, because of its connection with the chest. And it sometimes dislocates outwards: and that is evident to the senses.
And its restoration is that the sick person lie on their back, and extend their arms. Then grasp the place with your hand firmly, for it will return.
Then place a plaster and pads over it, and bind it.
However, its extremities that follow the elbow, and are continuous with it, are not dislocated, except rarely. If, however, it dislocates on any day, then it is necessary that it be reduced and straightened according to what we have said, and what is prepared for you.
Then place a plaster and pads over it, and a bandage. And instruct the sick person to adhere to tranquility and quiet until they are healed.
And with this same treatment, the humerus extremity is reduced, when it is also separated from its place.
Concerning the reduction of the dislocation of the Humerus.
Know that the Humerus is not dislocated except according to three ways.
One of which is that it is dislocated to the side of the armpit to the lower part. The second is that it is dislocated to the side of the chest. And perhaps it is dislocated to the upper part of the Humerus; and that happens rarely.
And it is not dislocated to the back, because of the scapula. And it is not dislocated to the front, because of the nerves.
And very often it is dislocated and comes out to the lower part towards the groin; especially in those whose flesh is scant. Because it comes out quickly in them, and goes in quickly.
However, in those whose flesh is plentiful, it is the opposite; namely, because it comes out with difficulty, and goes in with difficulty.
And perhaps it happens to some people by a blow, or a fall, and the humerus becomes inflamed with a hot abscess, and it is thought on account of that, that it is already dislocated.
Therefore, you must examine it until you stand on the certainty of it, and then proceed in its treatment.
And dislocation is recognized when it is turned downwards towards the armpit: and distinguish between a dislocated humerus and a healthy humerus. For you find a clear difference among them, and you find a concavity in the head of the humerus, and under the armpit, when touching the head of the humerus, it feels like an egg: and the weak person cannot bring the hand to his ear, nor move it in all directions.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest, or upwards, then you find a clear touch, and it is not hidden.
And indeed, a simple reduction of this dislocation is made when it is recent, or the child is weak.
And its reduction is that the attendant should raise his hand upwards. Then place your thumbs under his armpit, and raise the joint with strength upwards to its place: and the attendant should raise his hand and extend it upwards. Then he should place it downwards, for it indeed returns quickly.
However, if it does not return by what we have said, and many days have passed since the dislocation occurred, then it is necessary that the sick person be bathed in warm water, and an embrocation be administered which softens and soothes, such as if roots of althea, and fenugreek, and melilot are boiled in water, and administered.
Then the sick person should be laid on their back: and a sphere and wool, which should be equal between softness and tightness, should be placed under their armpit.
Then the physician should place his heel upon the sphere, and raise the head of the shoulder with force, and pull the sick person's hand downwards.
And if you wish, reduce it in this manner: and it is that you should have a man present who is taller than the sick person, and he should stand on the side, and insert his shoulder beneath the sick person's armpit: and he should raise their armpit upwards, until the sick person is suspended in the air. And another attendant should pull the sick person's hand downwards towards their abdomen. If however the sick person is light, then it is necessary that something else be suspended with him, so as to weigh him down: for the dislocation returns immediately.
And it is also reduced by another method: and it is that a long piece of wood be fixed in the ground, whose head is round in shape, like a mortar's sleeve: which should not be thick nor thin. Then the sick person is placed beneath it, after soft cloths are placed on the head of the wood, and the sick person stands along the length of the wood.
Then his hand should be extended downwards on one side, and his body on the other side extended with strength: for the joint returns to its place quickly.
If its reduction is difficult by all that we have said, then use this treatment: and it is that you should take wood whose length is the amount of two arms, and its width the amount of four fingers, and its thickness the amount of two fingers: which should have a round head, so that its entrance into the depth of the titillic may be eased, according to this shape.
Then soft cloths are bound over the round head, so that the wood does not harm the sick person.
Then it is placed under the sick person's titillic, so that the hand, or arm, is extended over the wood down to the lower parts, and the wood is tied to the support, arm, and hand's extremity.
Then the arm is placed over a step of a ladder across its width, and the hand is extended downwards, and the rest of the body is allowed to hang from the other side. For the joint immediately enters.
When therefore its entry is completed in whatever way is possible, then it is necessary that you place a sphere of equal quantity made of wool under the titillic. Then place the prepared plaster made from mill powder with frankincense and egg white over the entire shoulder, as it revolves above.
Then bind a suitable bandage under the titillic, and wrap the bandage over the plaster above; and tie its hand to its neck, and let it hang, and do not move its hand for seven days.
And it is necessary that little food be given to the sick person, until the limb is strengthened; because this is quicker for its healing.
Then a larger one is placed after seven days, or five, and it is exercised with movement. If therefore it is strengthened and not softened, then it is now healed.
If however the joint is dislocated many times, because of the moisture which happens to it, or because of another cause: then it is necessary that a cautery with three openings be administered in it, according to what has been stated in the chapter on cautery.
If all this is done, and the ligament is loosened after seven days, and the joint is not strengthened, and the plaster and bandage are applied to it repeatedly, and it is not strengthened, and it falls, and cannot be raised upwards: then know that the nerves connected to the shoulder head are already cut, or stretched, or softened: and know that the joint will never be fixed in its place.
The reduction of dislocations that occur towards the breast and nipple, or to the back, is done with pushing and pulling with the hands, until it returns, and the remaining bandages and treatment are administered to it until it is healed.
If after healing there is heaviness in the limb and slowness in its movement, then the sick person should use a bath repeatedly until that heaviness is lessened and it returns to its original nature.
On the treatment of elbow dislocation.
Know that the elbow joint dislocates with difficulty, and similarly returns with difficulty as well: and indeed it dislocates to all parts, and especially to the front or to the back.
And its dislocation is not hidden from you, since it lies under sight and under touch, according to whatever form it is dislocated. And when you join the dislocated elbow with the sound one, that will be clearly evident to you. For the joint is hollowed out, and cannot double the arm, nor touch its shoulder with it.
And it is necessary that you hurry and reduce the dislocation immediately, before a hot abscess happens to it.
For if a hot abscess happens to it, its reduction becomes difficult, and perhaps it will not be healed completely, especially when it is towards the back. For it is worse than all other types of dislocation, and causes them more intense pain, and often death descends with it.
And its restoration, when it is of those that can be reduced, is that his hand be extended with two hands, and his arm be stretched out, and the hands of the physician be above and below the elbow, and he impel the joint with the thumbs of his hands together, or with the root of his palm, until it returns to its place.
Or indeed, the dislocation is towards the front: then it returns thus, that he double his hand with his hand, until he strikes with the root of his palm the elbow which is dislocated.
If the dislocation does not yield to reduction, then administer a forceful, strong extension; and it is that two assistants extend the arm, and two assistants hold it also, so that it is not moved during the extension.
Then the arm is rotated in every direction after a long doubled cloth, or a wide bandage, is wrapped around his hand.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, let him anoint it with oil, so that it is from those things that aid in the lubrication of the joint with ease. Then he should push the joint with a forceful impulse until it returns.
And after its reduction, it is necessary that a plaster be placed upon it, in which there is astringency and desiccation with egg white; and a decent bandage be bound tight. And let the arm be suspended from the patient's neck, and leave it for several days.
Then it should be loosened. If therefore the joint is made firm in its place, then loosen the bandage from it, and leave it.
And if you see the joint not being properly bound, then reapply the plaster and bandage, and leave it for several days until it is properly bound. Then loosen it.
However, if after the reduction of the joint, there is heaviness, and the condition is in motion, then administer moistening in bathing, and gentle friction, and compression, until it is lined, or place upon the joint the fat of a ram's rump. Then bandage it, and leave it upon it day and night. Then remove it, and immerse it in a bath. When it sweats, then rub the joint with moderate friction. Then repeat upon it the application of the aleliati, once, and a second time, and a third, with each bath entry, until it is soothed.
And if you wish, place upon it warm, moistened cow dung with butter, and bandage over it.
Do that several times. For thus it is soothed, and returns to its proper state.
Concerning the treatment of dislocation of the Almahassem.
The joint between the arm and the hand, the Mahassem, is often dislocated.
And the reduction of its dislocation is easy. In contrast to other joints. However, speed is necessary in the reduction of its dislocation at the time of dislocation, before the area becomes suppurated; or a hot abscess occurs there.
And the reduction of the dislocation is to place the dislocated hand on a table, and the attendant to extend the hand; and the physician to place his palm over the prominence of the joint, and to push it until it returns.
However, it is necessary to consider that if the palm has already receded toward the palm of the hand, then let the patient place the back of his hand on the table in extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is proceeding towards the back of the hand, then let the position of his hand be internally on the table, that is, the inside part of his hand be placed on the table, so that the hand of the physician falls upon the very prominence of the joint.
If it returns immediately, it is good; and if not, then bind it with a soothing plaster for the abscess: and leave it, and do not repeat it.
For if it does not tolerate, nor can you achieve its reduction, after the days have passed, yet the joint remains according to its twist: and does not harm the patient anything except that the hand becomes soft, and cannot grasp anything; then know that the nerves are severed, or worn away.
Therefore, there is no remedy in it, except to cauterize near its fracture. And perhaps it does not help at all.
When therefore the Almahassem does not return, then place upon it the plaster, which we have narrated. Then it should be bound and left for five days; afterward, it should be loosened and left, and the hand exercised.
If however its movement is deprived, and something serious happens to it, then gently treat it with warm water and friction several times, until it is relieved.
Concerning the treatment of dislocated fingers.
When a finger is dislocated in any direction, or a finger is dislocated towards the back of the palm, or its front: then extend the finger, and drive the dislocation with your thumbs, until it returns.
Then support the head of the finger, and suspend it towards the part to which it was dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then suspend it, and extend it, until it is straight in its proper place that day.
Therefore, when night comes, bind it according to the aforementioned account: and do not cease to loosen it during the day, and exercise it with movement: and bind it at night.
Do this for several days, until it is strengthened.
And similarly do this with it if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand; and bind it to that very part, and do your first operation upon it until it is healed.
And similarly do this with it when it is dislocated to other parts.
Concerning the treatment of the dislocation of the spine of the back.
When it happens to someone that the spine of the back, or the neck, is completely dislocated, or that many vertebrae are removed from their places, then there is no cure for it, since death is swift for the afflicted person.
And a sign of this is that the excretion of the sick person comes out without will, and he cannot hold it: and very often some of his limbs, or his two legs, or his two arms, or one of them, are softened; or a single vertebra is removed from its place.
For often it is removed, which often happens as a removal to four parts.
Therefore, that which is removed to the rear is called a hump.
However, its treatment is that you should consider then, if the hump occurs from childhood: then there is no treatment for it, nor is there any cure from it at all.
However, in that which occurs from a fall, or a blow, or similar things to these, the ancients have already described methods of treatment in lengthy discourse. Of which much does not return with benefit.
I, however, have now abbreviated it from that, which a little excuses from much of it from my proximity to the intention: and by my exposition by which I expounded it, and I fashioned its instrument in a different way from that by which they showed it, and expounded it.
I say therefore, that in the hump which occurs from the anterior part, there is no remedy, nor cure from it.
And similarly in that which is made to both sides also.
And it is not cured from them, unless that which occurs on the back properly, with that which I narrate: and it is, that the sick man be extended on his face on a level platform near a wall: and that damp straw be spread under him, so that his chest is not injured.
Then let a straight piece of wood be fixed in a hole in the ground, at the edge of the platform, approximately at his head: and another piece of wood approximately at his feet at the other edge of the platform: and let an assistant hold the wood, and let it not be firmly fixed in the hole. And another assistant hold the other.
Then wrap around the head of the sick person, and under his armpits, a soft, firm bandage: and extend the end of the bandage to the wood that is near his head: and tie it to it.
Then tighten another bandage over his hips, and over his knees, and at his ankles. Then gather all the bandages together, and tie them to the other piece of wood that is at his feet.
Then let each attendant extend the bandage towards the piece of wood, and do not move the two pieces of wood from their places where they are fixed: however, they should not be firmly secured, as we have said.
And let the physician place his palms upon the vertebra with force, until it returns to place; or let him place a board upon it. Then he binds his feet to the board, until it returns to place.
If, however, it does not return through this treatment, then take a board whose length is approximately three arms' lengths. And make a hollow in the wall, of which we said that it is near the sick person, a place into which the end of the board may enter. Then place the middle of the board upon the vertebra. And let the physician place his feet upon the other extremity. And let him pull well, until the vertebra is constricted, and returns to its place.
Then if you wish, operate with a hand-turned instrument: and it is, that you fix in the earth near the sick person's head at the end of the footstool two pieces of wood, each of the length of one arm; and near his head at the end of the footstool two pieces of wood.
And let the length between each piece of wood be one palm; and now a hole has been made in every place where the instrument turns; and let every piece of wood be firmly fixed in the earth, so that it does not move at all.
And let a round piece of wood be inserted; and the instrument is rotated in the two holes of the two pieces of wood.
And at its extremity is a hole in which is fixed a piece of wood, whose length is a palm with that which is rotated; and in the other two pieces of wood is similarly one of that kind.
Then tighten the bandages you have applied to the patient's chest with the instrument that is near his head; and the bandages you have applied to his legs with the instrument that is near his feet.
Then let a servant stand near each instrument, who shall turn his hand with the cord with which the instrument is rotated; and the physician shall equalize the Spondyle, as we have said.
This is the form of the instrument, which follows.
Then, after the Spondyle has returned and the place is equalized, it is necessary to apply a drying plaster with egg white. Then, a linen bandage. Then place on top of the plaster a strip of wood, whose width is of three fingers, or approximately so. And its length is a quantity that covers the place of the Hump, and over the healthy part of the Spondyle.
Then bind it with the ligature that we mentioned. And let the sick person use subtle nourishment until he is healed.
If something of prominence remains in the place at the end of the healing: then it is necessary that medicines which soften and soothe be administered, along with the use of the board which we described for a long time. And now a lead plate is administered on it.
And sometimes a prominence occurs in the final part of the spine of the back, and it is thought that a dislocation is through it, and that is the bone which now protrudes. Therefore, it is not fitting that presumption be made with this medicine upon him; for perhaps it will cause death.
Concerning the treatment of a dislocated shoulder.
Know that dislocation of the shoulder joint, and the humerus joint, does not happen except for dislocation itself; nor does it happen to them what happens to the other joints from slight displacement, and concavity.
And indeed the shoulder joint is dislocated according to four parts. That is, because it is dislocated forward and backward.
And it is most often dislocated inward; and slightly dislocated forward, and backward.
And the treatment of its inward dislocation is this: because when you join the weak leg with the healthy one, it is longer; and its knee is more prominent than the healthy one, and the weak one cannot bend its foot at the groin; and there is a place that follows the groin, ulcerated with a hot ulcer, because the hip bone has reached there.
And the sign of the one who suffers an outward dislocation is that its symptoms are the opposite of these symptoms.
And the sign of the one who suffers an anterior dislocation is that the healthy leg extends fully; however, it does not bend without pain in the knee; and if it attempts to walk, it cannot go forward on it; and its urine is retained, and its groin is ulcerated, and in walking, its heel is on the ground.
And the sign of the one who suffers a posterior dislocation is that the knee does not extend, nor can it bend before the groin bends; and its leg is also shorter than the other; and the groin is softened, and the hip bone head is at the place of the ilium.
The reduction of this type of dislocation is, however, that you should consider then if the dislocation is old, which has already become established in the patient. Then if you administer its reduction and it remains in its position, then there is no cure for it at all. Therefore, it is not appropriate to make a presumption about it entirely.
However, in the one whose dislocation is recent, and is of one of the four types of dislocation, then hasten to this, so that you wish to reposition the joint and extend it to the inside and outside, and move it to the right and left: perhaps it will return, and will not need another treatment.
If, however, it does not return in this way, then it is necessary that you prepare a strong assistant, and that he extend his leg from the lower part, either with his hands or with a bandage that is tied above his leg above the knee, and another assistant extend his hand from above, so that he inserts his hands under his toes. Then he should tie it with a gentle bandage over the root of the thigh, and hold the end of the other bandage.
And let its extension be either from the front part of the groin to the part of the crotch, or from the back to the part of the back.
And let the extension of all of them be in turn, until the sick man is lifted with his body from the earth, and remains hanging.
And this kind of extension is a common kind of the four kinds.
If therefore the dislocation returns with what we have said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary for every kind that which I shall say about its own cure.
In the reduction of it, indeed, when the dislocation is to the interior, it is necessary that the sick person lie on his healthy side. Then let a bandage be placed over the root of the thigh, in that which is between the head of the thigh, and the place that is under the groin. Then extend the bandage upwards from the groin to the higher part of the body, to the part of the crotch.
Then let another strong servant take his arms, and diminish the bulky place of the weak thigh, and extend it outwards with vigorous extension. For now it returns to its place.
And this method is easier than the other methods of curing, by which this limb is reduced.
However, if it is excused from you, and does not obey you for entrance with this method of treatment at all: then it is necessary that the two feet of the weak person be bound together with a strong, soft bandage, over the two heels and over the two knees: and the length of each be equal to the quantity of four fingers from its counterpart: and let the weak leg be extended more than the other by the quantity of two fingers.
Then let the weak person be suspended over a piece of wood, which is in the house: and let its length from the ground be the quantity of two arms.
Then command a strong young man to secure the head of the thighbone; and let him be suspended with the weak person by another young man; and let another young man, securing the thighbone with strength, push; for the joint returns to its place with speed.
However, in its own return, when the dislocation is outward, it is necessary that the weak person lie on a platform, according to what we have narrated in the case of the hunchback. And a bandage shall be tightened over his leg, the weak one properly, and over his chest.
Then let two pieces of wood be placed, one at his feet, and the other at his head. Then let a piece of wood be placed added in the middle of the platform, firmly fixed; over which let damp cloths be wrapped so as not to injure the weak person; and let the wood be between his thighs, so that he is not pulled downward upon extension.
Then let every attendant pull in their direction, and let the physician equalize the reduction with his hand.
If therefore it obeys the return, it will be good.
And if not, place it upon the board itself, and press it, just as we said concerning a hump, equally. However, it is necessary that the sick person lie on their healthy side.
And when the dislocation is towards the front, then it is necessary that the sick person's leg be pulled quickly; and it is according to this same account on the platform; and the physician should place the palm of his right hand on the sick person's groin. Then he should grasp it with the other hand, and with that grasp, place it extended downwards towards the knee.
And if the dislocation is towards the back, then it is not necessary that the sick person be extended downwards and raised from the ground; rather, it is necessary that he be placed on a hard object, as is proper, as is also done if his hip is dislocated outwards, according to what we said about his lying on two hips; and it is on his face, and ligaments extended, according to what we said recently.
And it is necessary that pressure be administered with a board also over the place to which the joint is going, when the reduction is completed as is necessary.
And the sign of its return is not hidden from you: and it is that you extend the two legs of the sick person; and when you see them equal, and the sick person tightens their leg, and expands it without excuse, then know that the limb has now returned, as is proper; then join the two hips, and place a plaster, and bind with a bandage, that is, a roller: so that the hip does not move to any part; and the sick person adheres to rest for three days, or four.
Then loosen the bandage and the plaster, and compare the leg with the other; then if you see them equal in state, then know that the dislocation is now firm; therefore, release the sick person to walk.
And if you see any softening, then return and plaster it, and bind it according to your first binding; and leave it also for three days.
Then loosen it and make him/her walk slowly on both legs for a few days, until it strengthens well.
Concerning the dislocation of the Knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated outwards, inwards, and downwards, that is, backwards.
And it is not dislocated forwards.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you should instruct the sick person to apply his leg to his thigh. If however it does not adhere to the thigh: then know that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all the ways of its dislocation is that you should make the sick person sit upright, so that he may extend his leg as far as possible over it, and apply your back to his face, and place his foot between your feet.
Then make his knee adhere with your palms, and place them straight between your fingers above his knee. Then apply with your palm the two sides of his knee with strength. And another attendant should extend his foot, until the knee returns to its place.
And the sign of its return is that the leg adheres to the thigh with gentleness, not with effort.
Then plaster it, and make the leg adhere to the thigh. Then bind them together with a bandage for three or four days. Then loosen them.
And let the sick person not use walking for some days, until he is healed.
If, however, its reduction is refused to you, then administer strong extension with ligaments, whose description preceded in the treatment of the hip, until it returns.
On the treatment of a dislocated heel.
The heel sometimes is displaced by a small movement, and sometimes is dislocated completely. And its dislocation occurs either inward or outward.
And the sign of its dislocation is that you see the heel swollen, protruding towards the side to which it is dislocated.
The treatment of its displacement is easy repositioning: and it is that it be gently pulled and straightened until it returns.
In treating it when it is completely dislocated, it is necessary to have the sick person sit upright; and a very strong attendant must hold him firmly from behind, at the middle of his back.
Then hold his foot with your right hand at its upper part, and with your left hand at the lower part of his foot, at the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. Then with the left twice.
Then extend with the left, and push the foot's instep with the right toward the leg without effort.
Do that twice, as we have recounted.
Then push the instep of the foot towards the leg a third time, and you pull the heel.
If therefore it returns in one time, or in two according to this method, and you see the foot even, it is good; and if not, then repeat the operation upon it, because it returns.
If however the reduction of it with what we have recounted is forbidden to you: then make the sick person lie on their back on the ground, and fix a firmly planted peg in the ground, so that it falls between his thighs; and you have now wrapped a cloth around it, so that it does not injure the sick person.
Then let the attendant take his thigh; afterwards let another attendant stretch the foot, either with their hands or with a bandage that is tied around the ankle. Then let every attendant pull against the other's pull; and the peg remain standing between the two thighs of the sick person, holding him so that his body is not pulled downwards during the stretching.
Then the physician should adjust the dislocation with his hands, and another attendant should hold the healthy leg downwards. For the dislocation returns with speed.
Therefore, when the dislocation returns, and its return is evident to you, then place a plaster and a bandage, and bind it firmly with ligaments, and tie the foot with a bandage to the leg.
And it is necessary that you beware or fear for the nerves that are above the posterior heel, so that the bandage is not forceful over them and injures them.
Then leave it for two days, or three. If the ligament softens, bind it again; then loosen it on the third or fourth day; and prohibit the infirm person from walking for forty days. If, however, he attempts to walk before that period, there is no security that the dislocation will not resolve upon him, and be corrupted, and not receive healing after that.
If a hot abscess occurs to him, then it is necessary that you administer to its sedation that which our narrative preceded in another place regarding treatment, and an embrocation, until it is removed.
Concerning the dislocation of the toes of the foot, and its treatment.
It is necessary that that which is dislocated from them be made equal with a slight extension, without effort; and indeed, this is not difficult, but rather easy.
However, if the dislocation is in some of the dorsal knots of the foot, then it is necessary that the infirm person sit his foot on a level place, or on a board; and he is standing like walking.
Then you stand, and place your foot on that which projects from those joints. Then press it with your foot with force, until it returns and you see it now made equal, and a projection does not appear in the place.
Then place a board under the sole of his foot that holds the whole foot, with two ends. Then bind it with a suitable bandage for three days. Then loosen it, and keep him from walking for many days, until it is tightened and secured from reduction.
Concerning the types of dislocation with wound or fracture.
When something of this sort occurs, and you attempt its cure and restoration, often death ensues.  Therefore, it is not appropriate for anyone to presume to cure such a thing, unless he is skilled in the long practice of the art, merciful, pious, slow, not bold, nor presumptuous; and that he should use, in the initial treatment, only medicines that soothe hot abscesses. And leave the sick person to divine judgment. Unless you see, or hope for, salvation from destruction with the alleviation of the illness, and in which something of hope appears. Therefore, attempt its reduction immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a hot abscess occurs.
If, therefore, the limb returns as we wish, then administer a regimen that soothes the abscess, and treat the wound with whatever suitable drying ointments are appropriate.
If the dislocation is with a fracture, and separated bony protrusions occur, then attempt their removal, and do in that what we said in simple illnesses, from what preceded in their places.
And beware according to your ability, and guard yourself from entering the path of fear, according to what my instruction preceded to you.
For that is better for you before your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and more safely for your body amongst those in your time.
